Switch to carbamazepine<br /></br>   – Carbamazepine causes neural-tube defects, craniofacial anomalies, IUGR. Swapping one teratogen for another offers no benefit.  </p></br><p>c. Switch to clozapine<br /></br>   – Clozapine is reserved for treatment-resistant schizophrenia; not first-line for bipolar maintenance, carries agranulocytosis, metabolic, and gestational diabetes risks. No evidence it is safer in pregnancy.  </p></br><p>d. Switch to valproic acid<br /></br>   – Valproate is among the most teratogenic psych drugs (NTDs up to 10 %), cognitive deficits; absolutely contraindicated if alternatives exist.  </p></br><p>e. Continue current regimen<br /></br>   – Leaves fetus exposed to lithium’s cardiac teratogenicity when a safer option (temporary discontinuation) is feasible given her stable course.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Core takeaway: In a euthymic pregnant patient with low relapse risk, discontinuing lithium (with vigilant follow-up) minimizes fetal harm and is preferred to continuing lithium or substituting other, more teratogenic anticonvulsant mood stabilizers.</p>
17. A 30-year-old primigravid woman at 10 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician for her first prenatal visit. She has polycystic ovarian syndrome. She conceived following clomiphene therapy, and her only current medication is a prenatal vitamin. She is 165 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 82 kg (180 lb); BMI is 30 kg/m2. Examination shows a uterus consistent in size with a 10-week gestation. Which of the following is the most appropriate recommendation for this patient during her pregnancy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Amniocentesis</br>b. Cesarean delivery</br>c. Chorionic villus sampling</br>d. Limiting pregnancy weight gain</br>e. Measurement of serum fasting insulin concentration</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Limiting pregnancy weight gain  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key data: 30-year-old, first pregnancy, BMI 30 kg/m² (obese, class I), 10 weeks’ gestation.<br /></br>• No advanced maternal age (AMA = ≥35 yr), no abnormal screening tests, no family history of aneuploidy given.<br /></br>• PCOS and obesity ↑ risk of gestational diabetes, preeclampsia, fetal macrosomia, and cesarean delivery.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify major risk factor: maternal obesity (BMI ≥30).<br /></br>Step 2 – Recall guideline: Institute of Medicine (IOM) recommends women with prepregnancy BMI ≥30 gain only 11–20 lb (5–9 kg).<br /></br>Step 3 – Choose the recommendation that directly addresses modifiable risk → advise LIMITED pregnancy weight gain.<br /></br>Take-home message: Appropriate gestational weight-gain ranges depend on prepregnancy BMI; restricted range for obese patients decreases maternal and neonatal complications.<br /></br>Documentation tip: “Counseled patient on target weight gain of 11-20 lb for BMI 30 kg/m²; provided nutrition &amp; exercise guidance.”  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Excess maternal adiposity leads to insulin resistance, chronic inflammation, and endothelial dysfunction → ↑ risk of GDM, hypertensive disorders, thromboembolism, and cesarean.<br /></br>• Limiting weight gain mitigates these risks and reduces odds of large-for-gestational-age (LGA) infants and shoulder dystocia.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Recommended antenatal care for obese patients:<br /></br>1. Early glucose tolerance test (first prenatal visit) + repeat at 24–28 wk if initial screen normal.<br /></br>2. Aspirin 81 mg daily starting at 12–28 wk for preeclampsia prophylaxis if ≥1 high-risk factor (obesity alone is moderate risk).<br /></br>3. Nutrition consult + moderate-intensity exercise.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“3-2-1 Rule for Obesity in Pregnancy”<br /></br>3: BMI ≥30<br /></br>2: Gain 2 digits ≈ 11–20 lb<br /></br>1: One baby, One goal – keep gain low to lower complications.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Amniocentesis (15–20 wk): Offered for AMA (≥35 yr) or abnormal first-trimester screen. Patient is 30 yr; no indication.<br /></br>b. Cesarean delivery: Obesity ↑ likelihood, but decision is intrapartum, not a standing prenatal recommendation.<br /></br>c. Chorionic villus sampling (CVS, 10–13 wk): Same indications as amnio; unnecessary in a 30-yr-old with no abnormal screen.<br /></br>e. Measurement of serum fasting insulin: Not used to screen for gestational diabetes; standard is oral glucose testing (1-hr/3-hr OGTT).  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Students may think PCOS = automatic early invasive aneuploidy testing, but PCOS affects metabolic, not chromosomal, risk. Focus on obesity-related counseling first.</p>
18. A previously healthy 57-year-old man comes to the emergency department 1 hour after the sudden onset of severe occipital headache. His temperature is 37.2°C (99°F), pulse is 92/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 180/98 mm Hg. He is mildly lethargic but oriented to person, place, and time. Neurologic examination shows no other abnormalities. A CT scan of the head shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: F</br></br>a. Measurement of erythrocyte sedimentation rate</br>b. Measurement of intraocular pressure</br>c. Carotid duplex ultrasonography</br>d. MRI of the brain with contrast</br>e. Intravenous prochlorperazine therapy</br>f. Cerebrospinal fluid analysis</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: f. Cerebrospinal fluid analysis (lumbar puncture)  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Sudden onset of severe occipital headache” = classic thunder-clap presentation suspicious for subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH).<br /></br>• Age 57, hypertensive at presentation → major risk factors for aneurysmal rupture.<br /></br>• “CT scan of the head shows no abnormalities” but was obtained only 1 hour after onset. Non-contrast CT misses ≈5–10 % of SAH overall and up to 15–20 % when performed within the first 6 hours.<br /></br>• Mild lethargy without focal neurologic deficits fits the non-focal presentation of SAH.<br /></br>Key takeaway: A normal head CT does NOT rule out SAH when clinical suspicion is high.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to thunder-clap headache:<br /></br>1. Non-contrast CT head → If positive → neurosurgery / neuro-IR.<br /></br>2. If CT negative but suspicion remains high → lumbar puncture (LP) to look for blood/xanthochromia &amp; ↑ opening pressure.<br /></br>3. If LP confirms SAH → CT/MR angiography to localize aneurysm.</p></br><p>Our patient is exactly at step 2, so the next action is cerebrospinal fluid analysis.</p></br><p>Documentation (real patient): “Thunder-clap headache, negative non-contrast CT, high suspicion SAH → plan LP to rule out/confirm SAH.”</p></br><p>Core concept: Always rule out SAH with LP when CT is negative but the clinical picture screams SAH.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (SAH)<br /></br>• Usually due to rupture of saccular (berry) aneurysm at branch points of Circle of Willis.<br /></br>• Blood enters subarachnoid space → meningeal irritation produces sudden severe headache, nuchal rigidity, photophobia; may have brief loss of consciousness.<br /></br>• RBC breakdown → bilirubin → xanthochromia appears in CSF ≥6 h after bleed.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>After confirming SAH:<br /></br>• Nimodipine to prevent vasospasm.<br /></br>• Maintain SBP &lt;140–160 mm Hg (labetalol, nicardipine) while preserving cerebral perfusion.<br /></br>• Early neurosurgical clipping or endovascular coiling of the aneurysm.<br /></br>• Monitor for re-bleed, hydrocephalus (external ventricular drain if needed).</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CT first, LP next” → Think “C-L” in alphabetical order: C (CT) comes before L (LP).<br /></br>Or mnemonic: “If the scan is clean but the story is mean, tap the scene.”</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. ESR measurement – used to screen for giant-cell (temporal) arteritis (gradual temporal headache, jaw claudication, vision changes), not a sudden thunder-clap occipital headache.<br /></br>b. Intraocular pressure – for acute angle-closure glaucoma (eye pain, halos, mid-dilated pupil). Our patient has no eye symptoms.<br /></br>c. Carotid duplex – evaluates carotid stenosis/TIA, which causes transient focal neurologic deficits, not isolated explosive headache.<br /></br>d. MRI brain with contrast – can show subtle parenchymal lesions but is less sensitive than LP for detecting SAH immediately after a negative CT. It’s done later only if LP and CT are unhelpful.<br /></br>e. IV prochlorperazine – anti-migraine/anti-emetic treatment; treating a presumed migraine before excluding SAH is unsafe and violates the “rule out worst-case first” principle.</p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a patient with thunder-clap headache and normal non-contrast CT, lumbar puncture is mandatory to exclude subarachnoid hemorrhage.</p>
19. A 45-year-old woman comes to the clinic because of a 2-month history of increasingly severe epigastric pain, bloating, and intermittent nausea and vomiting. During this time, she has had decreased appetite resulting in a 3.6-kg (8-lb) weight loss. She has hypertension and type 2 diabetes mellitus. The conditions have been poorly controlled during the past 2 years, because she has not been adherent to her medication regimen. The patient has no history of operative procedures. Medications are metformin, insulin glargine, atorvastatin, lisinopril, and daily aspirin. She appears well. She is 157 cm (5 ft 2 in) tall and weighs 91 kg (200 lb); BMI is 37 kg/m2. Temperature is 37.0°C (98.6°F), pulse is 78/min, respirations are 12/min, and blood pressure is 124/68 mm Hg. The abdomen is protuberant; there is no organomegaly or tenderness to palpation. Bowel sounds are decreased. Results of a complete blood count, metabolic profile, and determination of serum lipase activity are within the reference ranges. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography</br>b. Esophageal manometry</br>c. Gastric emptying study</br>d. MRI of the abdomen</br>e. Ultrasonography of the abdomen</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 2-month history of progressively worse <strong>epigastric pain</strong>, post-prandial <strong>bloating</strong>, <strong>nausea/vomiting</strong>, and <strong>weight loss</strong><br /></br>  – Normal CBC, chemistry panel, and <strong>serum lipase</strong><br /></br>  – No surgical history; BMI 37 kg/m² (risk for gallstone disease)<br /></br>  – <strong>Poorly controlled diabetes</strong> for 2 years (a clue that autonomic neuropathy–related gastroparesis is possible, but also a risk factor for gallstones and pancreatobiliary disease)<br /></br>  – <strong>Decreased bowel sounds</strong> (suggests a partial obstructive process in the proximal gut rather than esophageal dysmotility)</p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No jaundice, pruritus, fever, or leukocytosis (argues against acute cholangitis)<br /></br>  – Normal lipase (makes acute pancreatitis less likely but does not exclude chronic pancreatic or biliary ductal pathology)<br /></br>  – No dysphagia, odynophagia, or reflux symptoms (esophageal manometry less useful)</p></br><p>Taken together, the main diagnostic fork in the road is “Is this primarily a <strong>mechanical/structural pancreatobiliary disorder</strong> producing gastric outlet–type symptoms, or is it <strong>diabetic gastroparesis</strong>?”  The NBME expects you to prioritize structural lesions (which may need urgent intervention) over a functional motility disorder.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. A gallstone or benign stricture in the distal common bile duct or pancreatic duct can intermittently obstruct the <strong>ampulla of Vater</strong>, giving rise to post-prandial epigastric pain, nausea/vomiting, and progressive gastric outlet–type symptoms.<br /></br>2. Obstruction of the pancreatic duct can be indolent; serum lipase is frequently normal in <strong>chronic obstructive pancreatitis</strong>.<br /></br>3. ERCP is the only test on the list that is both <strong>diagnostic AND therapeutic</strong> for ampullary/common bile duct obstruction—allowing stone extraction or stent placement at the same session. Because this patient has worrisome weight loss and obstructive‐type symptoms un­explained by labs, ERCP is the most efficient next step.</p></br><p>Take-home message: In a middle-aged patient with risk factors for gallstones who presents with progressive gastric-outlet–type symptoms and weight loss, evaluate the pancreatobiliary tree with the modality that can diagnose AND treat—ERCP.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls for a real encounter<br /></br>• Characterize pain, dietary triggers, and vomiting frequency<br /></br>• Past imaging/laboratory data reviewed<br /></br>• Discussed risks/benefits of ERCP, including pancreatitis, bleeding, perforation<br /></br>• Informed consent obtained; patient agrees</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Gallstones (cholesterol stones are highly associated with obesity, female sex, age &gt;40—the classic “4 F’s”) can migrate into the common bile duct.<br /></br>• Intermittent obstruction at the ampulla can block both bile and pancreatic juice outflow, causing painless jaundice, pancreatitis, or, as in this case, an indolent, non-inflammatory obstructive picture with gastric stasis.<br /></br>• Chronic obstruction → fibrosis of the pancreatic duct system → chronic pancreatitis (often with normal lipase/amylase).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic Tests &amp; Treatment Strategy<br /></br>1. <strong>ERCP</strong><br /></br>   – Diagnostic: direct cholangiopancreatography to visualize filling defects, strictures, or ampullary lesions<br /></br>   – Therapeutic: sphincterotomy, stone extraction, stenting<br /></br>2. If ERCP is non-diagnostic or contraindicated: MRCP or endoscopic ultrasound (EUS) may follow.<br /></br>3. Subsequent management depends on findings (cholecystectomy for stones, stent for stricture, biopsy for mass).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Pain + Pounds lost + Post-prandial vomiting in an Obese 40-ish woman” (the 4 P-O mnemonic) → think <strong>Obstructive gallstone at the Ampulla</strong> → ERCP.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Less Appropriate<br /></br>b. Esophageal manometry – Evaluates esophageal motility disorders (achalasia, spasm). Vignette lacks dysphagia or chest pain.<br /></br>c. Gastric emptying study – Appropriate for suspected diabetic gastroparesis, but structural causes producing a pseudo-outlet obstruction must be ruled out first; ERCP can both diagnose and treat the more urgent obstructive possibility.<br /></br>d. MRI abdomen – Non-invasive view of biliary tree (MRCP), but cannot provide therapy, so a positive study would still require ERCP; NBME often favors the “one-stop shop” when intervention may be needed.<br /></br>e. Ultrasonography – First-line for gallstones in many settings, but it frequently misses common bile duct stones and ampullary lesions in obese patients; would not provide therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Common Pitfall<br /></br>Jumping straight to a gastric emptying scan in a diabetic patient with nausea/vomiting without first excluding a surgically correctable mechanical obstruction of the biliary or pancreatic ducts can delay definitive treatment and worsen outcomes.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Progressive epigastric pain, early satiety, vomiting, and weight loss in an obese middle-aged woman should prompt direct evaluation of the pancreatobiliary tree with a modality that allows immediate therapy—Endoscopic Retrograde Cholangiopancreatography.</p>
20. A 54-year-old man comes to the office because of a 3-week history of a dull ache along the back of his left ankle that is worse in the morning when he gets out of bed. The pain also worsens when he plays tennis on weekends. Acetaminophen once daily has provided moderate relief of the pain. Medical history is unremarkable, and the patient takes no other medications. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 99 kg (219 lb); BMI is 31 kg/m2. Vital signs are within normal limits. The patient appears well. There is mild swelling and tenderness to palpation at the proximal aspect of the Achilles tendon. The remainder of the examination discloses no abnormalities. Thompson test is negative. In addition to recommending rest, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Add oxycodone as needed for pain  </br>b. Increase the frequency of acetaminophen to four times daily  </br>c. Inject cortisone into the calcaneal insertion site of the Achilles tendon  </br>d. Recommend physical therapy  </br>e. Schedule surgical intervention for the Achilles tendon</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Recommend physical therapy</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point to Achilles tendinopathy (chronic over-use injury, NOT rupture)<br /></br>• 3-week history → subacute, over-use pattern rather than acute trauma.<br /></br>• Dull ache at the back of the ankle, worse with first steps in the morning and with tennis → classic for tendinopathy/plantar fasciitis–type over-use pain.<br /></br>• Mild swelling and point tenderness at the proximal Achilles tendon.<br /></br>• Negative Thompson test (calf squeeze produces plantar-flexion) → rules out complete or even partial Achilles rupture.<br /></br>• Risk factor: BMI 31 (obesity) + weekend sports (“weekend warrior”).  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No sudden “pop,” no ecchymosis, no inability to plantar-flex → argues against rupture.<br /></br>• No systemic illness → argues against inflammatory arthropathy or infection.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Why PT is next  </p></br><p>Step-wise management of Achilles tendinopathy:<br /></br>1. Activity modification &amp; relative rest (already advised).<br /></br>2. Conservative therapy aimed at correcting the underlying mechanical overload:<br /></br>   • Structured physical therapy focusing on eccentric calf-muscle strengthening, stretching of the gastrocnemius–soleus complex, heel-cord flexibility, and proprioceptive training.<br /></br>   • Footwear change, heel lifts, or orthotics may be added.<br /></br>3. NSAIDs or acetaminophen may be used adjunctively but do NOT treat the root cause.<br /></br>4. Corticosteroid injection is avoided because it markedly increases risk of tendon rupture.<br /></br>5. Surgery reserved only for chronic (&gt;6–12 months) refractory cases or significant structural tear.</p></br><p>Thus, after rest, the single most appropriate “next” evidence-based intervention is referral to physical therapy.</p></br><p>Take-home message: Chronic over-use tendon disorders are treated first-line with a structured PT program emphasizing eccentric strengthening; steroid injections around weight-bearing tendons are contraindicated.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Note duration, pain characteristics, negative Thompson test, BMI, activity modification advised, and referral placed to PT.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Achilles tendinopathy = degenerative microtears &amp; mucoid degeneration within tendon from repetitive overload → pain &amp; morning stiffness. Unlike tendinitis (acute inflammation), histology shows collagen disorganization and neovascularization—hence why mechanical rehabilitation, not anti-inflammatory injection, is cornerstone therapy.</p></br><p>Risk factors: sudden increase in activity, obesity, poor calf flexibility, fluoroquinolones or steroids (not present here), inflammatory arthropathy.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>Diagnostics: Primarily clinical; ultrasound or MRI only if diagnosis is uncertain or to evaluate partial tear.<br /></br>Therapy:<br /></br>• Relative rest / activity modification.<br /></br>• Eccentric calf exercises (eg, heel drop off a step).<br /></br>• Supportive measures: heel lifts, proper shoes.<br /></br>• Oral NSAIDs/acetaminophen for pain (short-term).<br /></br>• Shock-wave therapy or platelet-rich plasma—experimental.<br /></br>• Surgery: debridement or tendon augmentation only after ≥6 months failed conservative management.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“ACE” the Achilles problem:<br /></br>A – Activity modification<br /></br>C – Calf eccentric strengthening (PT)<br /></br>E – Evaluate for rupture with Thompson test  </p></br><p>Steroids “SNAP” tendons → avoid injections in weight-bearing tendons (Achilles, patellar).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Add oxycodone as needed for pain<br /></br>• Opioids are inappropriate first-line therapy for mild-moderate musculoskeletal pain; do nothing for pathologic cause; high misuse potential.</p></br><p>b. Increase acetaminophen to four times daily<br /></br>• Treats symptoms only and risks hepatotoxicity (max 4 g/day). Does not address mechanical overload; patient already getting only “moderate” relief.</p></br><p>c. Inject cortisone into the calcaneal insertion site<br /></br>• Temporarily decreases pain but greatly increases risk of Achilles rupture; therefore contraindicated in tendinopathy of weight-bearing tendons.</p></br><p>e. Schedule surgical intervention for the Achilles tendon<br /></br>• Reserved for complete rupture or chronic tendinopathy unresponsive to ≥6–12 months of conservative therapy. Not indicated after only 3 weeks of symptoms.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────</p>
21. A 10-year cohort study is conducted to investigate the effect of cigarette smoking on the incidence of head and neck cancers in adults. A total of 10,000 adult participants with no history of any type of cancer are enrolled in the study; 2000 participants are current cigarette smokers and 8000 are current nonsmokers and have never smoked cigarettes. During the 10-year study period, 20 smokers and eight nonsmokers develop head and neck cancer. Based on these data, which of the following best represents the attributable risk from smoking for head and neck cancer among current cigarette smokers?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. 0.001</br>b. 0.009</br>c. 0.01</br>d. 0.09</br>e. 0.1</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. 0.009  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key numbers: 20 cancers / 2000 smokers, 8 cancers / 8000 nonsmokers.<br /></br>• Study design: Prospective cohort → lets you measure incidence directly.<br /></br>• Question stem asks for “attributable risk from smoking … among current cigarette smokers” → classic epidemiology term meaning risk difference (incidence in exposed – incidence in unexposed).  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-by-step:<br /></br>1. Calculate incidence in the exposed group (smokers):<br /></br>   20 ÷ 2000 = 0.01 (1% over 10 yr)<br /></br>2. Calculate incidence in the unexposed group (nonsmokers):<br /></br>   8 ÷ 8000 = 0.001 (0.1% over 10 yr)<br /></br>3. Attributable risk (AR) = IE – IU = 0.01 – 0.001 = 0.009  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: AR quantifies the absolute excess risk in the exposed group that can be attributed to the exposure itself. Here, 0.009 means 0.9% (or 9 excess cases per 1000 smokers in 10 years) are attributable to smoking.  </p></br><p>If documenting in a chart or manuscript: “Incidence among smokers 1%, among nonsmokers 0.1%; attributable risk 0.9%.”  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Tobacco smoke contains carcinogens (e.g., polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons, nitrosamines) that cause DNA adduct formation, p53 mutations → malignant transformation in oropharyngeal &amp; laryngeal mucosa.<br /></br>• Risk rises with duration and intensity; cessation reduces—but does not eliminate—risk.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Not directly tested, but head &amp; neck cancer work-up involves flexible laryngoscopy, CT/MRI, and biopsy.<br /></br>• Main preventive “treatment” is smoking cessation; counseling and pharmacotherapies (varenicline, bupropion, NRT) recommended.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“AR = ‘Added Risk’ ” → think of what was added when you smoked.<br /></br>Formula box:<br /></br>• IE = a ÷ (a + b)<br /></br>• IU = c ÷ (c + d)<br /></br>• AR = IE – IU  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 0.001<br /></br>• This is the incidence in nonsmokers, not the excess risk.  </p></br><p>c. 0.01<br /></br>• This is the incidence in smokers, not the difference.  </p></br><p>d. 0.09<br /></br>• Ten-fold too large; would imply a 9% absolute excess, inconsistent with the numbers.  </p></br><p>e. 0.1<br /></br>• Misplaces the decimal; 10% risk difference is not supported by the given incidences.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: confusing attributable risk (absolute difference) with relative risk (ratio IE / IU = 0.01 / 0.001 = 10). Remember: “relative = ratio, attributable = subtraction.”</p>
22. A 15-year-old girl is brought to the office for a well-child examination. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Her temperature is 36.9°C (98.4°F), pulse is 86/min, respirations are 16/min, and blood pressure is 100/60 mm Hg. A photograph of the back is shown. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. In addition to routine annual growth and development assessments, which of the following is the most appropriate recommendation regarding routine screening in this patient?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Yearly CT scan of the chest and abdomen  </br>b. Yearly MRI of the brain  </br>c. Yearly nerve conduction studies  </br>d. Yearly ophthalmologic examination  </br>e. No additional screening is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Yearly ophthalmologic examination  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age &amp; sex: 15-year-old girl (classic age when cutaneous stigmata of neurocutaneous syndromes are obvious).<br /></br>• Physical finding (photo of back): Multiple café-au-lait macules and/or cutaneous neurofibromas are the only skin lesions that would prompt a USMLE question about additional screening in an otherwise healthy teen.<br /></br>• Normal vitals and exam otherwise → no acute illness, focus shifts to anticipatory guidance/surveillance for complications of an underlying genetic disorder.  </p></br><p>Putting these clues together → Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1. Recognize NF1:<br /></br>  – ≥ 6 café-au-lait spots (&gt;1.5 cm post-puberty) ± axillary/inguinal freckling, neurofibromas, Lisch nodules.<br /></br>Step 2. Identify the most common &amp; potentially vision-threatening complication in childhood/adolescence → optic pathway glioma (seen in up to 15% of NF1 patients, usually before age 8 but can develop later).<br /></br>Step 3. Evidence-based surveillance: annual dilated ophthalmologic exam to check visual acuity, color vision, fields, and for Lisch nodules/optic disc changes. This is endorsed by the American Academy of Pediatrics and NF1 consensus guidelines.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Patients with NF1 require yearly eye exams to detect asymptomatic optic gliomas early and preserve vision.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Gene: NF1 tumor-suppressor (neurofibromin) on chromosome 17 ⇒ ↑ Ras activity ⇒ tumor risk.<br /></br>• Complications: optic glioma, learning disabilities, scoliosis, pheochromocytoma, renal artery stenosis, malignant peripheral nerve-sheath tumors.<br /></br>• Optic pathway gliomas present insidiously → ↓ visual acuity, proptosis, strabismus ⇒ need surveillance rather than waiting for symptoms.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• No routine imaging unless visual symptoms/abnormal exam.<br /></br>• Other routine care: annual BP (renovascular HTN, pheochromocytoma), yearly skin exam, developmental assessment.<br /></br>• MRI only if ophthalmologic exam abnormal or neurologic Sx.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“SEE NF1 every year”:  S = Skin exam, E = Eye exam, E = Education (learning assessment). Remember “Eyes on 17” (chromosome 17 → yearly eye check).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Yearly CT of chest/abdomen – Not recommended; radiation risk; abdominal imaging reserved for symptoms or catecholamine excess suggesting pheochromocytoma.<br /></br>b. Yearly brain MRI – Costly, anesthesia in kids, low yield without symptoms or eye findings; reserved for abnormal neuro-ophthalmic exam.<br /></br>c. Yearly nerve conduction studies – Useful for peripheral neuropathies or carpal tunnel, not for NF1 surveillance.<br /></br>e. No additional screening – Wrong because optic glioma risk mandates proactive eye exams.</p></br><p>Key differences: Only the ophthalmologic examination detects the silent, common, vision-threatening complication specific to NF1, whereas the other options screen for conditions with much lower incidence or add unnecessary risk/cost.</p>
23. A 7-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she is concerned that her daughter has had two episodes of vaginal bleeding during the past 3 months. She also has had breast development during this time. Development had been appropriate for age. She is at the 48th percentile for height and 47th percentile for weight and BMI. Breast development is sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 2, and axillary and pubic hair development is SMR stage 3. Examination shows normal-appearing external genitalia. Pelvic examination is deferred. An MRI of the pelvis shows a normal vagina and cervix. The uterus is 4 cm long, and a 7 × 6-cm left ovarian mass is seen. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Chromosomal analysis</br>b. Measurement of serum CA 125 concentration</br>c. CT scan of the pituitary gland</br>d. Fine-needle aspiration of the mass</br>e. Left oophorectomy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Left oophorectomy  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age &lt; 8 yr + vaginal bleeding + breast budding = true precocious puberty.<br /></br>• SMR 2 breasts + SMR 3 pubic hair but NO growth spurt → suggests peripheral (gonadotropin-independent) source rather than central GnRH activation.<br /></br>• MRI: 7 × 6-cm unilateral ovarian mass → obvious estrogen-producing source.<br /></br>• Normal pituitary &amp; CNS imaging already done (pelvic MRI), so focus shifts to the ovary.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives<br /></br>– Early thelarche/menarche<br /></br>– Large unilateral ovarian mass<br /></br>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>– No neurologic symptoms or café-au-lait spots (rules out central cause, McCune-Albright)<br /></br>– Normal external genitalia (rules out virilizing tumor)  </p></br><p>Pattern: Isosexual peripheral precocious puberty caused by an estrogen-secreting ovarian tumor (classically a juvenile granulosa-cell tumor).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Child is &lt; 8 yr with secondary sexual characteristics → precocious puberty.<br /></br>Step 2: Pelvic imaging already localizes a 7-cm ovarian lesion → peripheral estrogen source.<br /></br>Step 3: First task is BOTH diagnosis and definitive treatment of that lesion. In pediatric ovarian neoplasms, the standard is surgical excision with unilateral salpingo-oophorectomy to:<br /></br>  a) obtain tissue for histology/staging,<br /></br>  b) prevent rupture/spillage, and<br /></br>  c) cure the hormonally driven symptoms.  </p></br><p>Take-home: A single, solid ovarian mass in a prepubertal girl with estrogenic signs should be removed surgically, not biopsied or watched.  </p></br><p>Documentation points (real patient): consent for fertility-sparing unilateral oophorectomy, counseling re: path report &amp; follow-up tumor markers.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Juvenile granulosa-cell tumors → secrete estradiol → endometrial proliferation → intermittent bleeding; breast development; advanced bone age.<br /></br>• Non-GnRH driven (low LH/FSH).<br /></br>• Untreated, risk of torsion, rupture, malignant spread.<br /></br>• Surgical resection usually curative (Stage I).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line: Fertility-sparing unilateral oophorectomy with surgical staging (peritoneal washings, omentum biopsy, lymph node sampling).<br /></br>Post-op: monitor inhibin A/B and estradiol as tumor markers.<br /></br>Adjuvant chemo only if advanced stage.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Precocious Puberty + Pelvic mass? → Pop it out.”<br /></br>PP + Palpable/Visible unilateral mass = Peripheral → oophorectomy.<br /></br>Central causes start at the HEAD; Peripheral causes start at the HIPs (ovary/adrenal).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Chromosomal analysis<br /></br>• Indicated for ambiguous genitalia or suspected gonadal dysgenesis (e.g., Turner 45,X). This child has normal female phenotype and an unequivocal mass.  </p></br><p>b. Serum CA-125<br /></br>• Marker for epithelial ovarian cancer in adults; juvenile granulosa tumors rarely elevate CA-125, and the result would not change the immediate need for surgery.  </p></br><p>c. CT scan of the pituitary gland<br /></br>• Central precocious puberty yields bilateral symmetric gonadal enlargement with HIGH LH/FSH, not a unilateral ovarian mass. Imaging the pituitary ignores the obvious peripheral source.  </p></br><p>d. Fine-needle aspiration of the mass<br /></br>• Contraindicated: risk of tumor spillage and peritoneal seeding; tissue obtained is often insufficient for definitive histology/staging. Pediatric ovarian masses go straight to operative removal.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>In a prepubertal girl with estrogenic precocious puberty and a unilateral ovarian mass, prompt unilateral oophorectomy is both diagnostic and curative.</p>
24. A 3-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of a scaling lesion on the scalp for 7 days. The family has no pets; the day-care center that the child attends has a pet turtle. The other children at the day-care center are asymptomatic. Examination of the scalp shows a thin plaque with alopecia and scaling. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy to prevent spread to the children in the day-care center?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Oral griseofulvin for the patient only</br>b. Oral griseofulvin for the patient and his classmates</br>c. Topical hydrocortisone for the patient only</br>d. Topical hydrocortisone for the patient and his classmates</br>e. Topical selenium sulfide for the patient only</br>f. Topical selenium sulfide for the patient and his classmates</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Oral griseofulvin for the patient only  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key buzzwords: “3-year-old,” “thin plaque with alopecia and scaling,” “scalp.”<br /></br>• Pattern recognition: A scaly patch + hair loss in a child = tinea capitis (dermatophyte infection of hair shaft).<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: Active scale, alopecia, 7-day evolution.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No pustules or honey-colored crust (rules out impetigo); no smooth non-scaly patches (rules out alopecia areata).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Diagnose: Scalp dermatophyte infection = tinea capitis.<br /></br>Step 2 – Management principle: Hair-shaft penetration → need systemic (not topical) fungicidal levels.<br /></br>Step 3 – Drug of choice in children: Oral griseofulvin (8 wk typical course).<br /></br>Step 4 – Public-health aspect: Only treat proven cases. Routine prophylaxis of asymptomatic contacts is NOT recommended; instead, monitor and examine classmates.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Tinea capitis always needs an oral antifungal, and prophylactic treatment of contacts is unnecessary unless they develop symptoms.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Scalp kerion / tinea capitis suspected; will start griseofulvin 20–25 mg/kg/day × 6–8 weeks; advise parents to return if classmates develop scalp scaling.”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Dermatophytes (Trichophyton tonsurans &gt; Microsporum).<br /></br>• Mechanism: Fungus invades keratinized hair shaft → inflammatory scaling + hair breakage.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Day-care crowding, hats/combs sharing, pets (esp. cats/dogs). Turtles are irrelevant (they spread Salmonella, not dermatophytes).<br /></br>• Clinical variants: Non-inflammatory “black-dot” pattern, inflammatory kerion, or diffuse scaling/alopecia.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Review<br /></br>1. Oral therapy<br /></br>   – First line: Griseofulvin microsize 20–25 mg/kg/day with fatty meal.<br /></br>   – Alternatives: Terbinafine, itraconazole, fluconazole.<br /></br>2. Adjuncts<br /></br>   – Selenium sulfide or ketoconazole shampoo 2–3×/wk decreases spore shedding but does NOT replace systemic therapy.<br /></br>3. Monitoring: LFTs rarely needed with short-course griseofulvin; counsel about photosensitivity.<br /></br>4. No prophylactic systemic therapy for classmates; simply screen for symptoms.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Tips<br /></br>• “Tinea CAPitis needs oral CAPSules.”<br /></br>• Topical for skin, Oral for scalp/nails = TOpical:Trunk, Oral:On head/Onychomycosis (T-O mnemonic).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Oral griseofulvin for patient &amp; classmates – Over-treats healthy contacts; unnecessary exposure to drug side effects.<br /></br>c/d. Topical hydrocortisone – Steroid would mask inflammation, worsen infection (“tinea incognito”); no antifungal activity.<br /></br>e. Topical selenium sulfide for patient only – Helpful adjunct but inadequate monotherapy; fungus persists inside hair shafts.<br /></br>f. Topical selenium sulfide for patient &amp; classmates – Same inadequacy + needless prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Confusing tinea capitis with seborrheic dermatitis (also scaly). Remember alopecia + need for systemic therapy distinguish tinea capitis.</p>
25. A 5-year-old boy is brought to the physician by his parents as a new patient because they are concerned that he may have an immunodeficiency. The family recently moved to the area. The parents say their son’s previous physician had been examining him for the condition. The patient has had six episodes of pneumonia and 10 episodes of sinusitis since birth. He underwent uncomplicated surgical drainage of the sinuses at the ages of 3 and 4 years. He had his first episode of otitis media at the age of 2 months and underwent uncomplicated placement of tympanostomy tubes at the age of 7 months. His umbilical stump fell off at the age of 2 weeks. The patient’s previous leukocyte, lymphocyte, and platelet counts; serum immunoglobulin and CH50 concentrations; and diphtheria, tetanus, pneumococcal, and Haemophilus influenzae type b titers are within the reference ranges. Six previous chest x-rays showed lower lobe consolidations in inconsistent locations; subsequent x-rays showed clearing of the infiltrates. Today, vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no abnormalities. A CT scan of the chest is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Common variable immunodeficiency</br>b. Complement deficiency</br>c. Leukocyte adhesion defect</br>d. Primary ciliary dyskinesia</br>e. Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Primary ciliary dyskinesia (Kartagener syndrome spectrum)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues that immediately narrow the differential:</br>• Recurrent pyogenic upper- and lower-airway infections beginning in early infancy: ≥6 pneumonias, ≥10 sinus infections, recurrent otitis media that required tubes.<br /></br>• Completely normal quantitative immune studies: normal total leukocyte/lymphocyte counts, platelet count, immunoglobulin levels, vaccine-specific antibody titers, and CH50.<br /></br>• Umbilical stump detached at 2 weeks (normal) ⇒ rules out leukocyte adhesion defect (cord separation classically &gt;30 days).<br /></br>• Chest CT (image not provided to you) in a child with this history is almost certainly showing bronchiectasis—permanent dilation of airways from repeated infection/poor clearance.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: “Normal labs + chronic otitis/sinusitis/pneumonia + bronchiectasis = impaired mucociliary clearance (primary ciliary dyskinesia).”</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-by-Step)<br /></br>1. Recurrent sinopulmonary disease suggests either antibody deficiency, complement defect, neutrophil problem, or mucociliary problem.<br /></br>2. Normal quantitative/functional antibody studies and normal complement (CH50) eliminate humoral and complement deficiencies.<br /></br>3. Normal cord separation, no history of skin/soft-tissue abscesses, and no leukocytosis eliminate neutrophil adhesion/chemotaxis defects.<br /></br>4. Remaining plausible etiology: failure to physically clear mucus and bacteria → primary ciliary dyskinesia.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When infections are confined almost exclusively to the respiratory tract, AND the immune evaluation is normal, think of a structural/mechanical clearance problem—primary ciliary dyskinesia.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Recurrent sinopulmonary infections since infancy with normal immune work-up, CT evidence of bronchiectasis—suspicious for primary ciliary dyskinesia; refer for genetic testing and fertility counseling.”</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Autosomal-recessive defects in genes encoding dynein arms, radial spokes, or other axonemal proteins → immotile/ dyskinetic 9+2 cilia.<br /></br>• Consequences: impaired mucociliary escalator ⇒ chronic ear, sinus, and lung infections → bronchiectasis; 50% have situs inversus (Kartagener triad: bronchiectasis, chronic sinusitis, situs inversus).<br /></br>• Immotile sperm and impaired fallopian tube cilia ⇒ infertility/subfertility.<br /></br>• Normal immune cell numbers and function; no effect on immunoglobulin synthesis.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Management &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Diagnostics: nasal nitric oxide ↓ (screening), high-speed video microscopy of ciliary beat, electron microscopy of axoneme, or genetic testing.<br /></br>Treatment:<br /></br>1. Aggressive airway clearance (chest physiotherapy, hypertonic saline).<br /></br>2. Prompt culture-directed antibiotics for exacerbations (e.g., amoxicillin-clavulanate, inhaled tobramycin for Pseudomonas).<br /></br>3. Vaccinations (pneumococcal, flu).<br /></br>4. Consider bronchodilators, macrolide prophylaxis, and ultimately lung transplant if severe.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Kartagener’s KART:”<br /></br>K – Karrying infections (recurrent sinopulmonary)<br /></br>A – Autosomal recessive, Ab levels OK<br /></br>R – Reversed organs (situs inversus in 50 %)<br /></br>T – Trouble beating tails (immotile cilia &amp; sperm)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Common variable immunodeficiency (CVID)<br /></br>• Presents later (teen/adult).<br /></br>• Low IgG ± IgA/IgM; poor vaccine titers.<br /></br>• Labs here are normal ⇒ rules out.</p></br><p>b. Complement deficiency<br /></br>• Classic board buzz: recurrent Neisseria (C5–C9) or encapsulated (+low early complement, lupus).<br /></br>• CH50 (total complement activity) would be reduced. It’s normal in this patient.</p></br><p>c. Leukocyte adhesion defect<br /></br>• Delayed (&gt;30 days) umbilical cord separation, recurrent skin/soft-tissue infections without pus, marked neutrophilia. None present here; cord fell at 2 weeks.</p></br><p>e. Wiskott-Aldrich syndrome<br /></br>• X-linked boy with triad: Eczema, small-platelet thrombocytopenia, recurrent infections. Platelet count here normal, no eczema.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>Recurrent otitis, sinusitis, and pneumonias with NORMAL quantitative immune labs should push you to think “structural clearance problem,” most classically primary ciliary dyskinesia.</p>
26. A physician, who is covering for a colleague who is out of town, receives a telephone call at the office from a dentist regarding whether antibiotic prophylaxis is needed for a scaling procedure for the colleague’s patient. The patient is a 50-year-old woman with mitral valve prolapse diagnosed 15 years ago prior to her transferring to the colleague’s care. She has never had endocarditis. Further review of the patient’s medical record indicates no documentation of echocardiography. She also has hypertension, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and an allergy to penicillin. Medications are lisinopril and metformin. Cardiac examination from the patient’s last office visit 6 months ago showed a normal pulse and no murmurs. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management of the colleague’s patient?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Advise the dentist to proceed with the procedure without antibiotic prophylaxis  </br>b. Ask the dentist to postpone the procedure, and then schedule outpatient echocardiography  </br>c. Ask the dentist to postpone the procedure, and then schedule an examination in the office  </br>d. Prescribe amoxicillin to be taken 1 hour before the procedure  </br>e. Prescribe clindamycin to be taken 1 hour before the procedure</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Advise the dentist to proceed with the procedure without antibiotic prophylaxis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key phrases: “mitral valve prolapse (MVP) diagnosed 15 yrs ago,” “never had endocarditis,” “no murmurs,” routine dental “scaling.”<br /></br>• Pertinent negative: no prosthetic material, no prior infective endocarditis (IE), no cyanotic congenital heart disease, no transplant valvulopathy.<br /></br>• Question stem: asks only about need for antibiotic prophylaxis before a dental procedure.</p></br><p>These clues immediately trigger recall of the 2007/2021 AHA guidelines that drastically limited who needs IE prophylaxis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Identify cardiac condition: Simple MVP without audible murmur ⇒ presumed trivial/no regurgitation.<br /></br>2. Apply guideline: IE prophylaxis is reserved for patients at highest morbidity/mortality risk from IE (see list below). MVP—whether or not a murmur or trivial regurgitation is present—is explicitly NOT on the list.<br /></br>3. Dental scaling = procedure that can cause transient bacteremia. But since patient is not in a high-risk cardiac category, no prophylaxis is needed.<br /></br>Core takeaway: For dental work, prophylaxis is only for prosthetic valves, prior IE, certain congenital lesions, or cardiac transplant with regurgitant valvulopathy.</p></br><p>Documentation if real patient: note phone discussion, guideline citation, lack of indication for antibiotics, and plan to proceed.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)</br>• Transient oral streptococcal bacteremia can seed abnormal endocardial surfaces.<br /></br>• Past guidelines included MVP with regurgitation; evidence showed prophylaxis prevented few cases while exposing many to abx risks → recommendations narrowed.</p></br><p>High-Risk Cardiac Lesions Requiring Dental IE Prophylaxis<br /></br>1. Prosthetic cardiac valve or prosthetic material used for repair<br /></br>2. Prior infective endocarditis<br /></br>3. Unrepaired cyanotic congenital heart disease or repaired congenital heart disease with residual shunts/valvular regurgitation, or repair within the past 6 mo<br /></br>4. Cardiac transplant recipients who develop valvulopathy  </p></br><p>Anything else (including isolated MVP, CABG, rheumatic or acquired native valve disease, most congenital lesions post-repair) = NO prophylaxis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• First-line drug when indicated: Amoxicillin 2 g PO 30–60 min pre-procedure.<br /></br>• Penicillin-allergic: Clindamycin 600 mg PO 30–60 min pre-procedure.<br /></br>• No diagnostic testing (e.g., echo) is required solely to decide on prophylaxis if diagnosis and exam already exclude a high-risk lesion.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“PROPHY for the PROs”:<br /></br>P – Previous IE<br /></br>R – Repair material/prosthetic valve<br /></br>O – Ongoing cyanotic CHD (unrepaired)<br /></br>S – Six-month window after prosthetic CHD repair / residual defect<br /></br>+ “Tx” – Transplant valve disease  </p></br><p>Everything else? No pills!</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>B. Postpone for outpatient echo – Unnecessary; decision is guideline-based, not echo-based. Echo would not change risk category.<br /></br>C. Postpone for office exam – She was examined 6 mo ago; guidelines do not require re-examination to decide on prophylaxis.<br /></br>D. Prescribe amoxicillin pre-procedure – Would be correct ONLY if she met high-risk criteria; she doesn’t.<br /></br>E. Prescribe clindamycin pre-procedure – Same reasoning as D, plus used for pen-allergic pts; still not indicated because risk category is low.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>MVP (even with trivial regurgitation) is no longer an indication for infective endocarditis prophylaxis before routine dental procedures. Proceed without antibiotics.</p>
27. A 52-year-old woman with amyotrophic lateral sclerosis has been receiving mechanical ventilation in the hospital for the past 4 weeks. She is taking no medications. Attempts at discontinuing mechanical ventilation have been unsuccessful. Her parents visit her often and participate in her health care decisions. The physician tells her and her parents that she could require mechanical ventilation for several more years and recommends transfer to a facility for ventilator-dependent patients. Although her parents approve of this recommendation, the patient refuses placement and asks to have mechanical ventilation and all other life-sustaining treatments withheld. She says, "I would rather die now than spend the rest of my life in one of those places." Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Ask the parents privately about the patient's prior beliefs about long-term care  </br>b. Assess the patient's mental capacity to make her own health care decisions  </br>c. Continue current treatment and start additional treatment as needed  </br>d. Continue current treatment but do not start any new life-sustaining treatment  </br>e. Seek a court order to appoint a guardian to make her health care decisions</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>b. Assess the patient’s mental capacity to make her own health-care decisions</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• 52-year-old competent-appearing adult (legal decision-maker is the patient, not her parents)<br /></br>• Chronic, progressive, ventilator-dependent ALS → prognosis poor, quality-of-life concerns common<br /></br>• Explicit request: “Have mechanical ventilation and all other life-sustaining treatments withheld. I would rather die now … ”<br /></br>• Conflict: patient refuses, parents approve continued treatment/transfer</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: clear verbal refusal of life-sustaining therapy.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no mention of confusion, psychosis, intoxication, or dementia that would automatically negate capacity.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:</br>1. Identify the decision-maker. In adults, the patient’s autonomy prevails if she has decision-making capacity.<br /></br>2. Before honoring—or overriding—her request, you must verify that capacity is intact.<br /></br>3. If capacity is confirmed, the physician must respect her decision, even if family disagrees.<br /></br>Core takeaway: When an adult patient refuses life-sustaining care, the FIRST action is to evaluate decision-making capacity; everything else follows from that determination.</p></br><p>Document in chart: mental-status examination addressing the four capacity elements, discussion of risks/benefits, patient’s stated preferences.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)</br>ALS → progressive loss of upper &amp; lower motor neurons → respiratory muscle failure → chronic ventilator dependence. Cognition is usually preserved, so most patients remain capable of medical decision-making unless hypoxic, CO2-retaining, delirious, depressed, or on sedatives.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Management Framework</br>Decision-making capacity = ability to<br /></br>1. Communicate a CHOICE<br /></br>2. UNDERSTAND relevant information<br /></br>3. APPRECIATE situation &amp; consequences<br /></br>4. REASON about options<br /></br>(“C-U-A-R” mnemonic)</p></br><p>If any element is absent → lack of capacity → next surrogate (here, parents) or court involvement.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Why Other Options Are Wrong</br>a. Ask parents privately about prior beliefs<br /></br>   • Family input is valuable but never substitutes for assessing the patient’s own capacity first.</p></br><p>c. Continue current treatment and start additional treatment<br /></br>   • Ignores the patient’s explicit refusal; violates autonomy and could be battery if she has capacity.</p></br><p>d. Continue current treatment but do not start any new life-sustaining treatment<br /></br>   • Still disregards her request to discontinue ventilation; autonomy not honored.</p></br><p>e. Seek a court order to appoint a guardian<br /></br>   • Court involvement is unnecessary unless capacity is lost or there is irresolvable disagreement among surrogates.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tip</br>“Capacity FIRST, then Honor or Hand-off.”<br /></br>Whenever a competent adult refuses treatment, remember CAPACITY is the gateway question.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>Before acting on, or overruling, a competent adult’s wish to withdraw life-sustaining therapy, physicians must document a formal capacity assessment. If the patient possesses decision-making capacity, her informed refusal must be respected.</p>
28. A 62-year-old man comes to the physician because of a lesion on his right temple for 3 months. He works as a farmer. Examination shows a 1.5-cm ulcerated lesion over the right temple area; the lesion has a raised margin and a waxy border. If left untreated, which of the following is the most likely sequela of the lesion?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Local spread</br>b. Lymphatic invasion</br>c. Regression</br>d. Systemic metastases</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>a. Local spread</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key phrases: “62-year-old man,” “farmer” (chronic sun exposure), “1.5-cm ulcerated lesion … raised margin and waxy border” on a sun-exposed area (temple).<br /></br>• Classic description of a pearly/waxy, rolled (raised) border with central ulceration → basal cell carcinoma (BCC).<br /></br>• BCC is the most common skin cancer and is strongly linked to cumulative UV-B exposure; it arises in older, fair-skinned adults who spend time outdoors.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify skin cancer pattern: pearly, telangiectatic, rolled/raised edge = BCC.<br /></br>2. Recall natural history: BCC grows slowly, invades locally, destroys surrounding tissue (cartilage, bone) but almost never metastasizes (&lt;0.1%).<br /></br>3. Therefore, untreated sequela = progressive LOCAL tissue destruction.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Basal cell carcinoma is “locally aggressive but rarely travels.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Etiology: malignant proliferation of basal keratinocytes due to DNA damage from chronic UV-B exposure; p53 mutations common.<br /></br>• Risk factors: fair skin, old age, chronic sun/UV exposure, arsenic, immunosuppression, radiation therapy.<br /></br>• Types: nodular (pearly papule with telangiectasias), ulcerated (“rodent ulcer”), superficial, morpheaform.<br /></br>• Behavior: Invades dermis/subcutis → erosion of cartilage, bone, orbital contents if near eye. Metastasis and lymphatic spread are extraordinarily rare.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review</br>• Diagnosis: Usually clinical + shave or punch biopsy for histology (peripheral palisading nuclei).<br /></br>• Management: Surgical excision with clear margins (standard excision or Mohs micrographic surgery for cosmetically sensitive areas), electrodessication &amp; curettage, or topical imiquimod/5-FU for superficial lesions.<br /></br>• Advanced/unresectable: Vismodegib (hedgehog pathway inhibitor).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“BBC = Basal cell ‘Behaves Calmly’ (doesn’t travel), just keeps broadcasting locally.”<br /></br>Picture a local TV news station (BCC) that never sends reporters out of town—only covers its own neighborhood.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>b. Lymphatic invasion<br /></br>– Typical of squamous cell carcinoma (SCC) of skin, which presents as a keratotic or ulcerated plaque/nodule often on lower lip/hands and is more likely to spread to regional lymph nodes.</p></br><p>c. Regression<br /></br>– Seen with benign lesions like seborrheic keratoses or thin superficial actinic keratoses; BCC does not spontaneously regress.</p></br><p>d. Systemic metastases<br /></br>– Characteristic concern for melanoma (especially nodular subtype, Breslow depth &gt;1 mm). BCC metastasis is exceedingly rare.</p></br><p>Bottom line: The waxy rolled-edge ulcer on a sun-exposed area is basal cell carcinoma, which, if ignored, erodes adjacent structures but virtually never spreads distantly—local spread is the expected outcome.</p>
29. A 57-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a 6-hour history of fatigue and mild shortness of breath. He has type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, and anuric renal failure requiring hemodialysis. He reports having missed his regularly scheduled dialysis session 2 days ago. His current medications are enalapril, amlodipine, insulin, calcitriol, and calcium supplementation. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 110/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 160/100 mm Hg. Examination shows no jugular venous distention. Crackles are heard at both lung bases. Heart sounds are normal. Serum studies show a potassium concentration of 6.8 mEq/L, urea nitrogen concentration of 120 mg/dL, and creatinine concentration of 9.9 mg/dL. An ECG shows sinus tachycardia at 115/min, with widening of the PR and QRS intervals and peaked T waves. Intravenous administration of which of the following is the most appropriate next step in treatment?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Atenolol</br>b. Calcium chloride</br>c. Furosemide</br>d. Lidocaine</br>e. Magnesium sulfate</br>f. 0.9% Saline</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>b. Calcium chloride (IV bolus)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key findings: missed hemodialysis, anuric renal failure → impaired K⁺ excretion; potassium 6.8 mEq/L; ECG with widened PR/QRS and peaked T waves (classic hyperkalemia changes); symptoms (fatigue, dyspnea) likely from uremia and pulmonary edema.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no JVD → pulmonary congestion from volume/renal failure but not massive right-sided failure.<br /></br>• The dangerous element is the rapidly progressive ECG change, not volume status; predicting ventricular arrhythmia is the examiner’s focus.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach to hyperkalemia:</br>1. Recognize severe hyperkalemia (K⁺ ≥6.5 mEq/L or any ECG change).<br /></br>2. Immediately stabilize cardiac myocyte membrane with IV calcium (chloride or gluconate).<br /></br>3. Temporize serum K⁺ shift into cells (IV insulin + glucose, β₂-agonist, NaHCO₃ if acidotic).<br /></br>4. Remove K⁺ from body (hemodialysis, loop diuretics, sodium zirconium cyclosilicate, etc.).<br /></br>The first, life-saving move is step 1: IV calcium. Calcium chloride has three times more elemental Ca²⁺ than calcium gluconate and works within 1–3 min.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• In hyperkalemia, elevated extracellular K⁺ partially depolarizes cardiac myocyte resting membrane potential, slowing phase 0 upstroke (Na⁺ channels inactivated) → PR prolongation, QRS widening, sine-wave, ventricular fibrillation/asystole.<br /></br>• IV calcium raises the threshold potential, restoring the gap between resting and threshold, counteracting membrane excitability without changing serum K⁺.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>Immediate: 1 g CaCl₂ IV push over 5 min, cardiac monitor.<br /></br>Follow with insulin + dextrose, albuterol, bicarbonate if warranted, and emergent dialysis (definitive K⁺ removal).<br /></br>Document: indication (K⁺ 6.8, ECG changes), dose/time, rhythm response, plan for dialysis.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“C A B, D”:<br /></br>C – Calcium (stabilize)<br /></br>A – Albuterol, insulin + Dextrose (shift)<br /></br>B – Bicarbonate (if acidotic)<br /></br>D – Dialysis/Diuretics/Drugs to dump K⁺ (remove)</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Atenolol – β-blockers actually block β₂-mediated K⁺ shift into cells and can exacerbate hyperkalemia; does not stabilize myocardium.<br /></br>c. Furosemide – Requires functioning kidneys; patient is anuric. No immediate cardiac protection.<br /></br>d. Lidocaine – Antiarrhythmic for ventricular ectopy/ischemia; does nothing for membrane stabilization in hyperkalemia.<br /></br>e. Magnesium sulfate – Used for torsades de pointes or hypomagnesemia; irrelevant here.<br /></br>f. 0.9% Saline – Helpful for prerenal azotemia or volume depletion; will not correct K⁺ and could worsen pulmonary edema.</p></br><p>Take-Home Message</br>Any patient with severe hyperkalemia and ECG changes gets IV calcium FIRST to prevent fatal arrhythmia, then therapies to shift and remove potassium.</p>
30. A 13-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of intermittent pain in his right thigh during the past 6 weeks. The symptoms are exacerbated by running, and his mother reports that he limps for 30 minutes after running. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He recalls no recent trauma to either leg. He has practiced with the school football team three times weekly for the past 2 months. He is at the 75th percentile for height and 95th percentile for weight and BMI. Examination shows a normal gait. There is no tenderness of the right hip or thigh. Internal rotation of the right hip is difficult, especially when the knee is flexed. Genital and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 3. An x-ray of the hips is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate treatment?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Physical therapy</br>b. Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug therapy</br>c. Cast immobilization of the right hip and thigh</br>d. Surgical stabilization of the femoral head</br>e. No treatment is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Surgical stabilization of the femoral head (urgent in-situ single-screw pinning)</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• Age/sex: 13-year-old boy in early puberty (SMR 3).<br /></br>• Body habitus: 95th percentile weight/BMI → classic risk factor.<br /></br>• Symptoms: 6-week insidious thigh/hip pain, worse with activity; transient limp.<br /></br>• Exam: Limited/“painful” internal rotation of the flexed hip (obligatory external rotation). No point tenderness. Normal gait when not provoked.<br /></br>• Imaging: Hip film (not shown) in this setting almost certainly demonstrates posterior–inferior displacement of the femoral epiphysis relative to the neck (“ice-cream slipping off the cone”).  </p></br><p>Pattern = Slipped Capital Femoral Epiphysis (SCFE).</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. Overweight early-teen with hip/thigh/knee pain → think SCFE.<br /></br>2. Provocative finding: loss of internal rotation (earliest motion lost in SCFE).<br /></br>3. Radiograph confirms slip → next step is to prevent further displacement &amp; avascular necrosis.<br /></br>4. Gold-standard management = prompt surgical in-situ pinning (percutaneous screw fixation); patient placed non–weight bearing until surgery.</p></br><p>Take-away: Any suspected SCFE = crutches + ortho surgery ASAP. Conservative measures are inadequate and dangerous.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: neurovascular status of limb, degree of slip (Southwick angle), counsel non-weight bearing, surgical consent.</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Pathophysiology High-Yield Review</br>• Etiology: Weakening of hypertrophic zone of the physis during rapid growth → shear stress causes epiphysis to slip posterior-inferiorly.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Obesity, rapid growth spurt, endocrine disorders (hypothyroid, GH deficiency).<br /></br>• Complications if untreated: Avascular necrosis, chondrolysis, early osteoarthritis, limb-length discrepancy.</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Snapshot</br>• Immediate: Non-weight-bearing crutches.<br /></br>• Definitive: Single-screw in-situ fixation of the epiphysis (sometimes prophylactic pinning of the contralateral hip).<br /></br>• Follow-up: Serial radiographs, weight-management, screen for endocrine disease if bilateral or atypical.</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Memorization Tips</br>“SCFE = Slip in a Chunky (obese) Kid who Can’t internally Fully Externally rotate.”<br /></br>Picture your ice-cream slipping off a cone held by a chubby teenager.</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Physical therapy – Strengthening/flexibility does not stop physeal slippage and risks worsening displacement.<br /></br>b. NSAID therapy – May reduce pain but fails to address mechanical instability; delays care.<br /></br>c. Cast immobilization – Casting cannot control shear forces across the physis; not standard of care and also delays definitive management.<br /></br>e. No treatment – Untreated SCFE progresses to severe deformity, avascular necrosis, and early degenerative joint disease.</p></br><p>════════════════════════════════════════════════════</br>Core Exam Pearl</br>Any adolescent (especially overweight) with hip, thigh, or referred knee pain + limited internal rotation → order bilateral hip radiographs and keep non-weight bearing; urgent ortho referral for surgical pinning.</p>
31. A 47-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-month history of substernal chest tightness and shortness of breath associated with exercise and relieved by rest. She previously walked 2 miles daily, but now she is limited to walking one-half block because of chest pain, dyspnea, and light-headedness. She has never smoked and drinks one to two beers daily. Her temperature is 36.8°C (98.2°F), pulse is 98/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 110/82 mm Hg. Jugular venous pressure is 8 cm H2O. Crackles are heard at both lung bases. The point of maximal impulse is increased but not displaced; S1 is normal, but S2 is decreased. There is a grade 3/6, harsh systolic murmur at the cardiac base. Serum studies show a total cholesterol concentration of 210 mg/dL, HDL-cholesterol concentration of 95 mg/dL, and LDL-cholesterol concentration of 100 mg/dL. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's heart failure?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Alcohol use disorder</br>b. Amyloidosis</br>c. Anemia</br>d. Aortic stenosis</br>e. Atrial fibrillation</br>f. Cor pulmonale</br>g. Hemochromatosis</br>h. Ischemic heart disease</br>i. Mitral regurgitation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Aortic stenosis  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Progressive exertional chest tightness, dyspnea, and presyncope/light-headedness = classic “angina, dyspnea, syncope” triad of severe aortic stenosis (AS).<br /></br>• Relief with rest → supply-demand ischemia from fixed outflow obstruction.<br /></br>• Harsh grade 3/6 systolic murmur at the cardiac base (right 2nd intercostal space) → classic AS murmur.<br /></br>• Decreased S2 → heavily calcified/immobile aortic valve cusps.<br /></br>• Point of maximal impulse (PMI) “increased but not displaced” → concentric LV hypertrophy produced by pressure overload (no volume overload, so PMI not laterally displaced).<br /></br>• Crackles = pulmonary congestion from LV failure.<br /></br>• JVP 8 cm H2O (upper limit of normal) rules against significant right-sided failure or cor pulmonale.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no irregularly irregular rhythm (AF), no holosystolic murmur (MR), no alcohol history suggesting cardiomyopathy, no anemia signs, no COPD for cor pulmonale, no systemic disease clues for amyloid/hemochromatosis.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify murmur characteristics → harsh midsystolic, base, diminished A2 = AS.<br /></br>2. Correlate symptoms (exertional angina, dyspnea, presyncope) with severe AS.<br /></br>3. Recognize pressure overload → concentric LVH → diastolic then systolic HF → pulmonary edema.<br /></br>Take-home: In a middle-aged patient with exertional angina/DOE + harsh systolic base murmur + soft/absent A2, think aortic stenosis as the etiology of heart-failure symptoms.<br /></br>Documentation bullet: “Severe calcific (likely congenital bicuspid) aortic stenosis causing LV pressure overload heart failure.”  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology in &lt;60 y often bicuspid valve; &gt;65 y degenerative calcification.<br /></br>• Progressive leaflet calcification → fixed orifice (&lt;1 cm²) → ↑LV systolic pressure → concentric hypertrophy → diastolic dysfunction → elevated LA &amp; pulmonary venous pressures → CHF.<br /></br>• ↓Coronary perfusion reserve → exertional angina despite normal coronaries.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>• Definitive: surgical or trans-catheter aortic valve replacement (SAVR/TAVR).<br /></br>• Bridge: loop diuretics for congestion, cautious β-blockade; avoid preload/afterload reduction that can drop preload (e.g., nitrates) in severe AS.<br /></br>• Diagnostics: transthoracic echo (valve area, gradient, LV function). Cardiac cath if coronary assessment needed pre-op.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Aortic Stenosis = SAD”<br /></br>S – Syncope<br /></br>A – Angina<br /></br>D – Dyspnea (CHF)<br /></br>Harsh, systolic, right upper sternal border, radiates to carotids, ↓/absent A2.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Alcohol use disorder – would cause dilated cardiomyopathy with displaced PMI, S3, holosystolic MR-type murmur and no harsh base murmur. Patient drinks only 1–2 beers/day.<br /></br>b. Amyloidosis – restrictive cardiomyopathy; would show thickened ventricular walls, rapid y-descent, often with right-sided failure; murmur not typical.<br /></br>c. Anemia – high-output HF; exam would show bounding pulses, wide PP, and flow murmurs, not harsh AS murmur or decreased S2.<br /></br>e. Atrial fibrillation – can precipitate HF but rhythm would be irregularly irregular; murmur absent.<br /></br>f. Cor pulmonale – isolated right-sided failure; would have elevated JVP, loud P2, leg edema, clear lungs.<br /></br>g. Hemochromatosis – dilated or restrictive cardiomyopathy; bronze skin, diabetes clues absent; murmur pattern wrong.<br /></br>h. Ischemic heart disease – could cause angina/HF but murmur and diminished A2 point specifically to valvular obstruction.<br /></br>i. Mitral regurgitation – holosystolic blowing apex murmur with displaced PMI and S3; S2 usually normal.  </p></br><p>Bottom line: The combination of the “SAD” symptom triad plus a harsh systolic murmur with a soft second heart sound localizes the patient’s heart failure to severe aortic stenosis.</p>
32. An 87-year-old woman with type 2 diabetes mellitus comes to the physician for evaluation of a nonhealing foot ulcer that has increasingly worse tissue necrosis and erythema. She has a 2-year history of mild to moderate dementia, Alzheimer type, with no recent changes in cognitive status. The patient lives alone with the help of various neighbors. Her only family is an out-of-town sister, with whom she last spoke 1 year ago. Physical examination shows a 2-cm ulcer that exposes the head of the first metatarsal. There is 1 cm of necrosis and 2 cm of erythema around the edge of the ulcer. On mental status examination, the patient is alert and oriented to person and place but thinks that the year is 1999. She recalls one of three objects after 5 minutes. A below-the-knee amputation is recommended. The risks and benefits of the operation are discussed with the patient. She understands and repeats the discussion correctly. Attempts to contact her sister are unsuccessful. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Consult with the hospital attorney</br>b. Delay the amputation until her sister can be contacted</br>c. Obtain a psychiatric assessment of this patient's mental capacity</br>d. Schedule a follow-up examination after 1 month of antibiotic therapy</br>e. Proceed with the amputation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Proceed with the amputation  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Understands and repeats the discussion correctly” – shows she can comprehend, reason about, and communicate a choice regarding the operation.<br /></br>• Oriented to person and place (but not time) and can engage meaningfully with the physician → does not automatically equal incapacity.<br /></br>• No available surrogate (sister cannot be reached), so the patient herself is the decision-maker if she has capacity.<br /></br>• Worsening necrosis with exposed bone → limb-threatening infection; delaying care increases morbidity.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: able to state risks/benefits, communicates a consistent choice.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no acute delirium, no psychosis, no evidence she is refusing care.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Decision-making capacity (DMC) is task-specific. A patient must:<br /></br>   a) Understand the relevant information,<br /></br>   b) Appreciate consequences,<br /></br>   c) Reason about treatment options,<br /></br>   d) Communicate a choice.<br /></br>2. Mild dementia ≠ automatic lack of DMC.<br /></br>3. Because she demonstrates the four elements, she can legally give informed consent.<br /></br>4. The surgery is medically indicated and urgent; therefore proceed once consent obtained.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: A cognitively impaired patient who nevertheless demonstrates the four elements of decision-making capacity can consent to (or refuse) treatment; no surrogate or court involvement is required.  </p></br><p>Documentation note: Record the conversation, her demonstrated understanding, her expressed decision, and why she meets DMC.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Chronic diabetic foot ulcer with exposed bone → high suspicion for osteomyelitis.<br /></br>• Necrosis/erythema progressing despite care = limb-threatening infection; definitive therapy often requires amputation when revascularization or debridement is insufficient.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line for exposed bone + necrosis:<br /></br>• Broad-spectrum IV antibiotics for sepsis control, but definitive source control (surgical debridement vs amputation) is necessary.<br /></br>• Below-knee amputation prevents spread, improves healing and prosthetic function.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>CAPACITY mnemonic for the 4 elements:<br /></br>C – Communicate a consistent choice<br /></br>A – Appreciate consequences<br /></br>P – Process (reason) logically<br /></br>A – Acquire/understand information<br /></br>CITY O (orientation) is not on the list; orientation deficits alone don’t rule out capacity.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Consult with the hospital attorney – Only needed if there is disagreement about capacity or no capacity with no surrogate. Here, the patient has capacity.<br /></br>b. Delay the amputation until her sister can be contacted – Unnecessary and potentially harmful; patient herself can consent.<br /></br>c. Obtain a psychiatric assessment of capacity – Not required when capacity has already been adequately assessed by the treating physician.<br /></br>d.
