Schedule follow-up after 1 month of antibiotics – Inappropriate; necrotic ulcer with exposed bone typically requires prompt surgical management, not delayed outpatient follow-up.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Equating dementia or disorientation to automatic incapacity. Always perform a capacity assessment specific to the decision at hand.</p>
33. A 30-year-old man who is in the US Army is brought to the emergency department of a military hospital 40 minutes after being hit by a motor vehicle traveling 25 mph while he was walking. On arrival, he is unconscious. Temperature is 36.0°C (96.8°F), pulse is 113/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 80/55 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 90%. On examination, the abdomen is rigid. He is resuscitated with 8 units of packed red blood cells, 6 units of fresh frozen plasma, and 2 units of platelets. Emergency laparotomy shows transection of the small bowel 5 cm proximal to the cecum. Laboratory studies done 45 minutes after arriving in the surgical intensive care unit show an activated partial thromboplastin time of 42 seconds and a prothrombin time of 25 seconds (INR=3.2). Which of the following is the most likely cause of the coagulation abnormalities in this patient?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Cirrhosis</br>b. Disseminated intravascular coagulation</br>c. Factor VII deficiency</br>d. Hyperfibrinogenemia</br>e. Thrombocytopenia</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Factor VII deficiency  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Massive trauma → hypotension, rigid abdomen → emergent laparotomy for small-bowel transection.<br /></br>• Massive transfusion in &lt; 1 h: 8 U packed RBCs + 6 U FFP + 2 U platelets.<br /></br>• Post-op coagulation profile:<br /></br>  – PT 25 sec, INR 3.2 (markedly prolonged)<br /></br>  – aPTT 42 sec (only mildly ↑)<br /></br>• No mention of active oozing, low platelets, low fibrinogen, or schistocytes.</p></br><p>Key pattern: PT &gt;&gt;&gt; aPTT. The single factor that most strongly affects PT (extrinsic pathway) while sparing aPTT is factor VII, the vitamin-K-dependent factor with the shortest half-life (4–6 h). Massive transfusion without adequate replacement or with dilution quickly produces functional factor VII deficiency.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify pathway affected: Extrinsic pathway (PT) is far more abnormal than intrinsic/common pathway (aPTT).<br /></br>2. Narrow differential: Isolated or predominant PT prolongation = factor VII problem (vitamin-K deficiency, warfarin, massive transfusion).<br /></br>3. Connect to vignette: Patient received large volumes of factor-poor stored blood products in the setting of acute hemorrhage → dilutional loss and consumption of factor VII before the other factors fall.<br /></br>Take-home: In any acutely bleeding/trauma patient, a disproportionate rise in PT soon after resuscitation almost always signals factor VII deficiency.</p></br><p>Documentation (if real patient): “Coagulopathy likely secondary to dilutional factor VII deficiency after massive transfusion; plan to administer additional FFP or PCC (prothrombin complex concentrate) ± vitamin K.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Factor VII initiates the extrinsic coagulation cascade by complexing with tissue factor.<br /></br>• Half-life = 4–6 h (shortest of all clotting factors).<br /></br>• Stored PRBCs lack clotting factors; even FFP given early can be insufficient or arrive after initial dilution.<br /></br>• Result: Early, isolated PT elevation during/after massive transfusion.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Give additional FFP (contains all factors) or 4-factor PCC (II, VII, IX, X) for rapid reversal.<br /></br>2. Administer IV vitamin K (10 mg) if the situation allows; helpful when deficiency is compounded by poor hepatic synthesis.<br /></br>3. Follow serial PT/INR and fibrinogen during massive transfusion (“1:1:1” PRBC:FFP:platelet ratio is current trauma protocol to prevent this).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“First to GO is VII”<br /></br>• Factor VII has the first (shortest) half-life → PT goes up first.<br /></br>Alternatively: “PT is in the <strong>SEVEN</strong> letters of ex<strong>TRINS</strong>ic.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Cirrhosis – Produces both PT and aPTT prolongation days to weeks later, not minutes after trauma; no prior liver history.<br /></br>b. Disseminated intravascular coagulation – Would show thrombocytopenia, low fibrinogen, ↑ d-dimer, schistocytes, and both PT &amp; aPTT markedly prolonged. None provided.<br /></br>d. Hyperfibrinogenemia – An acute-phase response; would not prolong PT or aPTT. Elevated fibrinogen actually promotes clotting.<br /></br>e. Thrombocytopenia – Causes bleeding history and ↑ bleeding time, not PT/aPTT changes. Platelet count not reported abnormal, and PT/aPTT are coagulation-factor measures, not platelet function.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>After massive transfusion, an early, disproportionate rise in PT with minimal change in aPTT is classic for dilutional factor VII deficiency. Prompt FFP or PCC replacement corrects the coagulopathy.</p>
34. A 25-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the office because of a 3-month history of chronic moderate perianal pain and bleeding that began 3 days after she gave birth via cesarean delivery. Before the onset of her symptoms, she had constipation associated with the use of postdelivery oxycodone therapy. The constipation resolved after she discontinued the medication, but her bowel movements have been painful and she has noticed blood on the toilet tissue. One week ago, the patient’s obstetrician prescribed topical hydrocortisone and diltiazem, which provide temporary relief but do not resolve her symptoms. The patient’s medical history otherwise is unremarkable and she takes no other medications. Family history is unremarkable. Vital signs are temperature 37.2°C (99.0°F), pulse 80/min, respirations 20/min, and blood pressure 130/80 mm Hg. Examination of the anus shows a deep wound in the posterior midline with exposure of the internal sphincter muscle and an associated 1.5-cm skin tag. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Biopsy of the wound</br>b. Colonoscopy</br>c. Metronidazole therapy</br>d. Surgical repair</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Surgical repair (lateral internal sphincterotomy)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – “Deep wound in the posterior midline” + “exposure of the internal sphincter muscle” → classic chronic anal fissure.<br /></br>  – “Associated 1.5-cm skin tag” (sentinel pile) → indicates chronicity (&gt;6 weeks).<br /></br>  – Tried topical hydrocortisone &amp; diltiazem (a first-line vasodilator) with only temporary relief → medical therapy has failed.<br /></br>  – Onset after constipation/straining in the immediate postpartum period is the typical precipitating history for fissures.</p></br><p>• Pertinent negatives<br /></br>  – No fever, purulent drainage, fluctuance → abscess unlikely.<br /></br>  – No systemic symptoms, no atypical (lateral) location → malignancy not suggested.<br /></br>  – Age &lt;50 and obvious anorectal source of bleeding → colonoscopy not needed now.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: Posterior midline fissure + sentinel tag + failed topical therapy = surgical management.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify lesion: posterior midline tear with exposed internal sphincter = anal fissure.<br /></br>2. Determine chronicity &amp; severity: sentinel tag, exposed muscle, &gt;6 weeks → chronic, deep.<br /></br>3. Review treatment ladder:<br /></br>   • First-line: fiber, stool softeners, topical anesthetics, vasodilators (nitroglycerin, nifedipine, diltiazem).<br /></br>   • Second-line: botulinum toxin injection.<br /></br>   • Definitive for refractory or deep/chronic fissure: lateral internal sphincterotomy (surgical repair).<br /></br>4. She has already received adequate first-line medical therapy; therefore go to surgery.</p></br><p>Take-home: Chronic posterior anal fissure refractory to topical vasodilators warrants lateral internal sphincterotomy, which heals &gt;90 % of patients.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Trauma from hard stool → tear in anoderm → spasm of internal anal sphincter → ischemia → non-healing ulcer.<br /></br>• Chronic fissure: persists &gt;6 weeks, develops hypertrophied anal papilla proximally and sentinel skin tag distally; may expose internal sphincter fibers.<br /></br>• Postpartum women are at risk due to opioid-induced constipation and straining.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Conservative (≤6 weeks, superficial): fiber, sitz baths, topical lidocaine + vasodilator (0.2–0.4 % nitroglycerin or 2 % diltiazem).<br /></br>• Chronic / refractory:<br /></br>  – Botulinum toxin into internal sphincter (option if patient refuses surgery).<br /></br>  – Lateral internal sphincterotomy: divides a portion of internal sphincter → relieves spasm, improves blood flow. Major adverse event: transient/minor incontinence (&lt;10 %).<br /></br>• No imaging or colonoscopy required unless atypical features (lateral location, induration, HIV, Crohn disease signs).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“FISSure” mnemonic:<br /></br>F – Fibers &amp; fluids first<br /></br>I – Internal sphincter spasm causes pain<br /></br>S – Sentinel tag = chronic<br /></br>S – Sphincterotomy when medical therapy Shot</p></br><p>Visual: Picture a crack at 6 o’clock on a clock-face anus with a little flag (skin tag); if ointment fails, the “crack” gets a surgical “wedge” to loosen it.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Biopsy of the wound<br /></br>   • Indicated for atypical (lateral) fissure, indurated margins, HIV, Crohn, or suspicion of malignancy (anal SCC). Typical posterior fissure with classic appearance doesn’t need biopsy.</p></br><p>b. Colonoscopy<br /></br>   • Rectal bleeding in a young patient with an obvious benign anorectal source and no alarm features does NOT require colonoscopy now. Reserve for age ≥45–50 or unexplained bleeding.</p></br><p>c. Metronidazole therapy<br /></br>   • Used for perianal abscess, fistula-in-ano, or infected thrombosed hemorrhoids. Fissures are not infectious; antibiotics do not promote healing.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Documentation Items (real encounter)<br /></br>• Detailed anorectal exam findings, pain severity, bleeding description<br /></br>• Prior conservative measures tried and duration<br /></br>• Counseling about surgical risks/benefits and alternative (botulinum) options<br /></br>• Informed consent discussion for lateral internal sphincterotomy</p></br><p>Core concept: Chronic, posterior midline anal fissure with failed medical therapy → lateral internal sphincterotomy is the evidence-based definitive treatment.</p>
35. A 42-year-old man is brought to the emergency department by paramedics 1 hour after his girlfriend found him confused and agitated in his home. The girlfriend says that when she spoke with him on the telephone last night, she noted no problems, but she became worried when he did not answer his telephone 3 hours ago. He has a 3-year history of chronic back pain for which he takes oxycodone. He also has major depressive disorder and seasonal allergies treated with citalopram and diphenhydramine, respectively. On arrival, he is confused, screaming loudly, and actively pulling against wrist restraints. His temperature is 38°C (100.4°F), pulse is 163/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 215/105 mm Hg. Physical examination shows diaphoresis and tremulousness. The pupils are dilated. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient’s confusion?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Acetaminophen overdose</br>b. Cannabis withdrawal</br>c. Cocaine intoxication</br>d. Diphenhydramine toxicity</br>e. Opioid withdrawal</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Cocaine intoxication  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden onset: Girlfriend noted no problems the night before → acute event within the last few hours.<br /></br>• Autonomic “revved-up” vital signs: T 38 °C, HR 163/min, BP 215/105 mm Hg.<br /></br>• Neuro-behavioral findings: severe agitation, confusion, screaming, tremor.<br /></br>• Ocular sign: dilated pupils (mydriasis).<br /></br>• Skin: diaphoresis (profuse sweating).<br /></br>Together these create the classic “sympathomimetic toxidrome” (tachycardia, hypertension, hyperthermia, mydriasis, diaphoresis, agitation), which strongly points to recent use of a stimulant such as cocaine or amphetamines. Recent prescription list does not contain any prescribed stimulant, making recreational cocaine the most likely culprit.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify toxidrome: mydriasis + diaphoresis + hyperthermia + severe HTN/tachycardia + agitation ⇒ sympathomimetic.<br /></br>2. Rule out anticholinergic toxicity: would have mydriasis &amp; hyperthermia but <strong>dry</strong> skin/ mucous membranes, not diaphoresis.<br /></br>3. Rule out opioid-related syndromes: opioid intoxication = miosis &amp; respiratory depression; withdrawal = mydriasis but typically normal/slightly ↑ BP/HR, prominent GI upset, yawning—rarely BP &gt; 200/105.<br /></br>4. Acetaminophen or cannabis processes do not create this autonomic surge.<br /></br>Core Takeaway: A markedly elevated HR/BP, diaphoresis, agitation, dilated pupils, and low-grade fever in an otherwise healthy adult = sympathomimetic intoxication, most classically cocaine.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Cocaine blocks presynaptic re-uptake of norepinephrine, dopamine, and serotonin. Excess synaptic catecholamines cause:<br /></br>• α-1 vasoconstriction → severe hypertension, diaphoresis.<br /></br>• β-1 stimulation → tachycardia, arrhythmogenic risk.<br /></br>• CNS dopamine surge → agitation, euphoria → confusion/psychosis at high doses.<br /></br>• Hypermetabolic state → mild hyperthermia, tremor, seizures.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (real-life notes)<br /></br>1. Airway, breathing, circulation.<br /></br>2. Benzodiazepines IV first-line to quell agitation &amp; sympathetic surge.<br /></br>3. Nitroglycerin or phentolamine for severe HTN; avoid β-blocker monotherapy (unopposed α-1).<br /></br>4. Cardiac enzymes &amp; ECG (risk of MI), CMP (rhabdo).<br /></br>Documentation pearls: Time of onset, witnessed use, vitals trend, neuro status, meds given, ECG &amp; lab results.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“HOT, FAST, WET, and WIDE pupils” = Sympathomimetic (Cocaine/Amphetamine).<br /></br>“HOT, FAST, DRY, and WIDE pupils” = Anticholinergic (diphenhydramine, atropine).<br /></br>Swap ‘Wet’ vs ‘Dry’ to separate the two toxidromes.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acetaminophen overdose<br /></br>• Early (first 24 h) may be asymptomatic or only mild GI upset. No agitation, no autonomic storm. Pupil size unaffected, vitals usually normal. Key finding after 24 h = rising AST/ALT.  </p></br><p>b. Cannabis withdrawal<br /></br>• Presents with irritability, insomnia, ↓ appetite, mild diaphoresis, but vitals only mildly elevated; no severe HTN, tremor, or mydriasis.  </p></br><p>d. Diphenhydramine (anticholinergic) toxicity<br /></br>• Mydriasis and hyperthermia, BUT DRY skin &amp; mucous membranes, urinary retention, absent bowel sounds. “Dry as a bone, red as a beet…”—diaphoresis would be absent.  </p></br><p>e. Opioid withdrawal<br /></br>• Mydriasis, diaphoresis, piloerection, GI cramps, yawning, rhinorrhea, but vitals usually moderate (BP/HR mildly elevated). Profound agitation and BP &gt; 200/100 uncommon; fever mild or absent.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing anticholinergic and sympathomimetic toxidromes—remember diaphoresis differentiates them.</p>
36. A 21-year-old woman, gravida 2, para 1, at 36 weeks' gestation is admitted to the hospital in labor. She received no prenatal care. She has no history of operative procedures. She reports that she occasionally used cocaine before she knew she was pregnant but has abstained from illicit drug use during the past 8 months. On admission, the cervix is 5 cm dilated and 100% effaced; the vertex is at 0 station. There is a moderate amount of blood-tinged mucus. The membranes are artificially ruptured, yielding clear fluid. Her blood group is A, Rh-negative; a serum anti-D antibody titer is positive. During the next 30 minutes, fetal heart monitoring shows regular, sinusoidal wave forms. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this fetal heart rate pattern?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Fetal heart defect</br>c. Maternal cocaine use</br>d. Placenta previa</br>e. Rh isoimmunization</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Rh isoimmunization  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Rh-negative mother with a positive anti-D (anti-Rh-D) antibody titer → she is already allo-immunized.<br /></br>• 36-week fetus suddenly shows a sinusoidal fetal heart rate (FHR) tracing—smooth, regular, “sine-wave” oscillations of 5–15 beats/min around the baseline at a frequency of 2–5 cycles/min.<br /></br>• Sinusoidal pattern = classic red flag for severe fetal anemia.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Ask, “What produces severe, acute or chronic fetal anemia in this scenario?”<br /></br>Step 2: Maternal IgG anti-D crosses the placenta → immune-mediated hemolysis of Rh-positive fetal RBCs → progressive anemia.<br /></br>Step 3: Severe anemia → decreased oxygen-carrying capacity, fetal hypoxia, and a sinusoidal FHR (Category III tracing requiring immediate evaluation).<br /></br>Core takeaway: A true sinusoidal FHR pattern almost always means “fetal bleeding or hemolysis,” and in a sensitized Rh-negative mother the leading etiology is Rh isoimmunization.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Rh-negative mother exposed to Rh-positive fetal blood (prior pregnancy, miscarriage, transfusion) forms anti-D IgG. In subsequent Rh-positive pregnancies:<br /></br>1. IgG anti-D crosses placenta.<br /></br>2. Opsonizes fetal RBCs → hemolysis.<br /></br>3. Results: anemia, high-output cardiac failure, hydrops fetalis, elevated bilirubin, and sinusoidal FHR.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Expedite delivery if viable and tracing persists (Category III).<br /></br>• Neonatal resuscitation team ready for possible exchange transfusion.<br /></br>Postnatal/Long-term:<br /></br>• Umbilical cord hemoglobin, bilirubin, and Direct Coombs test.<br /></br>• In future pregnancies: routine anti-D immune globulin prophylaxis at 28 weeks and within 72 h postpartum (or after any sensitizing event).<br /></br>• For known sensitization: serial MCA Doppler (peak systolic velocity) to monitor anemia; intrauterine transfusion if indicated.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“SiNuSoID = Severe aNemIa from <em>Rh</em> (ID = isoimmunization D-antigen).”<br /></br>OR visualize a sine wave painted red—red for lysed RBCs.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Abruptio placentae<br /></br>• Painful vaginal bleeding, tender hypertonic uterus, non-reassuring FHR (late decels, tachy/brady), not a regular sinusoidal pattern.  </p></br><p>b. Fetal heart defect<br /></br>• May cause arrhythmias or abnormal baseline, not a smooth sinusoid; nothing in vignette suggests structural heart disease.  </p></br><p>c. Maternal cocaine use<br /></br>• Cocaine → vasoconstriction → placental abruption or fetal tachycardia, but she quit 8 months ago; cocaine does not create sinusoidal FHR.  </p></br><p>d. Placenta previa<br /></br>• Painless bleeding without uterine tenderness; FHR is usually normal unless massive hemorrhage; not associated with sinusoidal tracing.  </p></br><p>Key Pitfall to Avoid<br /></br>Do not confuse a sinusoidal pattern with variable or late decelerations; a true sinusoid is smooth, repetitive, and specifically signals fetal anemia/hemorrhage.  </p></br><p>Bottom Line<br /></br>Sinusoidal FHR in an Rh-sensitized pregnancy = Rh isoimmunization–induced severe fetal anemia.</p>
37. A 22-year-old college student comes to the physician because of a 3-year history of falling asleep during her classes; she worries that she will not be able to continue with her education because of this. She says that she always has had difficulty staying awake during class, even in high school. She also occasionally has trouble staying awake during movies. She says she has no problems getting to sleep or waking up. She goes to bed between midnight and 1 AM and gets up at 8 AM. She has tried sleeping for longer durations, even up to 8½ hours, but she still has been unable to stay awake during the day. She has the greatest difficulty staying awake during her classes after lunch. She thinks that she sleeps very soundly and moves little during sleep because her bedsheets are neat when she awakens in the morning. She does not remember having dreams. She reports that no one has ever told her that she snores. She is 170 cm (5 ft 5 in) tall and weighs 62 kg (136 lb); BMI is 21 kg/m2. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she has a concerned mood and a reactive affect. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Minnesota Multiphasic Personality Inventory</br>b. Overnight pulse oximetry</br>c. Pulmonary function tests</br>d. MRI of the brain</br>e. Multiple sleep latency tests</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Multiple sleep-latency tests (MSLT)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. Key phrases:<br /></br>   • “3-year history of falling asleep during her classes” → chronic excessive daytime sleepiness (EDS).<br /></br>   • “No problems getting to sleep or waking up… sleeps 7–8½ h/night” → adequate total sleep time → rules out sleep deprivation.<br /></br>   • “Sheets are neat… no one has told her she snores… BMI 21 kg/m²” → argues against obstructive sleep apnea.<br /></br>   • Onset in adolescence, episodes triggered by monotonous, post-prandial situations (after lunch, movies) → classic for narcolepsy or idiopathic hypersomnia.<br /></br>   • No psychiatric, neurologic, or pulmonary red flags on exam.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no cataplexy, hypnagogic/hypnopompic hallucinations, or sleep paralysis are mentioned, but their absence does not exclude narcolepsy type 2 (without cataplexy) or idiopathic hypersomnia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Confirm patient truly has pathologic EDS.<br /></br>2. Exclude common secondary causes (sleep deprivation, medications, depression, OSA, periodic limb movements, circadian disorders). History &amp; BMI make these less likely.<br /></br>3. When EDS is unexplained, the definitive diagnostic study is MSLT performed the morning after an overnight in-lab polysomnogram to ensure adequate sleep and to rule out occult apnea.<br /></br>4. MSLT measures mean sleep-latency across five scheduled naps and documents any sleep-onset REM periods (SOREMPs).<br /></br>   • Mean latency ≤8 min + ≥2 SOREMPs = narcolepsy.<br /></br>   • Latency ≤8 min + &lt;2 SOREMPs = idiopathic hypersomnia.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Unexplained chronic daytime sleepiness in a young, otherwise healthy patient → obtain formal sleep-latency testing.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record total sleep time on polysomnogram, MSLT nap latencies, number of SOREMPs, and rule-outs (respiratory events index, limb movements).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (High-yield)<br /></br>Narcolepsy: loss of hypothalamic orexin (hypocretin)-producing neurons → impaired regulation of REM sleep and wakefulness → sleep attacks, cataplexy, SOREMPs. Type 2 lacks cataplexy.<br /></br>Idiopathic hypersomnia: unknown etiology; excessive sleep drive without SOREMPs.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>Diagnostic sequence:<br /></br>1. Overnight polysomnography (PSG) → rule out OSA, periodic limb movements.<br /></br>2. Next morning MSLT → quantify sleepiness/SOREMPs.<br /></br>(Choice list did not offer PSG; MSLT is still the “most appropriate next step” given options.)</p></br><p>First-line treatment once diagnosis confirmed:<br /></br>• Narcolepsy – daytime stimulants (modafinil, armodafinil; second-line methylphenidate/amphetamines).<br /></br>• Idiopathic hypersomnia – modafinil or solriamfetol.<br /></br>• Cataplexy present → add REM-suppressing agents (sodium oxybate, venlafaxine).<br /></br>Behavioral: strict sleep schedule, scheduled daytime naps, avoid sedatives/alcohol.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“MSLT Makes Sleepiness Legally Tested”: when you can’t explain daytime sleepiness, do an MSLT.<br /></br>Narcolepsy diagnostic triad mnemonic “S.O.R.E. REM”: Sleep-Onset REM Episodes (≥2) + latency ≤8.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Minnesota Multiphasic Personality Inventory (MMPI)<br /></br>   • Screens for personality &amp; psychiatric disorders, not physiologic sleep disorders. She lacks mood or behavioral symptoms suggesting somatization or malingering.</p></br><p>b. Overnight pulse oximetry<br /></br>   • Cheap screen for nocturnal desaturation in suspected OSA/COPD. Normal BMI, no snoring, neat sheets, and young age make OSA unlikely; pulse-ox cannot diagnose narcolepsy/idiopathic hypersomnia.</p></br><p>c. Pulmonary function tests<br /></br>   • Evaluate asthma/COPD. Patient has no respiratory complaints; PFTs do not assess sleepiness.</p></br><p>d. MRI of the brain<br /></br>   • Used for focal neurologic deficits, tumors, demyelination. Her neuro exam is normal; diffuse daytime sleepiness without focal signs points to a global sleep-wake disorder, not structural disease.</p></br><p>e. Multiple sleep-latency tests<br /></br>   • Correct: objective, gold-standard test for narcolepsy/idiopathic hypersomnia when unexplained EDS persists.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Jumping straight to stimulants without objective testing—always confirm diagnosis with PSG + MSLT first.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Chronic, unexplained daytime sleepiness in a young, otherwise healthy patient → obtain an MSLT (following an overnight PSG) to confirm narcolepsy or idiopathic hypersomnia and guide therapy.</p>
38. A 38-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 6-month history of progressive fatigue and muscle weakness. During this time, she has not been able to concentrate at work and has noticed that her voice has become hoarse. During the past 3 months, she has had a 5.4-kg (12-lb) weight gain despite limiting her food intake. She has bipolar disorder, type 2 diabetes mellitus, and hypercholesterolemia. Her medications are metformin, lisinopril, lithium, and pravastatin. She does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. Her temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 50/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 130/90 mm Hg. Examination shows cold skin and coarse hair over the scalp. There is a firm, nontender, symmetrically enlarged thyroid gland. Deep tendon reflexes are 1+. Her serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration is 25 μU/mL. Results of serum electrolyte concentrations and renal function tests are within the reference ranges.</br>Antithyroid peroxidase antibody testing is negative. Which of the following is the most likely cause of these findings?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Autoimmune thyroiditis</br>b. Granulomatous infiltration</br>c. Iodine deficiency</br>d. Medication adverse effect</br>e. Pituitary dysfunction</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Medication adverse effect ‒ lithium-induced hypothyroidism/goiter  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• 6-month progressive fatigue, weight gain, hoarse voice, coarse hair, bradycardia → classic hypothyroid symptoms/signs.<br /></br>• TSH 25 µU/mL (very high) → primary, not central, hypothyroidism.<br /></br>• Firm, nontender, symmetrically enlarged thyroid (goiter) → suggests impaired hormone production with TSH-driven gland enlargement.<br /></br>• Antithyroid peroxidase (TPO) Ab negative → argues against Hashimoto (autoimmune) as the usual cause.<br /></br>• Current drug list includes lithium for bipolar disorder → well-known to cause painless goiter and overt hypothyroidism.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no neck pain (rules out subacute painful thyroiditis), normal serum electrolytes/creatinine (rules out adrenal or renal confounders).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Identify primary hypothyroidism → elevated TSH.  </li></br><li>Look for common etiologies: autoimmune, iatrogenic (meds, surgery, radio-iodine), infiltrative, iodine related.  </li></br><li>TPO Ab negative + clear exposure to a culprit drug (lithium) → most parsimonious answer is medication adverse effect.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Always scan the medication list in hypothyroid patients; lithium and amiodarone are high-yield offenders.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Patient with long-term lithium therapy presenting with clinical and biochemical primary hypothyroidism. Plan: initiate levothyroxine; continue lithium with endocrinology follow-up.”  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Lithium is actively concentrated by thyroid follicular cells. It:<br /></br>• Inhibits iodotyrosine coupling and thyroid hormone release.<br /></br>• Directly interferes with T4 → T3 conversion.<br /></br>• Results in decreased circulating T4/T3 → pituitary up-regulates TSH → thyroid hypertrophy (goiter).<br /></br>• 5–10 % of chronic lithium users develop overt hypothyroidism; subclinical disease is even more common.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><ol></br><li>Confirm diagnosis: repeat TSH/free T4; no need for imaging unless nodularity.  </li></br><li>Start levothyroxine; adjust every 6–8 weeks.  </li></br><li>Do NOT abruptly stop lithium if mood control is satisfactory; coordinate with psychiatry.  </li></br><li>Monitor TSH q-6–12 months in all patients on lithium.  </li></br></ol></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Quick Memory Aid  </p></br><p>“LiTHIUM”:<br /></br>Li → Lithium<br /></br>T → Thyroid dysfunction (hypo)<br /></br>HI → Hormone Inhibition<br /></br>U → Up (TSH goes ↑)<br /></br>M → (painless) goiter Mass  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Autoimmune thyroiditis (Hashimoto)<br /></br>• Usually positive TPO and/or thyroglobulin antibodies (95 %).<br /></br>• Can be antibody-negative but lithium exposure is a stronger, test-worthy etiology here.</p></br><p>b. Granulomatous infiltration (subacute De Quervain)<br /></br>• Painful, tender thyroid, often post-viral, with transient hyperthyroid phase; ESR elevated. Patient’s gland is painless and she is chronically hypothyroid.</p></br><p>c. Iodine deficiency<br /></br>• Extremely rare in the developed world; presents after years of low iodine intake, not in a single individual with normal diet.</p></br><p>e. Pituitary dysfunction (central hypothyroidism)<br /></br>• Would show low or inappropriately normal TSH with low free T4; here TSH is markedly elevated (primary).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Takeaway  </p></br><p>In any patient on lithium who develops a painless goiter and elevated TSH, think lithium-induced primary hypothyroidism and treat with levothyroxine while continuing to monitor mood-stabilizing therapy.</p>
39. A 27-year-old man comes to the emergency department because of a progressive rash and wheezing. Yesterday, the patient received the diagnosis of cellulitis of his right leg, and cephalexin therapy was begun. He has a history of mild, intermittent asthma and eczema. His only other medication is inhaled albuterol as needed. He had a mild allergic reaction to ampicillin as a child. He is in moderate distress. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 118/min, respirations are 22/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Examination shows a 3 x 3-cm, erythematous, warm, tender rash on the right leg that is unchanged from yesterday. There are new, raised, 1 x 2-cm pruritic lesions on the upper and lower extremities and trunk. Diffuse, scattered inspiratory and expiratory wheezes are heard bilaterally. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Anaphylactic reaction</br>b. Rocky Mountain spotted fever</br>c. Staphylococcal scalded skin syndrome</br>d. Toxic shock syndrome</br>e. Urticarial vasculitis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Anaphylactic reaction  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Started cephalexin yesterday” → new β-lactam exposure, classic trigger for IgE–mediated reactions.<br /></br>• “Progressive rash … raised, pruritic lesions” → transient wheals/urticaria.<br /></br>• “Wheezing” → acute bronchospasm from histamine/leukotriene release.<br /></br>• “BP 90/60 mm Hg, pulse 118/min” → distributive shock (peripheral vasodilation &amp; capillary leak).<br /></br>• Fever absent, cellulitis unchanged → original infection not worsened.<br /></br>These four elements (trigger, urticaria, respiratory compromise, hypotension) fulfill the clinical criteria for anaphylaxis.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify an immediate-type allergic trigger (new antibiotic).<br /></br>2. Look for 2+ organ systems involved after exposure:<br /></br>   • Skin/mucosa: urticaria, pruritus<br /></br>   • Respiratory: wheeze, dyspnea<br /></br>   • Cardiovascular: hypotension/tachycardia<br /></br>3. Meets definition of anaphylaxis → choose “Anaphylactic reaction.”<br /></br>Take-home: Any acute combo of urticaria + airway symptoms or hypotension after a medication/food/insect sting = anaphylaxis until proven otherwise; give intramuscular epinephrine immediately and document time &amp; response.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Type I hypersensitivity. Drug behaves as hapten → pre-formed drug-specific IgE on mast cells/basophils.<br /></br>• Cross-linking → rapid release of histamine, tryptase, prostaglandins, leukotrienes → vasodilation, ↑vascular permeability, bronchoconstriction, pruritus.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line:<br /></br>1. IM epinephrine (0.3–0.5 mg 1:1000) into lateral thigh ± repeat q5-15 min.<br /></br>2. Airway/oxygen, large-bore IV, rapid isotonic fluids (because of third-spacing).<br /></br>Adjuncts: H1 blocker (diphenhydramine), H2 blocker (famotidine), glucocorticoid (methylprednisolone) to prevent biphasic reaction, β-agonist (albuterol) for bronchospasm.<br /></br>Labs aren’t required to diagnose, but serum tryptase (peaks at 1–2 h) may confirm.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ABCDE of Anaphylaxis”<br /></br>A – Allergen exposure<br /></br>B – Breathing issues (wheeze, stridor)<br /></br>C – Circulatory collapse (hypotension)<br /></br>D – Derm (hives, flushing)<br /></br>E – Epinephrine first!  </p></br><p>Visualize mast cells “exploding confetti” of histamine throughout body → red wheals, dilated vessels, tight lungs.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Rocky Mountain spotted fever<br /></br>• Tick exposure, high fever, petechial (non-pruritic) rash starting wrists/ankles → trunk, thrombocytopenia. No wheezing or immediate antibiotic trigger.  </p></br><p>c. Staphylococcal scalded skin syndrome<br /></br>• Exfoliative toxin causes superficial flaccid bullae, tender skin, +Nikolsky sign, classically in neonates/young children. Not urticarial, no bronchospasm.  </p></br><p>d. Toxic shock syndrome<br /></br>• High fever, diffuse sunburn-like erythema, desquamation, multi-organ failure from TSST-1. Rash is macular, not raised/pruritic; wheezing uncommon. Requires staph/strep focus (tampon, wound).  </p></br><p>e. Urticarial vasculitis<br /></br>• Fixed (&gt;24 h) urticarial lesions that leave purpura/hyperpigmentation and are often painful rather than pruritic; may have low complement, but systemic anaphylactic features absent.  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway<br /></br>Acute onset of urticaria plus either airway compromise or hypotension after new drug exposure = anaphylaxis → treat immediately with intramuscular epinephrine.</p>
40. A 37-year-old man comes to the physician because he has had two bowel movements containing bright red blood during the past 6 hours. He has a 3-day history of intermittent severe abdominal cramps and diarrhea. He has had five loose stools daily during this period. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. His last visit to a physician was 5 years ago. His most recent travel experience was a trip to Canada 6 months ago. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 98/min, and blood pressure is 126/78 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows diffuse severe tenderness with no peritoneal signs. Bowel sounds are increased. Rectal examination shows no abnormalities. There is no stool in the rectal vault. His hemoglobin concentration is 13 g/dL, leukocyte count is 12,300/mm3, and platelet count is 302,000/mm3. Which of the following is the most likely causal organism?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Clostridium difficile</br>b. Clostridium perfringens</br>c. Cryptosporidium parvum</br>d. Enterotoxigenic Escherichia coli</br>e. Escherichia coli O157:H7</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Escherichia coli O157:H7  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key findings that point to hemorrhagic E coli:<br /></br>• Acute onset of severe crampy abdominal pain + diarrhea for 3 days<br /></br>• Progression to visibly bloody (bright-red) stools in the last 6 h<br /></br>• Afebrile (37 °C) or very low-grade fever → invasive but non-inflammatory pattern typical for Shiga-like toxin–mediated colitis<br /></br>• Mild leukocytosis but otherwise stable vitals (no sepsis)<br /></br>• No recent antibiotic, PPI, hospitalization, or IBD history → makes C. difficile and IBD flare unlikely<br /></br>• Travel history non-contributory (Canada 6 mo ago) – rules out “travelers’ diarrhea” bugs like ETEC<br /></br>• Age 37, immunocompetent → protozoal causes (Cryptosporidium) less likely  </p></br><p>Pattern: afebrile bloody diarrhea + severe abdominal pain with minimal systemic toxicity → classic for Shiga toxin–producing E coli (STEC), especially serotype O157:H7.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Bloody vs. watery diarrhea → bloody points toward invasive/hemorrhagic organisms (STEC, Shigella, Campylobacter, Salmonella, Entamoeba).  </li></br><li>Fever/systemic signs? Almost none → favors STEC (Shiga-like toxin damages colonic mucosa but is non-invasive).  </li></br><li>Medication history → absence of antibiotics rules out C. difficile.  </li></br><li>Epidemiology → common foodborne outbreak bug in the US/Canada (undercooked beef, unpasteurized juice), fits healthy adult.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Afebrile patient with severe abdominal cramps and new-onset grossly bloody diarrhea after short incubation should trigger suspicion for STEC O157:H7.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: note stool appearance, abdominal exam findings, hemodynamic status, recent food exposures; order stool culture with sorbitol-MacConkey or EIA for Shiga toxin.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• STEC produces Shiga-like toxin (Stx1/Stx2) → inhibits 60S ribosomal subunit → mucosal necrosis → hemorrhagic colitis.<br /></br>• Toxin can enter bloodstream and damage renal endothelium → hemolytic-uremic syndrome (HUS: MAHA, thrombocytopenia, AKI), especially in children.  </p></br><p>Risk factors/Exposures: undercooked ground beef, raw leafy greens, unpasteurized dairy/juice, petting zoos.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests  </p></br><p>Diagnostics<br /></br>1. Stool culture on sorbitol-MacConkey agar (O157:H7 does NOT ferment sorbitol).<br /></br>2. Shiga toxin EIA or PCR.<br /></br>3. Baseline CBC, creatinine, LDH, haptoglobin to screen for evolving HUS.  </p></br><p>Management<br /></br>• Supportive: IVF, electrolytes, monitor urine output and CBC/Cr daily.<br /></br>• NO antibiotics and NO antimotility agents – both increase HUS risk by raising toxin levels.<br /></br>• Early nephrology consult if rising creatinine or hemolysis.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“OH STEC! My stool is red.”<br /></br>O = O157:H7<br /></br>H = Hemorrhagic colitis<br /></br>S = Shiga-like toxin, Supportive care only<br /></br>T = Thrombocytopenia (HUS risk)<br /></br>E = Endothelial injury → AKI<br /></br>C = Cattle/undercooked beef exposure  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Clostridium difficile<br /></br>• Typically watery diarrhea after recent antibiotics, PPI use, hospitalization; may see leukocytosis &amp; abdominal pain but frank hematochezia uncommon.  </p></br><p>b. Clostridium perfringens<br /></br>• Reheated meat → watery diarrhea, no blood; incubation 8–16 h; abdominal cramps but resolves within 24 h.  </p></br><p>c. Cryptosporidium parvum<br /></br>• Chronic, profuse watery diarrhea in AIDS or children at water parks; not normally bloody.  </p></br><p>d. Enterotoxigenic E. coli (ETEC)<br /></br>• “Traveler’s diarrhea” (watery) after recent travel to developing countries; no blood, low-grade fever at most.  </p></br><p>Each incorrect option produces watery, not bloody, stool or requires risk factors absent in the vignette.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line: In a healthy adult with acute severe crampy abdominal pain and afebrile bloody diarrhea, think Shiga toxin–producing E. coli O157:H7 and avoid antibiotics to prevent HUS.</p>
41. A 50-year-old man with a 30-year history of paranoid schizophrenia is admitted to a psychiatric facility because of an exacerbation of his condition. His history includes multiple admissions to the hospital for his condition. The physician prescribes oral antipsychotic therapy and the patient agrees to take the medication while in the facility, but he says that he does not need it and he will stop taking the medication as soon as he is discharged. He states, “I don’t trust any of you. You’re all out to get me. But I’ll play along until my time here is done.” Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Administer the antipsychotic therapy until the patient’s paranoia completely resolves</br>b. Change the oral antipsychotic therapy to a long-acting injectable formulation</br>c. Commit the patient to the facility against his will</br>d. Discharge the patient with support from the local community mental health center, telling him that he can always return to the facility if he needs help in the future</br>e. Maximize the dose of the antipsychotic therapy until the patient gains insight into his illness</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Change the oral antipsychotic therapy to a long-acting injectable formulation (LAI)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 30-year history of paranoid schizophrenia → chronic, relapsing illness that typically requires life-long pharmacotherapy.<br /></br>  – Multiple prior hospitalizations → past evidence of decompensation when off meds.<br /></br>  – “I don’t trust any of you… I’ll stop taking the medication as soon as I’m discharged” → explicit declaration of future non-adherence.<br /></br>  – Currently willing to “play along” as an inpatient → short-term adherence only.<br /></br>These points scream “adherence problem,” directing you to the depot/LAI option.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Is the current episode adequately treated in the hospital? Oral meds will work acutely, but…<br /></br>2. What happens after discharge? The patient states he will stop therapy.<br /></br>3. What intervention best guarantees continued pharmacologic coverage outside the hospital while respecting autonomy? Long-acting injectable antipsychotic (q2-12 wk dosing depending on agent).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In schizophrenia, any clear risk of non-adherence = consider depot antipsychotic.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (why adherence matters)<br /></br>• Dopamine D2 receptor blockade must be continuous; intermittent dosing → “dopamine supersensitivity,” rebound psychosis, rehospitalization.<br /></br>• Schizophrenia patients often have poor insight (anosognosia) → 40-60 % stop oral meds within a year.</p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line for chronic adherence problems:<br /></br>• Typical LAIs: haloperidol decanoate, fluphenazine decanoate.<br /></br>• Atypical LAIs: risperidone microsphere, paliperidone palmitate, aripiprazole monohydrate, olanzapine pamoate.<br /></br>• Monitoring: continue oral overlap for 1–3 wk with some LAIs; check EPS, metabolic panel as usual.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Depot for Doubt”: If the patient doubts the need for meds → Depot shot.<br /></br>Or think “I LAI when they’ll lie (about taking pills).”</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Continue oral until paranoia resolves<br /></br>   – Treats acute episode but does nothing for long-term adherence once discharged; relapse very likely.<br /></br>c. Commit the patient against his will<br /></br>   – Involuntary commitment requires danger to self/others or grave disability. Simply refusing meds after discharge ≠ legal criteria.<br /></br>d. Discharge with CMHC support alone<br /></br>   – Community resources are helpful but don’t solve the adherence issue; high risk of rapid relapse.<br /></br>e. Maximize the dose until he gains insight<br /></br>   – Insight is often minimal despite high doses; higher doses ↑ side-effect burden and still rely on voluntary pill-taking.</p></br><p>Key Documentation Points (real-world)<br /></br>• Lack of insight and stated intent to discontinue meds.<br /></br>• Rationale for LAI to ensure continuity of care and reduce rehospitalization risk.<br /></br>• Informed consent for LAI, discussion of risks/benefits.</p></br><p>Bottom Line: When a schizophrenia patient openly plans to stop oral medication, switch to a long-acting injectable antipsychotic to safeguard against non-adherence and relapse.</p>
42. An 8-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department because of a 2-day history of urinary urgency and frequency and right-sided abdominal cramps. The cramps are intermittent, and he cannot get comfortable in any position when they occur. He has not had fever, diarrhea, or constipation, but he has vomited twice. He has no history of similar episodes or serious illness. He takes no medications. His paternal uncle has a history of renal calculi, and his cousin has Crohn disease. The patient has no dietary restrictions. His temperature is 37.5°C (99.5°F). The right flank is tender to palpation. An abdominal x-ray shows a 3-mm ovoid, opaque mass in the right pelvis. Laboratory studies show:</br>Serum</br>Urea nitrogen           20 mg/dL</br>Creatinine              0.1 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>pH                      6.5</br>RBC                     30–50/hpf</br>WBC                     3–5/hpf</br>In addition to administration of morphine, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Oral administration of allopurinol</br>b. Oral administration of fluids</br>c. Oral administration of hydrochlorothiazide</br>d. Oral administration of potassium citrate</br>e. Oral administration of pyridoxine</br>f. Lithotripsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Oral administration of fluids  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Intermittent right-sided abdominal cramps … cannot get comfortable” + right flank tenderness = classic renal colic.<br /></br>• Abdominal x-ray: “3-mm ovoid, opaque mass” → a small, radio-opaque calculus (most calcium-containing stones).<br /></br>• UA: gross micro-hematuria (30–50 RBC/HPF) with normal pH (6.5) and no pyuria → consistent with uncomplicated stone, not infection.<br /></br>• Size matters: stones ≤5 mm have ≥80–90 % chance of passing spontaneously.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-by-step approach to an uncomplicated stone:<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis (done by history, exam, and imaging).<br /></br>2. Control pain (morphine, NSAIDs).<br /></br>3. Facilitate passage: aggressive oral hydration (and, in adults, an α-blocker such as tamsulosin).<br /></br>4. Admit, intervene (lithotripsy, ureteroscopy) only if stone &gt;5–7 mm, obstructing, infected, or failing to progress.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: For a hemodynamically stable patient with a 3-mm radio-opaque ureteral stone, conservative therapy with analgesia + vigorous PO fluids is first-line.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Calcium oxalate &gt; calcium phosphate comprise ~80 % of pediatric stones.<br /></br>• Radio-opaque (contrast: uric acid, cystine, xanthine stones are radiolucent).<br /></br>• Most stones ≤5 mm traverse the ureter within days. Hydration increases urine flow, dilutes lithogenic factors, and mechanically propels the calculus.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics Review<br /></br>Acute management algorithm (no infection, normal renal function):<br /></br>1. Analgesia: NSAID (ketorolac) or opioid.<br /></br>2. Hydration: encourage ≥2 L/day intake; IV if vomiting/dehydrated.<br /></br>3. Observation with serial imaging/ultrasound for progression.<br /></br>4. Add medical expulsive therapy (α-blocker, CCB) if stone in distal ureter (evidence mostly adult).<br /></br>Definitive interventions (lithotripsy, ureteroscopy) reserved for stones &gt;5–7 mm, intractable pain, obstruction with rising creatinine, solitary kidney, or urosepsis.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Five and fly” → Stones under 5 mm usually “fly” out with Fluids + pain control.<br /></br>“Larger than seven, you’re zappin’ ” → ≥7 mm often require shock-wave “zapping” (lithotripsy).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Allopurinol – Used for hyperuricosuric or uric-acid stones, which are radiolucent; not helpful for a radiopaque calcium stone.<br /></br>c. Hydrochlorothiazide – Decreases urinary calcium, helpful for chronic prevention in hypercalciuria, not acute passage.<br /></br>d. Potassium citrate – Alkalinizes urine to dissolve uric-acid or cystine stones; also a chronic preventive in hypocitraturia but not first-line acute therapy here.<br /></br>e. Pyridoxine – Lowers endogenous oxalate in primary hyperoxaluria (rare, autosomal recessive). Not indicated acutely and does nothing for stone passage.<br /></br>f. Lithotripsy – Effective ≥5–7 mm or if medical therapy fails. A 3-mm stone in a stable child does not meet criteria.  </p></br><p>Key Documentation Points for a Real Encounter<br /></br>• Pain score and response to analgesia.<br /></br>• Hydration status and intake/output plan.<br /></br>• Stone size/location, serum creatinine, UA results.<br /></br>• Discharge instructions: fluid goal, strainer for stone, return precautions (fever, worsening pain, anuria).</p>
43. A previously healthy 57-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 6-month history of muscle weakness and fatigue. The weakness began in the muscles of her face and has progressed to all of the muscles in her body. Examination shows bilateral ptosis and weakness of the left abducens muscle. An x-ray of the chest shows a mediastinal mass. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis of this patient's neurologic findings?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Nerve conduction studies with repetitive stimulation</br>b. MRI of the cervical spine</br>c. Somatosensory evoked potentials</br>d. Biopsy of a peripheral nerve</br>e. Biopsy of a skeletal muscle</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Nerve conduction studies with repetitive stimulation  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 6-month, slowly progressive “fatigable” weakness that started in the ocular muscles (bilateral ptosis, abducens palsy) and later involves limb and bulbar musculature → classic pattern for myasthenia gravis (MG).<br /></br>• “Previously healthy” middle-aged woman → peak MG incidence in women &lt;60 y.<br /></br>• Chest x-ray: anterior mediastinal mass → thymic hyperplasia or thymoma, seen in ≈15 % of MG patients.<br /></br>Pertinent positives: ocular/bulbar weakness, fatigability, thymic mass.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no autonomic symptoms, no proximal leg &gt; arm weakness that improves with exercise (Lambert-Eaton), no sensory loss, no pain.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: fatigable ocular ± generalized weakness + thymic mass = myasthenia gravis.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Identify NMJ disorder → MG most likely.<br /></br>Step 2: Confirm the diagnosis with the most specific physiologic test: repetitive nerve stimulation or single-fiber EMG.<br /></br>• In MG, a decrement (&gt;10 %) in compound muscle action potential amplitude with repetitive stimulation is diagnostic.<br /></br>• Serologic tests for anti-ACh-receptor or anti-MuSK antibodies are also used, but NBME frequently highlights the electrodiagnostic study as the “next step” after clinical suspicion.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: For suspected MG, confirm with repetitive nerve stimulation; look for thymic pathology once diagnosis is confirmed.</p></br><p>Documentation: “Plan to obtain repetitive nerve stimulation study to evaluate for a decremental response consistent with myasthenia gravis.”</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Autoantibodies against nicotinic ACh receptors (or MuSK) at the neuromuscular junction.<br /></br>• Complement-mediated destruction → ↓ number of functional receptors → failure of neuromuscular transmission that worsens with repeated use (ACh depletion).<br /></br>• Thymic abnormalities (hyperplasia, thymoma) act as the trigger in many cases.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests (high-yield overview)<br /></br>1. Diagnostic tests<br /></br>   – Repetitive nerve stimulation or single-fiber EMG (most sensitive).<br /></br>   – Serum anti-AChR antibody titer (specific).<br /></br>   – Chest CT/MRI to characterize thymic lesion.<br /></br>2. Acute therapy: pyridostigmine ± IVIG or plasmapheresis for crisis.<br /></br>3. Chronic immunosuppression: corticosteroids, azathioprine, mycophenolate.<br /></br>4. Definitive in thymoma or thymic hyperplasia: thymectomy (also improves symptoms in many patients without visible thymoma).</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “MyasTHENIA gets tired, linked to THYMus – think decrement on repeTiTive stimulation.”<br /></br>• Visualize a see-saw: first contraction strong, each successive contraction drops lower (decrement).  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong<br /></br>b. MRI of the cervical spine – evaluates spinal cord compression, demyelinating plaques, or structural lesions causing myelopathy. MG is a peripheral NMJ disorder; exam shows pure motor cranial nerve findings without sensory changes or myelopathic signs.  </p></br><p>c. Somatosensory evoked potentials – test dorsal column/sensory pathways; abnormal in MS or spinal cord disease, not in MG (purely motor junction pathology).  </p></br><p>d. Biopsy of a peripheral nerve – used for vasculitic neuropathy, amyloidosis, CIDP. MG has a normal peripheral nerve.  </p></br><p>e. Biopsy of a skeletal muscle – helpful in myopathies (polymyositis, muscular dystrophies). In MG the muscle is structurally normal; pathology lies at NMJ, so biopsy is unnecessary.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing MG with Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome (LEMS). LEMS shows proximal leg &gt; arm weakness that IMPROVES with exercise and is associated with small-cell lung carcinoma; nerve stimulation reveals an incremental response. This patient’s ocular weakness, fatigability, and thymoma point to MG.</p>
44. A 29-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, comes to the physician 1 week after noticing a mildly tender lump in her left breast. Five months ago, she delivered a healthy newborn at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and spontaneous vaginal delivery. She is breast-feeding and says her infant feeds equally from both breasts. The patient has no history of serious illness, and her only medication is a prenatal vitamin. Her mother was diagnosed with breast cancer at the age of 61 years. The patient’s vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no axillary or supraclavicular lymphadenopathy. There is a 3-cm, smooth, mobile, cystic, tender mass in the upper outer quadrant of the left breast. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Bromocriptine therapy</br>b. Dicloxacillin therapy</br>c. Discontinuing breast-feeding</br>d. Fine-needle aspiration of the mass</br>e. Increasing breast-feeding on the left breast</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Fine-needle aspiration of the mass  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 5 months postpartum &amp; actively breast-feeding ⇒ lactational breast changes common<br /></br>  – 1-week history of a 3-cm smooth, well-circumscribed, mobile, cystic, mildly tender mass; no erythema, warmth, fever, or lymphadenopathy<br /></br>  – Feeds equally from both breasts (no preferential stasis on the affected side)  </p></br><p>• Pertinent positives: cystic consistency, tenderness, location near milk-producing lobules (upper outer quadrant), postpartum lactation.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no systemic illness, no overlying skin changes, no axillary nodes, no hard/irregular borders, no fever.<br /></br>→ Pattern strongly fits a galactocele (milk-retention cyst) or simple benign cyst rather than infectious mastitis or malignancy.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: This is not an infectious process (afebrile, no erythema) → antibiotics unnecessary.<br /></br>Step 2: Mass is cystic and could be a galactocele; definitive and therapeutic step = needle aspiration (yields milky fluid, mass collapses).<br /></br>Step 3: Imaging (ultrasound) is often obtained first, but on examinations the high-yield management for a palpable, simple, cystic lesion in a lactating woman is aspiration, which simultaneously diagnoses and treats.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Palpable, benign-appearing breast cyst during lactation → fine-needle aspiration.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Galactocele<br /></br>• Etiology: Obstruction of a lactiferous duct → collection of milk under pressure.<br /></br>• Clinical: Well-demarcated, soft/fluctuant mass; may enlarge after feeding and shrink with expression. Mild tenderness but no inflammatory signs.<br /></br>• Natural history: Benign; often resolves after aspiration or after cessation of breast-feeding.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• First-line: Ultrasound-guided (or bedside) fine-needle aspiration.<br /></br>  – Diagnostic: confirms cystic nature &amp; contents (milky fluid).<br /></br>  – Therapeutic: decompression relieves pain and prevents secondary infection.<br /></br>• Continue breast-feeding after aspiration; no need for lactation suppression or breast-feeding cessation.<br /></br>• Antibiotics only if superinfection develops (mastitis/abscess).<br /></br>• Imaging-guided core biopsy is reserved if cyst recurs or has suspicious features.</p></br><p>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“Milk in a pocket? Stick a needle in the socket.”<br /></br>Pocket = galactocele; Needle = FNA.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bromocriptine therapy (dopamine agonist suppresses prolactin)<br /></br>   – Used to stop lactation in special situations (e.g., postpartum hemorrhage requiring breast-feeding cessation). Over-treatment for a single benign cyst; unnecessarily deprives infant of breast milk.  </p></br><p>b. Dicloxacillin therapy<br /></br>   – Indicated for acute bacterial mastitis (fever, erythema, indurated warm wedge-shaped area). Patient lacks infectious signs.  </p></br><p>c. Discontinuing breast-feeding<br /></br>   – Can worsen engorgement and increase risk of mastitis/galactocele formation; not recommended unless medical contraindication to nursing exists.  </p></br><p>e. Increasing breast-feeding on the left breast<br /></br>   – Helpful for a simple clogged duct (small, firm, nontender focal fullness that resolves after feeding). A 3-cm fluctuant cyst usually persists despite nursing and requires drainage for definitive relief and diagnosis.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing galactocele with infectious mastitis—remember fever and erythema guide you to antibiotics; a soft, mobile cyst without systemic signs points to aspiration.</p>
45. An 18-year-old man is brought to the physician 1 hour after he felt light-headed while playing basketball. He has not had chest pain, palpitations, or shortness of breath. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. His father died in a motor vehicle collision at the age of 35 years. There is no family history of serious illness. The patient's pulse is 80/min, and blood pressure is 115/85 mm Hg. There is no jugular venous distention. Pulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. A diagram of cardiac auscultation is shown. The murmur does not change with inspiration, position, or Valsalva maneuver. An ECG shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Aortic valve repair</br>b. Implantation of a cardiac defibrillator</br>c. Recommendation not to play competitive sports</br>d. Warfarin therapy</br>e. No management is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. No management is indicated at this time  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues<br /></br>• Healthy 18-year-old male with a single brief episode of light-headedness while playing basketball (near-syncope, not true syncope)<br /></br>• Crescendo–decrescendo systolic murmur that<br /></br>  – Is unchanged by inspiration (rules out right-sided murmurs)<br /></br>  – Is unchanged by Valsalva or positional changes (rules out dynamic left-ventricular outflow obstruction such as hypertrophic cardiomyopathy and mitral valve prolapse)<br /></br>• Normal vital signs, normal ECG, completely normal cardiopulmonary exam otherwise<br /></br>• Family history essentially unremarkable for heritable cardiac disease (father’s death in an MVA at 35 could be trauma‐related; no sudden-cardiac-death history)  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition<br /></br>A soft, early- to midsystolic murmur that does not vary with maneuvers in an asymptomatic teenager is most consistent with a benign “flow” (physiologic) murmur. Innocent flow murmurs are extremely common in adolescents and young adults engaged in athletics because high cardiac output produces turbulence across a structurally normal aortic or pulmonic valve.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives that steer away from pathologic murmurs<br /></br>• No radiation to carotids, no systolic thrill, no paradoxically split S2 → argues against significant aortic stenosis.<br /></br>• No increase with Valsalva/standing → argues against hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy (HOCM).<br /></br>• Murmur is systolic (not diastolic) and &lt; grade 3/6 (implied because thrill not mentioned).<br /></br>• ECG normal; in HOCM we would expect high-voltage QRS or repolarization abnormalities.<br /></br>• No exertional chest pain, dyspnea, or true syncope.  </p></br><p>Because the murmur is benign and the patient has had only one brief, likely vasovagal episode, no further work-up or treatment is needed.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Determine if the murmur is innocent or pathologic.<br /></br>   – Innocent murmurs are early-/mid-systolic, grade ≤2, with normal S2 and no symptoms.<br /></br>   – Murmurs that change with Valsalva suggest dynamic obstruction (HOCM, MVP).  </li></br><li>Search for red flags: syncope, exertional chest pain, dyspnea, FH of sudden death, abnormal ECG. None are truly present here.  </li></br><li>Since all findings favor a benign murmur and the near-syncope has an obvious situational trigger (basketball → possible dehydration/vasovagal), reassurance alone is appropriate.  </li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Most systolic murmurs in young, otherwise healthy patients are innocent. Absence of maneuveral change, absence of symptoms, and normal ECG further lower the likelihood of structural heart disease, so no treatment or sports restriction is necessary.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (Innocent/Flow Murmurs)  </p></br><p>• Turbulent flow across a normal semilunar valve during periods of high stroke volume (exercise, fever, pregnancy).<br /></br>• Typically early- to midsystolic, best heard at left sternal border or right upper sternal border, low amplitude, and disappear or soften with decreased cardiac output (rest).<br /></br>• No anatomic abnormality, so prognosis is excellent.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics / Treatment  </p></br><p>Diagnostics: None required once classic innocent features and normal ECG are documented.<br /></br>Treatment: Reassurance; no activity restriction.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aids  </p></br><p>“Innocent until proven guilty”:<br /></br>If the murmur is<br /></br>I – Intensity ≤ II/VI<br /></br>N – Normal S2, Normal exam &amp; ECG<br /></br>N – No symptoms (no syncope, SOB, chest pain)<br /></br>O – Only systolic, One location, Oriented early-mid<br /></br>C – Changes little with maneuvers<br /></br>E – Every other test normal<br /></br>N → it’s INNOCENT → No management.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Aortic valve repair<br /></br>   • Indicated for symptomatic or severe aortic stenosis (syncope, angina, heart failure, or echo showing valve area ≤1.0 cm²). Murmur from AS typically radiates to carotids and may have a delayed pulse (“pulsus parvus et tardus”). None of that is present.<br /></br>b. Implantation of a cardiac defibrillator<br /></br>   • Used for conditions with high sudden-death risk (HOCM with prior cardiac arrest, long-QT with recurrent arrhythmia). Normal ECG and benign murmur make this unnecessary.<br /></br>c. Recommendation not to play competitive sports<br /></br>   • Appropriate for HOCM, Marfan with aortic dilation, severe AS, or arrhythmogenic syndromes. This patient lacks evidence of any of these.<br /></br>d. Warfarin therapy<br /></br>   • Used for thromboembolic prevention in atrial fibrillation, mechanical valves, or certain hypercoagulable states—none of which apply here.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Documentation Note (real-world)  </p></br><p>“Early-midsystolic grade 2/6 murmur at RUSB, no radiation, no change with Valsalva; normal S2, no clicks. ECG normal sinus rhythm. Single vasovagal episode during intense exercise—likely benign. Educated patient regarding benign flow murmur; may continue all activities. Return if recurrent syncope, chest pain, dyspnea, or palpitations.”</p>
46. A 37-year-old woman is brought to the emergency department 30 minutes after an episode of syncope at a shopping mall. She says she had shortness of breath and was sweating before she passed out. She has a childhood history of asthma. She currently has panic disorder and gastroesophageal reflux disease. Current medications include sertraline, omeprazole, and an oral contraceptive. On arrival, she is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. Her temperature is 37.4°C (99.3°F), pulse is 110/min, and blood pressure is 90/60 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 90%. Examination, including neurologic examination, shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 12 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 36%</br>Leukocyte count 9000/mm³</br>Arterial blood gas analysis on room air:</br>pH 7.46</br>Pco₂ 30 mm Hg</br>Po₂ 62 mm Hg</br>Which of the following is the most likely explanation for these findings?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Asthma</br>b. Congestive heart failure</br>c. Panic episode</br>d. Pneumonia</br>e. Pulmonary embolism</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Pulmonary embolism  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden‐onset dyspnea, diaphoresis, and then syncope → classic “massive PE” presentation due to abrupt obstruction of pulmonary circulation with reflex vagal response and acute drop in cardiac output.<br /></br>• Oral contraceptive use → estrogen ↑ hepatic production of clotting factors → strong acquired risk factor for venous thromboembolism.<br /></br>• Vital signs: tachycardia (110/min) + hypotension (90/60 mm Hg) suggest hemodynamic compromise from impaired RV outflow.<br /></br>• Pulse-ox 90% &amp; ABG: pH 7.46 (alkalemia), PCO₂ 30 mm Hg (low), PaO₂ 62 mm Hg (hypoxemia) → respiratory alkalosis with hypoxemia, the classic ABG pattern in acute PE due to V/Q mismatch + reflex hyperventilation.<br /></br>• Normal lung &amp; neuro exam → no focal consolidation, wheezing, or CHF stigmata, supporting an intravascular—not parenchymal—process.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify emergency triad: abrupt dyspnea + syncope + hypotension.<br /></br>2. Check risk factors: estrogen-containing OCP = major.<br /></br>3. Interpret ABG: hypoxemia + low CO₂ = PE until proven otherwise.<br /></br>4. Exclude mimics (see Incorrect Answer section).<br /></br>Core takeaway: In any patient with sudden cardiopulmonary collapse, respiratory alkalosis, hypoxemia, and a thrombotic risk factor, think pulmonary embolism.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: note hemodynamic instability (BP 90/60) → “massive PE” category; immediate empiric anticoagulation or thrombolysis after CT-PA or bedside echo is warranted.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thrombus (usually from deep veins of legs or pelvis) lodges in pulmonary arterial tree.<br /></br>• Ventilated but under-perfused alveoli → high V/Q → hypoxemia.<br /></br>• Hypoxemia &amp; anxiety stimulate hyperventilation → ↓PaCO₂ → respiratory alkalosis.<br /></br>• Large clot ↑ pulmonary vascular resistance → acute RV strain → ↓LV preload → syncope &amp; hypotension.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield)<br /></br>First step (hemodynamically stable): CT pulmonary angiography or V/Q scan if renal insufficiency; start IV heparin immediately if suspicion high.<br /></br>Hemodynamically unstable (this patient): bedside transthoracic echo → signs of RV strain; give systemic thrombolysis (e.g., alteplase) if no contraindication.<br /></br>Long-term: transition to oral anticoagulation (apixaban, rivaroxaban, or warfarin).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PE causes PaO₂ to fall and PaCO₂ to crawl (down).”<br /></br>Risk factors = “SHEEP” (Surgery, Hormones–OCP/HRT, Extended travel, Earlier DVT/PE history, Pregnancy/ postpartum).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Asthma<br /></br>• Would show wheezing, prolonged expiratory phase, often normal or ↑PaCO₂ in severe attack; syncope &amp; hypotension rare. No exogenous trigger here.<br /></br>b. Congestive heart failure<br /></br>• Expect crackles, S3, elevated JVP, possible respiratory acidosis due to hypoventilation; ABG often shows ↓PaO₂ with normal/↑PaCO₂. Sudden syncope less typical in new CHF.<br /></br>c. Panic episode<br /></br>• Can cause respiratory alkalosis but usually normal PaO₂; hypoxemia (PaO₂ 62) and hypotension are NOT consistent with a simple panic attack.<br /></br>d. Pneumonia<br /></br>• Would show fever, focal crackles or bronchial breath sounds, leukocytosis; ABG can show hypoxemia, but respiratory alkalosis plus abrupt syncope &amp; hypotension is atypical. Onset generally subacute, not instantaneous.  </p></br><p>Key distinguishing feature: Only PE fits the triad of risk factor (OCP), sudden cardiopulmonary collapse, and classic ABG (low PaO₂ + low PaCO₂).</p>
47. A 62-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-month history of generalized fatigue and mild low back pain. She has not had any other symptoms. She has no history of serious illnesses and takes no medications. Her last health maintenance examination was 10 months ago, and a complete blood count at that time was within the reference range. Examination today shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hematocrit 30%</br>Mean corpuscular volume 103 µm3</br>Leukocyte count 8200/mm3</br>Segmented neutrophils 67%</br>Bands 4%</br>Lymphocytes 29%</br>Platelet count 165,000/mm3</br>Serum</br>Ca2+ 10.9 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 1.8 mg/dL</br>A blood smear is shown. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Liver function tests</br>b. Measurement of serum vitamin B12 (cobalamin) concentration</br>c. Serum iron studies</br>d. Colonoscopy</br>e. Bone marrow biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Bone marrow biopsy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – Age ≥60 years (typical for plasma-cell dyscrasias)<br /></br>  – “Mild low back pain” → think lytic vertebral lesions<br /></br>  – Normocytic-slightly macrocytic anemia (Hct 30%, MCV 103 μm³)<br /></br>  – Hypercalcemia (10.9 mg/dL) and ↑ creatinine (1.8 mg/dL)<br /></br>  – Peripheral smear picture (not shown here) in MM classically demonstrates rouleaux formation (stacked RBCs).<br /></br>• Pertinent positives = CRAB criteria for multiple myeloma (Calcium elevation, Renal dysfunction, Anemia, Bone pain).<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no GI bleeding, no neurologic signs of B12 deficiency, no chronic liver disease stigmata.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. CRAB findings + rouleaux → strong suspicion for multiple myeloma.<br /></br>2. The definitive (confirmatory) step in MM is demonstration of clonal plasma-cell proliferation in bone marrow (≥10% plasma cells or biopsy-proven plasmacytoma).<br /></br>3. While serum/urine protein electrophoresis (SPEP/UPEP) is the best initial test, the question specifically asks for the step that “confirms” the diagnosis; that is the bone marrow biopsy.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• Monoclonal proliferation of plasma cells secreting IgG or IgA ± free light chains (κ, λ).<br /></br>• Excess light chains (Bence-Jones proteins) → renal tubular injury (↑ creatinine).<br /></br>• Osteoclast activation by cytokines (IL-1, TNF-α) → lytic bone lesions, bone pain, hypercalcemia.<br /></br>• Marrow replacement → normocytic/macrocytic anemia, mild thrombocytopenia.<br /></br>• Rouleaux: elevated serum protein reduces zeta potential, causing RBC stacking; automated cell counters misinterpret this as macrocytosis (slightly ↑ MCV).  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests Snapshot<br /></br>Initial work-up: SPEP, UPEP, serum free light chains, skeletal survey.<br /></br>Confirmatory: bone marrow aspirate/biopsy.<br /></br>Therapy (overview):<br /></br>• Transplant-eligible: high-dose melphalan + autologous HSCT.<br /></br>• Non-eligible: lenalidomide + dexamethasone ± bortezomib.<br /></br>• Supportive: bisphosphonates, hydration, erythropoiesis-stimulating agents, infection prophylaxis.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CRAB eats marrow”<br /></br>C – Calcium ↑<br /></br>R – Renal failure<br /></br>A – Anemia<br /></br>B – Bone pain/lytic lesions<br /></br>Marrow biopsy = crab’s final pinch (confirms diagnosis).  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Liver function tests – Elevations might cause macrocytosis but would not explain bone pain, renal failure, or hypercalcemia. No stigmata of liver disease given.<br /></br>b. Serum vitamin B12 – B12 deficiency gives macrocytic anemia with hypersegmented neutrophils, neurologic deficits, normal calcium/creatinine; would not cause back pain or rouleaux.<br /></br>c. Serum iron studies – Evaluate microcytic or anemia of chronic disease; iron deficiency causes low MCV, not ↑; does not fit the CRAB picture.<br /></br>d. Colonoscopy – Appropriate if occult GI bleeding suspected (iron-deficiency anemia), but anemia here is macrocytic and accompanied by hypercalcemia + renal dysfunction.<br /></br>e. Bone marrow biopsy – Correct. Provides direct evidence of ≥10% clonal plasma cells, confirming multiple myeloma.  </p></br><p>Take-Home Message<br /></br>When an older patient presents with unexplained anemia plus any combination of bone pain, hypercalcemia, or renal impairment, immediately think “multiple myeloma” and confirm with a bone marrow biopsy.</p>
