48. A 37-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. He feels well. He has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. He received the hepatitis B vaccine at the age of 28 years. He does not smoke or drink alcohol. During the past 6 months, he has been sexually active with one male partner, and they do not use condoms. The patient works as an accountant and has not traveled outside the USA. Examination shows no abnormalities. A blood sample is obtained for serum lipid studies and an HIV antibody test. In addition to counseling this patient about risk factors for sexually transmitted diseases, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management.	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. PPD skin test</br>b. Haemophilus influenzae type b vaccine</br>c. Hepatitis A vaccine</br>d. Meningococcal vaccine</br>e. Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine, 23-valent</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Hepatitis A vaccine  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key demographic/risk clue: “sexually active with one male partner, and they do not use condoms.” The patient is a man who has sex with men (MSM).<br /></br>• No other medical problems, U.S.–born, no travel, fully immunized for hepatitis B at age 28.<br /></br>• Goal of question: identify guideline-based, routine preventive care in an asymptomatic MSM patient.</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: MSM behavior (increases risk for several infections, notably Hep A).<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: No foreign travel (so travel vaccines not driving the decision), no chronic disease that would trigger pneumococcal vaccination, no HIV diagnosis yet (test pending), no residence or work exposure to TB.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: MSM ➜ vaccinate for hepatitis A (and ensure hepatitis B immunity).  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Ask: “Which vaccines or screening tests are specifically recommended for MSM?”<br /></br>   • Vaccines: Hepatitis A, Hepatitis B (already done), HPV (≤45 y; he is 37 so should also be offered if not received), and meningococcal only if additional indications exist.<br /></br>2. Evaluate each answer choice against CDC/ACIP adult guidelines.<br /></br>3. Hepatitis A is the only listed intervention that is routinely recommended for all MSM regardless of other risk factors.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: All adults with male-to-male sexual contact who lack documented immunity should receive the 2-dose hepatitis A vaccine series.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: In practice you would record counseling and consent for Hep A immunization, lot number, and schedule follow-up for the second dose in 6 months.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Hepatitis A virus spreads via fecal–oral route; MSM have higher exposure through certain sexual practices.<br /></br>• Unlike hepatitis B/C, HAV causes only an acute, self-limited hepatitis, but morbidity can be significant in adults; vaccination confers long-lasting immunity.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>Hepatitis A vaccine schedule: 2 doses (0 and 6–12 months) of either Havrix or Vaqta IM.<br /></br>No routine serologic testing is needed before vaccination unless the patient reports prior immunization or confirmed past infection.</p></br><p>Other MSM-specific health maintenance to remember (not listed in choices but high-yield):<br /></br>• Annual HIV, syphilis, gonorrhea, chlamydia screening.<br /></br>• HPV 9-valent vaccine through age 45 (3-dose series).<br /></br>• Consider meningococcal MenACWY if HIV-positive or other indications.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Hep A for A-ll MSM”: The letter “A” in MSM reminds you of hepatitis A.<br /></br>Visual: Imagine a rainbow (pride flag) superimposed on a liver—think “Protect this liver with Hep A vaccine.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. PPD (tuberculin) skin test<br /></br>   • Indicated for high-risk populations: recent immigrants, healthcare workers, prisoners, homeless, close contacts of active TB. None apply here. MSM status alone is not a TB risk.  </p></br><p>b. Haemophilus influenzae type b (Hib) vaccine<br /></br>   • Recommended only for patients with functional/anatomic asplenia or hematopoietic stem-cell transplant. Healthy adults do not need it.  </p></br><p>d. Meningococcal vaccine<br /></br>   • Indications: age 11-12 with booster, college dorm/military recruits, travelers to endemic areas, asplenia/complement deficiency, laboratory exposure, or HIV infection. This patient lacks these indications (HIV status unknown).  </p></br><p>e. Pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine (PPSV23)<br /></br>   • Given at age ≥65 or earlier for chronic heart/lung/liver disease, diabetes, alcoholism, cigarette smoking, CSF leak, cochlear implant, or immunocompromise. A healthy 37-year-old without those conditions does not qualify.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: assuming meningococcal vaccine is routinely recommended for all MSM—in reality, it is tied to HIV or other classic indications, whereas hepatitis A is universally advised for MSM.</p>
49. A 27-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 1-month history of sudden episodes of a "scary choking feeling." He has had three episodes during this period; the episodes are accompanied by nausea, sweating, and "heart pounding" sensations. The symptoms peak within 5 to 10 minutes and disappear about 15 minutes later. The first two episodes "came out of the blue" while he was at work; the last episode occurred when he was stuck in traffic. He is fearful that he will have another episode and that "someone will notice and think I'm going crazy." He has no history of serious medical illness and takes no medications. He is a veteran of the U.S. Marine Corps and experienced some intense fighting 5 years ago. He has occasional nightmares involving combat and intermittent sadness when he thinks about military comrades who were killed. He has occasional difficulty falling asleep. He has had no changes in energy level or appetite. He is 175 cm (5 ft 9 in) tall and weighs 82 kg (180 lb); BMI is 27 kg/m2. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he is anxious but relates well with the physician. His serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration is within the reference range. A 12-lead ECG shows a normal sinus rhythm. Which of the following is the most appropriate pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Bupropion</br>b. Buspirone</br>c. Haloperidol</br>d. Lithium carbonate</br>e. Paroxetine</br>f. Risperdal</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Paroxetine (selective serotonin re-uptake inhibitor, SSRI)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues that point to panic disorder:<br /></br>• “Sudden episodes of a scary choking feeling,” palpitations, sweating, nausea → classic autonomic surge of a panic attack.<br /></br>• Rapid onset, peaking within 10 min, resolving within ~15 min → meets DSM-5 temporal criteria for a panic attack.<br /></br>• “Came out of the blue” (unexpected) + persistent fear of additional attacks (“fearful that he will have another episode”) and worry about being judged (“someone will notice and think I’m going crazy”) → maladaptive change in behavior, fulfilling the ≥1-month “anticipatory anxiety” criterion for panic disorder.<br /></br>• Normal physical exam, normal TSH, normal ECG → organic causes ruled out.<br /></br>• PTSD symptoms (nightmares, combat memories) are present but sub-threshold; the acute, spontaneous, physiologic attacks with fear of recurrence dominate the clinical picture → primary diagnosis = panic disorder.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify recurrent, unexpected panic attacks.<br /></br>2. Confirm at least 1 month of worry/behavior change.<br /></br>3. Exclude medical/medication causes (hyper-thyroid, arrhythmia, stimulant use) → labs and ECG normal.<br /></br>4. First-line pharmacologic treatment for panic disorder = SSRI or SNRI; paroxetine, sertraline, fluoxetine, venlafaxine are high-yield options.<br /></br>5. Therefore choose paroxetine.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: For panic disorder (frequent Step2/ shelf topic), SSRIs are the evidence-based first-line long-term therapy; cognitive-behavioral therapy (exposure-based) is the first-line psychotherapeutic option and is often combined with an SSRI. Benzodiazepines may be used short-term for acute relief but are not first-line chronic therapy.</p></br><p>Documentation essentials (real life): frequency/description of attacks, duration, associated symptoms, functional impairment, substance/medical rule-outs, treatment plan (SSRI start, CBT referral, safety/ suicidality screen).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Panic attacks = sudden “false alarm” bursts of sympathetic activity likely arising from dysregulation of the locus ceruleus and amygdala.<br /></br>• Chronic fear of attacks leads to behavioral avoidance and cortical hypervigilance.<br /></br>• SSRIs enhance serotonergic tone, dampening overactive limbic circuitry, reducing frequency and severity of attacks within 2–6 weeks.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line maintenance:<br /></br>• SSRIs: paroxetine, sertraline, fluoxetine (start low, titrate up to minimize activation anxiety).<br /></br>• SNRIs: venlafaxine XR.<br /></br>Adjuncts:<br /></br>• Cognitive-behavioral therapy with interoceptive exposure.<br /></br>Acute rescue (while waiting for SSRI effect):<br /></br>• Short-acting benzodiazepines (eg, lorazepam) if no substance-use risk.<br /></br>Monitoring: watch for initial activation, suicidality in young adults, and SSRI side effects (sexual dysfunction, GI upset, weight change).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PANIC” for first-line meds:<br /></br>P – Paroxetine<br /></br>A – Another SSRI (sertraline/fluoxetine)<br /></br>N – SNRI (venlafaxine)<br /></br>I – Interoceptive CBT<br /></br>C – Clonazepam/Lorazepam (rescue)</p></br><p>Picture a parachute (Paroxetine) gently lowering a patient from a peak of panic.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Bupropion – Norepinephrine/Dopamine re-uptake inhibitor; can INCREASE anxiety and precipitate panic; contraindicated.<br /></br>b. Buspirone – 5-HT1A partial agonist helpful for generalized anxiety disorder (worry for ≥6 mo), but not effective for acute panic attacks.<br /></br>c. Haloperidol – Typical antipsychotic for schizophrenia/acute agitation; no role in panic disorder, high EPS risk.<br /></br>d. Lithium carbonate – Mood stabilizer for bipolar disorder; does not treat panic disorder.<br /></br>f. Risperidone – Atypical antipsychotic; used in psychotic and bipolar disorders, not first-line for panic; metabolic and prolactin side effects.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: choosing buspirone because “anxiety” is mentioned; exams test recognition that panic disorder ≠ generalized anxiety disorder and requires an SSRI/SNRI.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Recurrent unexpected panic attacks with ≥1 month of worry = panic disorder. First-line chronic therapy = an SSRI such as paroxetine.</p>
50. A previously healthy 25-year-old man is brought to the emergency department 2 hours after being involved in a motor vehicle collision. He was the restrained driver. On arrival, he is alert and oriented to person, place, and time. He says he has severe lower abdominal pain. His temperature is 37.5°C (99.5°F), pulse is 105/min, respirations are 18/min, and blood pressure is 120/70 mm Hg. Abdominal examination shows ecchymoses over the lower quadrants and an abrasion where the seat belt was fastened. There is suprapubic tenderness. Palpation of the iliac crests produces pain. There is blood at the urethral meatus. His hematocrit is 30%. X-rays of the pelvis show a fracture of the superior and inferior right pubic rami and the right sacroiliac joint. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Cystography</br>b. Retrograde urethrography</br>c. Placement of a suprapubic catheter</br>d. Intravenous pyelography</br>e. Bladder irrigation</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Retrograde urethrography  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Mechanism: High-energy motor-vehicle crash with lap-belt sign → pelvic ring disruption (fractured pubic rami + sacro-iliac joint).<br /></br>• Key clues:<br /></br>  – Blood at the urethral meatus<br /></br>  – Suprapubic pain/tenderness<br /></br>  – Pelvic fracture on imaging<br /></br>These three findings together strongly suggest a posterior urethral injury (classically the membranous urethra) and mandate evaluation of urethral integrity before any trans-urethral instrumentation.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: he is hemodynamically stable (BP 120/70), so emergent ex-lap or pelvic packing is not the immediate priority.  </p></br><p>Pattern to recognize:<br /></br>Pelvic fracture + blood at meatus ± inability to void/high-riding prostate → ALWAYS think urethral injury → next step = retrograde urethrogram (RUG).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1  Suspect urethral injury (blood at meatus).<br /></br>Step 2  Do NOT pass a Foley or contrast into the bladder until the urethra is proven intact.<br /></br>Step 3  Order a RUG.<br /></br>If RUG shows no extravasation → safe to pass Foley and then consider cystogram for bladder injury.<br /></br>If RUG shows extravasation → place suprapubic catheter and consult urology for primary endoscopic realignment vs delayed urethroplasty.<br /></br>Core takeaway: “Catheter last, urethrogram first” whenever urethral injury is suspected.  </p></br><p>What would be documented: presence of blood at meatus, pelvic fracture pattern, plan for RUG prior to catheterization.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• Posterior urethral injuries occur where the membranous urethra is sheared off the prostat ic apex by sudden anterior-posterior pelvic ring opening.<br /></br>• Associated injuries: bladder neck/ bladder rupture, vascular pelvic bleeding.<br /></br>• Classic signs: blood at meatus, inability to void, high-riding prostate, perineal/scrotal hematoma.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics &amp; Management (exam-level detail)<br /></br>First-line test: Retrograde urethrogram using water-soluble contrast.<br /></br>Bladder study (retrograde cystography or CT cystogram) is done only after urethra cleared or via suprapubic route if urethra disrupted.<br /></br>Definitive management: suprapubic diversion + delayed urethroplasty or early endoscopic alignment depending on injury grade.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “BUB” algorithm: Blood at meatus → Urethrogram → then Bladder study.<br /></br>• Picture a stop sign at the meatus: “Stop! Do a RUG before you plug.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Cystography – Appropriate to evaluate bladder rupture, but you cannot instill contrast trans-urethrally until you know the urethra is intact. Comes after a normal RUG or via suprapubic catheter if the urethra is torn.  </p></br><p>c. Placement of a suprapubic catheter – Correct only if urethral disruption already confirmed. It is premature before imaging.  </p></br><p>d. Intravenous pyelography – Old test for upper-tract injury; provides no information about urethra/bladder integrity after pelvic fracture.  </p></br><p>e. Bladder irrigation – Used for clot retention with hematuria; not diagnostic and contraindicated before urethral evaluation.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Take-Home Message<br /></br>In any trauma patient with pelvic fracture plus blood at the urethral meatus, the very next step is a retrograde urethrogram to rule out urethral injury before any catheter or bladder study is attempted.</p>
51. A 47-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 3-week history of moderate pain in her arms and legs. She states that her "bones ache" in all joints both at rest and with movement. She has hypertension, hyperlipidemia, and type 2 diabetes mellitus well controlled with diet. Current medications include hydrochlorothiazide and simvastatin. Her sister has Sjögren syndrome, and her mother has coronary artery disease. The patient does not smoke and drinks one glass of wine daily. Her blood pressure is 144/80 mm Hg. Cardiopulmonary examination shows no abnormalities. There is tenderness to palpation over the wrist, metacarpophalangeal, knee, and ankle joints. Clubbing of the fingers and toes is noted. A chest x-ray shows a left upper lobe mass with perihilar lymphadenopathy. X-rays of the tibia and fibula show periostosis. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Bone metastases</br>b. Hypertrophic pulmonary osteoarthropathy</br>c. Neuropathic arthropathy (Charcot joint)</br>d. Sarcoidosis</br>e. Systemic sclerosis (scleroderma)</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Hypertrophic pulmonary osteoarthropathy (HOA)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>1. “3-week history of moderate pain in her arms and legs … ‘bones ache’” → migratory, periarticular pain.<br /></br>2. Clubbing of the fingers and toes → abnormal proliferation of connective tissue at the nail bed; think intrathoracic malignancy, cyanotic heart disease, or HOA.<br /></br>3. Long-bone x-ray: “periostosis of the tibia and fibula” → classic radiographic hallmark of HOA (laminated or “onion-skin” new bone along shafts).<br /></br>4. Chest x-ray: “left upper-lobe mass with perihilar lymphadenopathy” → strongly suggests a primary lung carcinoma (classically adenocarcinoma) that can produce HOA as a paraneoplastic syndrome.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: no focal lytic/blastic lesions, no neuropathy, no skin thickening, no hilar granulomas—these steer away from the distractors.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>• Identify clubbing + long-bone periostitis → think HOA.<br /></br>• Search for an intrathoracic driver → lung mass present.<br /></br>• Therefore the painful “arthropathy” is not primary joint disease but a paraneoplastic syndrome secondary to the pulmonary tumor.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Painful joints + digital clubbing + periosteal new bone formation = hypertrophic pulmonary osteoarthropathy, most often due to lung cancer.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Note paraneoplastic HOA, need expedited oncologic evaluation of lung mass.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Trigger: Vascular/endothelial growth factors (e.g., PDGF, VEGF) released by an intrathoracic malignancy → increased periosteal vascularity and new bone formation.<br /></br>• Manifestations:<br /></br>  – Digital clubbing<br /></br>  – Symmetric arthralgias (often wrists, ankles, knees)<br /></br>  – Periostitis of long bones (tibia, fibula, radius, ulna)<br /></br>• Occurs with: non–small cell lung cancer (MC), cyanotic congenital heart disease, inflammatory bowel disease, liver cirrhosis.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>1. Treat the underlying cause (resection or chemo/radiation for the lung cancer) → HOA symptoms usually regress.<br /></br>2. Symptomatic relief: NSAIDs, bisphosphonates, or octreotide if severe pain.<br /></br>3. Imaging: Plain films show periosteal reaction; bone scan reveals increased uptake along cortices.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Mnemonic / Memory Aid<br /></br>“CLUB” for HOA:<br /></br>C – Cancer in lung<br /></br>L – Long-bone periostitis<br /></br>U – Uncomfortable joints<br /></br>B – Bulbous fingertips (clubbing)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Bone metastases<br /></br>• Would show focal lytic, blastic, or mixed lesions, not diffuse periosteal “onion-skin” layering.<br /></br>• Clubbing is not typical.<br /></br>• Pain often localized, worse at night, may cause pathologic fractures.</p></br><p>c. Neuropathic arthropathy (Charcot joint)<br /></br>• Destructive, painless joint deformity due to loss of proprioception (e.g., diabetic neuropathy, syphilis).<br /></br>• Radiographs show disorganized joint with “bag of bones,” not periosteal new bone of shafts.<br /></br>• No clubbing.</p></br><p>d. Sarcoidosis<br /></br>• Could cause arthralgias and hilar LAD, but periosteal bone changes and clubbing are uncommon; would expect lung reticulonodular infiltrates, erythema nodosum, uveitis, ↑ACE.<br /></br>• Chest x-ray usually bilateral hilar LAD without discrete mass.</p></br><p>e. Systemic sclerosis (scleroderma)<br /></br>• Presents with skin thickening, Raynaud phenomenon, sclerodactyly, telangiectasias.<br /></br>• Musculoskeletal pain stems from myopathy or tendon friction rubs, not periostitis.<br /></br>• Clubbing absent; lung disease is usually interstitial fibrosis or pulmonary HTN, not a solitary mass.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>When you see digital clubbing plus diffuse long-bone periostitis causing arthralgias, immediately think hypertrophic pulmonary osteoarthropathy and search for an underlying lung malignancy.</p>
52. A healthy 16-year-old girl comes to the physician for a school physical examination. She is sexually active with one partner and does not use contraception. She has smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 2 years. She is 163 cm (5 ft 4 in) tall and weighs 70 kg (156 lb); BMI is 27 kg/m2. Physical examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following behavioral modifications is most likely to have the greatest impact on mortality in this patient during the next 10 years?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Condom use</br>b. Monthly breast self-examination</br>c. Seat belt use</br>d. Smoking cessation</br>e. Weight loss</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Condom use  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 16-year-old, sexually active, no contraception → high risk for sexually transmitted infections (STIs) and unintended pregnancy.<br /></br>• Time horizon explicitly limited to the next 10 years (age 16 → 26). We must ask: “What is most likely to prevent death in that specific interval?”<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: unprotected sex, adolescent age.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no chronic illness, no crashes or trauma history, only 2-year smoking history, overweight but not obese.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: In adolescents and young adults, HIV/AIDS and obstetric complications from unintended pregnancy account for more preventable female deaths than cardiovascular disease or breast cancer; their risk materializes within a decade if exposure continues.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify behaviors associated with preventable mortality in 16- to 26-year-old females:<br /></br>   – Unprotected intercourse  → HIV, other STIs, ectopic pregnancy, maternal mortality.<br /></br>   – Motor-vehicle collisions → seat-belt use reduces but baseline compliance already ~90% in most states; incremental benefit smaller on an individual level.<br /></br>   – Smoking → cardiovascular and cancer death occur decades later.<br /></br>2. Calculate relative effect: Condoms reduce HIV transmission by ≈ 80 – 90 %, lower HPV/cervical cancer risk, and prevent pregnancy. In the U.S. a pregnant teen’s mortality (obstetric + postpartum) is ≈ 2- to 4-fold higher than her non-pregnant peers. HIV untreated may progress to AIDS in 8–10 years, directly falling inside the question’s window.<br /></br>3. Therefore, consistent condom use offers the single greatest immediate mortality benefit.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: For adolescents, prevention of HIV/STIs and unintended pregnancy through condom use saves more lives in the first decade than modifying habits whose lethal consequences emerge later.  </p></br><p>Documentation if real encounter: counsel on barrier contraception, offer pregnancy test, screen for Chlamydia/Gonorrhea, discuss HPV vaccine and safer-sex practices.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• HIV: average time from infection → AIDS ≈ 8 years; without ART, mortality follows rapidly.<br /></br>• Pregnancy: U.S. maternal mortality ≈ 17 per 100 000 births; teens have higher rates of eclampsia, postpartum hemorrhage, and suicide/homicide related to unintended pregnancy.<br /></br>• STIs: PID → infertility, ectopic pregnancy (≈ 1% fatal), chronic pelvic pain.<br /></br>Condoms provide a physical barrier blocking semen and genital fluid exchange, sharply reducing transmission of HIV, HSV, HPV, GC, CT, etc.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Preventive Measures<br /></br>First-line: Consistent latex (or polyurethane for allergy) condom use with every act of intercourse.<br /></br>Adjuncts: Long-acting reversible contraception (LARC) for pregnancy prevention + continued condom use for STI protection; HPV vaccination; routine STI screening.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Teens Die FAST”<br /></br>F – Fetus (obstetric deaths)<br /></br>A – AIDS/HIV<br /></br>S – STIs complications<br /></br>T – Trauma from MVAs<br /></br>In females, preventing the first three (condoms) edges out the fourth (seat-belt) within 10 years.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong  </p></br><p>B. Monthly breast self-examination<br /></br>• Breast cancer mortality is exceedingly rare under 25; no proven survival benefit from self-exam in any age group.</p></br><p>C. Seat belt use<br /></br>• Major cause of death in adolescents, but widespread usage already; the incremental mortality reduction for one individual is slightly smaller than the combined risks of HIV + pregnancy for a sexually active teen girl.</p></br><p>D. Smoking cessation<br /></br>• Vascular and cancer risk becomes clinically significant after 15–20 years; 10-year mortality benefit for a 16-year-old is negligible.</p></br><p>E. Weight loss<br /></br>• Overweight (BMI 27) carries minimal short-term mortality impact; benefits appear decades later through reduced cardiovascular events.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Automatically picking “seat belt use” or “smoking cessation” without considering the patient’s age and the specified time horizon. Always match the intervention’s time-to-benefit with the time window given in the stem.</p>
53. An 82-year-old woman who resides in a skilled nursing care facility is transferred to the hospital because of a 2-day history of fever and cellulitis of the left side of her face. She is hemiparetic, aphasic, and unable to swallow. She is fed through a gastrostomy tube. She has congestive heart failure, type 2 diabetes mellitus, hypertension, hyperlipidemia, major depressive disorder, and osteoporosis. Her medications are furosemide, metoprolol, digoxin, glyburide, lisinopril, carvedilol, atorvastatin, amitriptyline, alendronate, and 81-mg aspirin. She appears ill and moans frequently. Her temperature is 39.1°C (102.4°F), pulse is 84/min, respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 112/72 mm Hg. A firm, exquisitely tender mass is palpated from the left cheek to the angle of the mandible; this area is also erythematous and edematous. The left pinna appears normal, and the otic canal is not inflamed. The mouth is dry. Bilateral basilar crackles are heard. Cardiac examination shows no abnormalities. The abdomen is soft and nontender. The gastrostomy tube is in place with no inflammation. Her leukocyte count is 28,000/mm3 (78% segmented neutrophils, 18% bands, and 4% lymphocytes), and serum glucose concentration is 168 mg/dL. A chest x-ray shows cardiomegaly; there are bilateral basilar increased markings and atelectasis in the left lung base but no focal infiltrates. Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's fever?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute suppurative parotitis</br>b. Aspiration pneumonia</br>c. Mandibular osteomyelitis</br>d. Osteonecrosis of the jaw</br>e. Urinary tract infection</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Acute suppurative parotitis  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues<br /></br>• Very old, debilitated, NPO/PEG-tube fed → markedly ↓ salivary flow.<br /></br>• Dry mouth + anticholinergic (amitriptyline) → further xerostomia.<br /></br>• Abrupt onset high fever, marked leukocytosis with left shift.<br /></br>• “Firm, exquisitely tender mass … from the left cheek to the angle of the mandible; erythematous and edematous.” Classic location of the parotid gland (anterior to ear, extending to mandibular angle).<br /></br>• External ear canal &amp; pinna normal → rules out otitis externa‐media.<br /></br>These findings together scream acute bacterial infection of the parotid rather than pulmonary, urinary, or bony sources.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Risk factors: elderly, dehydrated, poor oral intake, anticholinergics, diabetes → ↓ salivary clearance.<br /></br>2. Pathogenesis: retrograde ascent of oral flora (usually Staphylococcus aureus &gt; streptococci, oral anaerobes) through Stensen duct.<br /></br>3. Local findings: unilateral painful swollen parotid, overlying erythema, trismus, purulent saliva at duct orifice (not described, but classic).<br /></br>4. Systemic signs: high fever, very high WBC with bandemia.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In frail, dehydrated, or postoperative patients who suddenly develop tender pre-auricular swelling + fever + leukocytosis, think acute suppurative parotitis (commonly S. aureus).  </p></br><p>What would be documented: size &amp; tenderness of parotid, overlying erythema, xerostomia, purulence at Stensen duct if seen, vitals, WBC, and empiric IV antistaphylococcal antibiotics started.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Ascending infection of parotid duct—most often Staphylococcus aureus; anaerobes can coinfect.<br /></br>• Predisposing factors:<br /></br>  – Dehydration/NPO status or tube feeds<br /></br>  – Anticholinergic meds (amitriptyline)<br /></br>  – Elderly/post-operative, debilitated, poor oral hygiene<br /></br>  – Diabetes mellitus<br /></br>• Gland becomes obstructed with thick saliva → bacterial overgrowth → abscess if untreated.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls<br /></br>• Empiric IV anti-staphylococcal coverage: nafcillin or oxacillin PLUS metronidazole or clindamycin for anaerobes; or vancomycin if MRSA risk.<br /></br>• Encourage salivary flow: adequate hydration, sialogogues (lemon drops), warm compresses, gland massage.<br /></br>• Ultrasound/CT if abscess suspected or no rapid improvement.<br /></br>• Surgical drainage only if abscess forms.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PARCHED” for acute Parotitis risk factors:<br /></br>P – Post-op / Poor oral intake<br /></br>A – Anticholinergics (amitriptyline)<br /></br>R – Retrograde infection (Stensen duct)<br /></br>C – Cheek swelling (pre-auricular)<br /></br>H – High fever / High WBC<br /></br>E – Elderly<br /></br>D – Dehydration / Diabetes  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Aspiration pneumonia<br /></br>• Would show new infiltrate in dependent lung segments (classically right lower lobe). CXR shows only basilar atelectasis without focal infiltrate. Local facial swelling not explained.  </p></br><p>c. Mandibular osteomyelitis<br /></br>• Presents as dull jaw pain, induration over mandible, often chronic; requires weeks-months, not sudden 2-day onset. Swelling is usually inferior to mandible, not over parotid region.  </p></br><p>d. Osteonecrosis of the jaw (bisphosphonate-related)<br /></br>• Associated with chronic alendronate use but manifests as exposed necrotic bone inside the oral cavity after dental extraction; not with external erythematous swelling or high leukocytosis.  </p></br><p>e. Urinary tract infection<br /></br>• Common source of fever in elderly, but vignette gives no urinary symptoms, no suprapubic/ CVA tenderness, and provides striking local findings over the face consistent with a focal infection elsewhere.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Take-Home Message<br /></br>In an elderly, dehydrated, or post-op patient with sudden fever + leukocytosis and a tender pre-auricular/mandibular angle swelling, think acute suppurative parotitis—almost always Staph aureus—treat promptly with IV anti-staphylococcal antibiotics and hydration to prevent abscess formation and sepsis.</p>
54. A hospitalized 42-year-old man has chills and a temperature of 38.3°C (101°F) 1 hour after transfusion of packed red blood cells for treatment of anemia secondary to a bleeding duodenal ulcer. He has never received a blood transfusion in the past. He has no known allergies. He is pale and flushed. He is 178 cm (5 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 79 kg (175 lb); BMI is 25 kg/m2. His pulse is 98/min and regular, and blood pressure is 128/82 mm Hg. Examination shows no abnormalities. The most appropriate next step in management is administration of which of the following?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Oral acetaminophen</br>b. Oral ibuprofen</br>c. Subcutaneous epinephrine</br>d. Intravenous diphenhydramine</br>e. Intravenous methylprednisolone</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Oral acetaminophen  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis (Key Clues)<br /></br>• “1 hour after transfusion” – acute reaction timeframe (≤ 6 h).<br /></br>• Only symptoms are fever (38.3 °C), chills, flushing; NO dyspnea, hypotension, back pain, hemoglobinuria, urticaria, or wheezing.<br /></br>• 1st transfusion → unlikely to have formed new anti-RBC antibodies yet.<br /></br>These features point squarely to an acute febrile non-hemolytic transfusion reaction (FNHTR).  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives that rule out other reactions:<br /></br>• Stable BP → not acute hemolytic reaction or anaphylaxis.<br /></br>• No shortness of breath / hypoxemia → not transfusion-related acute lung injury (TRALI).<br /></br>• No hives / pruritus → not urticarial allergic reaction.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Identify acute transfusion reaction.<br /></br>2. Decide which type based on symptoms only (fever/chills).<br /></br>3. FNHTR is benign; management = pause transfusion, give antipyretic, then if symptoms resolve and hemolysis is excluded, transfusion can often be restarted.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Isolated fever/chills during a transfusion → think FNHTR → give acetaminophen.  </p></br><p>Documentation: Stop time of transfusion, vitals, description of reaction, interventions (acetaminophen), lab work sent (direct Coombs, plasma free Hb) to confirm no hemolysis.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• FNHTR occurs when recipient antibodies react with donor leukocytes or, more commonly, when cytokines that accumulated during blood storage are transfused.<br /></br>• Results in release of IL-1, IL-6, TNF-α → fever and rigors.<br /></br>• Risk reduced by leukoreduction (removal of donor WBCs before storage).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>1. Stop transfusion, maintain IV access with normal saline.<br /></br>2. Assess for hemolysis (direct antiglobulin test, plasma free hemoglobin, serum bilirubin).<br /></br>3. Administer oral/IV acetaminophen 650–1,000 mg.<br /></br>Future prevention: ask blood bank for pre-storage leukoreduced products.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tip<br /></br>“FNHTR = Fever, Nothing else → give Tylenol.”<br /></br>Picture a “non-hemolytic thermometer” stuck in a blood bag; simply cool it down with Tylenol.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Oral ibuprofen – Also an antipyretic, but NSAIDs increase GI bleeding risk (this patient has a bleeding duodenal ulcer) and are not first-line; acetaminophen is preferred.<br /></br>c. Subcutaneous epinephrine – Used for anaphylaxis (hypotension, wheeze, angioedema). None of those findings are present.<br /></br>d. Intravenous diphenhydramine – First-line for urticarial/allergic transfusion reactions (pruritic hives). No rash or itching here.<br /></br>e. Intravenous methylprednisolone – Considered for severe allergic reactions or TRALI; unnecessary for isolated fever.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: confusing FNHTR with acute hemolytic reaction (fever + flank pain, dark urine, hypotension) – always look for hemolysis signs.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────</p>
55. A 77-year-old woman comes to the office because of a 1-year history of frequent urinary incontinence during the day and at night. The incontinence is not associated with coughing or sneezing, and she does not have urinary urgency before the episodes. She has not had pain with urination or vaginal discharge. She has a 10-year history of type 2 diabetes mellitus well controlled with metformin. Her other medications are atorvastatin and lisinopril. Examination shows some wetting of an absorbent pad that she is wearing. Results of laboratory studies are shown:</br>Hemoglobin A1c    6.2%</br>Serum</br>Na+               138 mEq/L</br>K+                3.7 mEq/L</br>Cl-               98 mEq/L</br>HCO3-             26 mEq/L</br>Urea nitrogen     12 mg/dL</br>Glucose           180 mg/dL</br>Creatinine        1.1 mg/dL</br>Urine</br>pH                normal</br>Glucose           1+</br>Protein           2+</br>RBC               none</br>WBC               3/hpf</br>Casts             none</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's urinary incontinence?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Hyperglycemia</br>b. Interstitial cystitis</br>c. Medication adverse effect</br>d. Neurogenic bladder</br>e. Stress incontinence</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Neurogenic bladder (diabetic autonomic neuropathy → overflow incontinence)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Chronicity: 1-year history, progressive.<br /></br>• Incontinence pattern: “Frequent … day and night,” NOT precipitated by cough/sneeze (rules out stress), NO preceding urgency/strong urge (rules out urge).<br /></br>• Diabetes mellitus ×10 yrs → raises suspicion for autonomic neuropathy.<br /></br>• Physical exam: only “wetting of an absorbent pad;” no mention of pelvic organ prolapse, no abdominal distention (could be subtle).<br /></br>• UA: mild glucosuria/proteinuria typical of long-standing diabetes; no pyuria/hematuria (argues against infection, interstitial cystitis).<br /></br>Pertinent positive = long-standing diabetes. Pertinent negatives = absence of urgency, stress triggers, pain, infection.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Classify type of incontinence.<br /></br>   – Stress → leakage with ↑intra-abdominal pressure (cough, laugh) → absent here.<br /></br>   – Urge → sudden urgency, can’t make it → absent.<br /></br>   – Overflow → continuous dribbling, day &amp; night, due to incomplete emptying; commonly from neurogenic bladder in diabetics.<br /></br>2. Identify cause of overflow → diabetic autonomic neuropathy damages parasympathetic innervation to detrusor → impaired contractility → chronic urinary retention → overflow leakage.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Elderly patient with long-standing diabetes + continuous dribbling without urgency or stress clues = overflow incontinence from neurogenic bladder.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic hyperglycemia → non-enzymatic glycosylation + sorbitol accumulation in nerves → autonomic neuropathy.<br /></br>• Parasympathetic (S2-4) denervation → atonic detrusor, large post-void residual (PVR) → bladder overdistends → constant leakage.<br /></br>• Often accompanied by peripheral neuropathy, gastroparesis, orthostatic hypotension.</p></br><p>Diagnostics/Management<br /></br>• Post-void residual (bladder scan or catheter) &gt;200 mL confirms overflow.<br /></br>• Urodynamic testing if diagnosis unclear.<br /></br>• Treatment:<br /></br>  – Scheduled or intermittent straight catheterization (first-line).<br /></br>  – Cholinergic agonist (bethanechol) sometimes adjunct.<br /></br>  – Optimize glycemic control; screen for other autonomic neuropathy complications.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“D for Dribble in Diabetes” → Diabetic neuropathy → Overflow Dribbling.<br /></br>Picture a floppy balloon (bladder) with tiny pinholes leaking constantly.</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Hyperglycemia: Would cause transient osmotic polyuria with intact urgency/volume, not chronic dribbling. Her glucose (180 mg/dL, A1c 6.2%) is modest and well-controlled.<br /></br>b. Interstitial cystitis: Presents with bladder pain relieved by voiding, urinary frequency/urgency, often in middle-aged women; pain absent here.<br /></br>c. Medication adverse effect: None of her drugs (metformin, lisinopril, atorvastatin) cause overflow incontinence. Agents such as anticholinergics, α-blockers, or diuretics would be implicated if present.<br /></br>e. Stress incontinence: Requires leakage with ↑intra-abdominal pressure; typically seen post-childbirth, atrophic pelvic floor, not evident in vignette.</p></br><p>Key Documentation Point<br /></br>Record PVR measurement, neuropathic symptoms, and counseling given on intermittent catheterization and glycemic optimization.</p></br><p>Remember: Continuous dribbling + diabetes = think neurogenic bladder from autonomic neuropathy.</p>
56. A 71-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of a 1-week history of increasingly severe pain in his left third toe. During the past 36 hours, he has not slept because of the pain. He has coronary artery disease, hypertension, and hyperlipidemia. Six years ago, he had a myocardial infarction. He has never undergone an operation. His medications are ramipril, labetalol, and aspirin. Acetaminophen and ibuprofen have provided no pain relief. He smoked one pack of cigarettes daily for 40 years but quit 6 years ago. He appears uncomfortable. His pulse is 84/min, and blood pressure is 170/90 mm Hg. A 4-cm, pulsatile, nontender mass is palpated in the left inguinal region. The left third toe is necrotic and exquisitely tender to palpation; there is no erythema or drainage. Pedal pulses are 2+ on the left. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. A CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis is shown. In addition to intravenous analgesic therapy, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Piperacillin-tazobactam prescription</br>b. Placement of an Unna boot</br>c. Placement of a vena cava filter</br>d. Rivaroxaban therapy</br>e. Surgical arterial repair</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: Surgical arterial repair  </p></br><hr /></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “4-cm, pulsatile, nontender mass … in the left inguinal region” → classic for a common femoral artery aneurysm (CFAA).<br /></br>• “Necrotic and exquisitely tender” toe but “pedal pulses 2+” → ischemic changes from micro-embolization (“blue toe syndrome”) rather than large-vessel occlusion (which would abolish pulses).<br /></br>• No fever, erythema, or drainage → dry gangrene, not an infection.<br /></br>• 71-y/o man with long atherosclerotic history (MI, CAD, HTN, smoker) → high risk for peripheral aneurysms and cholesterol crystal emboli.  </p></br><p>Together, a peripheral arterial aneurysm + distal ischemic emboli → treat the source surgically.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify source: Pulsatile groin mass = CFAA.<br /></br>2. Identify complication: Distal embolization causing digital ischemia while pulses remain intact.<br /></br>3. Management priority: Remove/repair aneurysm before more emboli, limb loss, or rupture occur.<br /></br>4. Analgesia is given acutely, but definitive therapy = surgical arterial repair (resection + interposition graft or endovascular exclusion).<br /></br>Core takeaway: A painful, blue/necrotic toe with preserved pulses usually means an upstream embolic source (often a peripheral aneurysm) that must be repaired, not simply anticoagulated or treated with antibiotics.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls (real patient)<br /></br>• Vascular exam findings (location/size of mass, pulse quality)<br /></br>• Degree and type of tissue loss (dry gangrene vs wet)<br /></br>• Plan for vascular surgery consult and operative intervention</p></br><hr /></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Peripheral arterial aneurysms (popliteal &gt; femoral) develop from atherosclerotic wall degeneration.<br /></br>• Mural thrombus forms in the aneurysmal sac → fragments shower distally → micro-infarcts of toes/forefoot (“trash foot,” cholesterol emboli).<br /></br>• Because the parent artery lumen is still open, distal pulses can be normal.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>First-line definitive treatment = open or endovascular repair with grafting or stent-graft exclusion of the aneurysm.<br /></br>Adjuncts: intra-operative thrombectomy of distal vessels, toe amputation after revascularization if tissue is non-viable.<br /></br>Imaging: Duplex US or CT angiography (already done here) defines size and anatomy.</p></br><hr /></br><p>High-Yield Memory Tip<br /></br>Blue (or black) toe + intact pulses = think “trash foot from an aneurysm or cholesterol shower.”  “Fix the sac—save the limb.”</p></br><hr /></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>A. Piperacillin-tazobactam: For infected (wet) gangrene or cellulitis. This toe has dry ischemic necrosis with no signs of infection.<br /></br>B. Unna boot: Compression therapy for venous stasis ulcers; contraindicated in arterial ischemia and would worsen pain.<br /></br>C. Vena cava filter: Prevents pulmonary emboli from DVT; nothing points to venous thromboembolism here.<br /></br>D. Rivaroxaban: Anticoagulation for DVT/PE or atrial fibrillation; will not eliminate the aneurysmal embolic source and is inadequate as sole therapy.<br /></br>E. Surgical arterial repair: Correct—removes the embolic nidus and prevents further limb-threatening events.</p></br><hr /></br><p>Mnemonic<br /></br>“Pulsatile groin = PAD bomb. Toe turns blue, fix the aneurysm too.”</p>
57. A 37-year-old woman comes to the physician requesting assistance in applying for disability income. She fell at home 12 months ago and left her job at a day-care center because of subsequent low back pain. She received the diagnosis of major depressive disorder 8 months ago; her sleep, appetite, and mood have improved with citalopram therapy. Her only other medication is ibuprofen. She asks the physician to document a permanent and total disability on an application form for disability income so that she will have the financial resources to start college education on a full-time basis. She explains that she will become depressed again if she resumes her stressful job as a child-care provider. She does not appear to be in distress. She is 157 cm (5 ft 2 in) tall and weighs 68 kg (150 lb); BMI is 27 kg/m2. Her temperature is 37 °C (98.6 °F), pulse is 64/min, and blood pressure is 108/60 mm Hg. Examination of the back shows no tenderness; range of motion of the lumbar spine is full. Neurologic examination shows no abnormalities. The physician will not fill out the form. In addition to informing the patient, which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Reassure the patient that she will be all right</br>b. Confront the patient about requesting participation in fraud</br>c. Discuss options for the patient to work while pursuing an education</br>d. Explain that the patient is unlikely to have another depressive episode if she follows her medication regimen</br>e. Obtain an x-ray of the lumbosacral spine</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Discuss options for the patient to work while pursuing an education  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Red flags that the request is inappropriate: normal physical exam, normal neuro exam, only mild BMI elevation, and ability to attend college full-time – none support “permanent and total” disability.<br /></br>• Major depressive disorder is currently well controlled on citalopram. No current depressive symptoms are given.<br /></br>• Key task for the physician: decline to falsify a disability claim yet remain supportive and therapeutic.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify the problem: Patient is requesting the doctor’s participation in a fraudulent disability filing.<br /></br>Step 2 – Physician’s immediate obligation: Refuse to falsify documentation.<br /></br>Step 3 – Maintain the therapeutic alliance: Instead of shaming or abandoning the patient, explore her underlying goals (going to college, avoiding a stressful job) and collaboratively develop legitimate solutions.<br /></br>Take-home concept: When a patient requests an unethical action, physicians should (1) refuse, (2) explain why, and (3) offer practical, patient-centered alternatives.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>No organic low-back pathology is demonstrated. Major depression is in remission with SSRI treatment. There is no current medical condition that would qualify for permanent disability.  </p></br><p>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• No further back imaging is indicated; absence of “red-flag” findings (neurologic deficits, trauma, fever, malignancy history, etc.) makes plain x-ray low-yield.<br /></br>• Management of depression: continue SSRI, supportive therapy, encourage healthy lifestyle, set vocational/educational goals.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“R-E-F-U-S-E” for unethical patient requests:<br /></br>R – Refuse to violate ethics/law<br /></br>E – Explain your reasoning<br /></br>F – Find the patient’s true goal<br /></br>U – Understand concerns/emotions<br /></br>S – Suggest legitimate alternatives<br /></br>E – Encourage follow-up/support  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Reassure the patient that she will be all right – Dismisses her expressed concerns and ignores the larger issue (fraudulent request).<br /></br>b. Confront the patient about requesting participation in fraud – Accusatory; risks rupturing the physician-patient relationship without addressing her goals.<br /></br>d. Explain that the patient is unlikely to have another depressive episode if she stays on medication – Provides education but sidesteps both the unethical request and her career/financial concerns.<br /></br>e. Obtain an x-ray of the lumbosacral spine – Unnecessary imaging; no exam findings that warrant it, and imaging does not address her disability request.  </p></br><p>Documentation Pearl<br /></br>Record the patient’s request, your refusal with rationale (no objective findings to justify disability), patient’s current functional status, and the plan to discuss work/education options and follow-up for mood monitoring.</p>
58. A 67-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 1-year history of a nonproductive cough and progressive shortness of breath with exertion. Her symptoms were not a problem at first, but she now has trouble walking her dog because of shortness of breath after walking one-half block. She has not had fever or any other symptoms. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She does not smoke. She retired from her position as an executive secretary 5 years ago. Vital signs are within normal limits. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 92%. Examination shows no cyanosis. Fine, dry crackles are heard at both lung bases. Examination of the hands shows clubbing. There is no peripheral edema. A chest x-ray shows fine linear opacities in the lower third of the lungs bilaterally. A CT scan of the chest confirms an interstitial pattern. Pulmonary function tests show an FVC of 48% of predicted and a diffusion capacity of the lung for carbon monoxide of 55% of predicted. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Measurement of serum ACE activity</br>b. Measurement of serum α1-antitrypsin concentration</br>c. Serum antinuclear antibody assay</br>d. Bronchoalveolar lavage</br>e. Lung biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Lung biopsy  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key findings: 1-year history of progressive, non-productive cough and exertional dyspnea; basilar “Velcro-like” dry crackles; digital clubbing; restrictive pattern on PFT (FVC 48 % predicted) with ↓DLCO (55 % predicted); chest x-ray and CT showing bilateral basilar reticular/linear interstitial infiltrates.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No fever, wheezing, hemoptysis, smoking history, occupational dust exposure, or connective-tissue disease symptoms.<br /></br>These data point strongly toward a chronic interstitial lung disease (ILD), and in a 67-year-old the leading consideration is idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis (IPF, pathologic pattern = usual interstitial pneumonia, UIP).  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Recognize restrictive PFT + ↓DLCO → ILD.<br /></br>2. Basilar crackles + clubbing in an older adult → think IPF/UIP.<br /></br>3. However, ILD is a broad category (sarcoidosis, hypersensitivity pneumonitis, CTD-associated ILD, pneumoconioses, drug-induced, etc.).<br /></br>4. HRCT here shows “an interstitial pattern” but no description of classic honey-combing/traction bronchiectasis that would make UIP “definite.”<br /></br>5. Therefore histopathologic confirmation is required to secure the diagnosis and to exclude other potentially treatable ILDs. Surgical (video-assisted thoracoscopic) lung biopsy sampling from &gt;1 lobe is the gold standard.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: When HRCT is not definitive for UIP/IPF, the next diagnostic step is surgical lung biopsy for histology.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>IPF involves patchy, progressive fibrosis of alveolar interstitium with a usual interstitial pneumonia pattern. Etiology is unknown; risk factors include older age and possibly environmental exposures. Progressive scarring impairs gas exchange → restrictive mechanics and ↓DLCO. Prognosis is poor; anti-fibrotic therapy (nintedanib, pirfenidone) may slow decline, and lung transplant is definitive.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Diagnostic hierarchy: history/PE → PFT → high-resolution CT.<br /></br>   – If HRCT shows classic UIP (subpleural, basal-predominant reticulation with honey-comb cysts), biopsy is not required.<br /></br>   – If CT is “possible” or “inconsistent” with UIP (as in this vignette), surgical lung biopsy is indicated.<br /></br>• Blood tests (ANA, RF, anti-CCP, anti-Scl-70, ACE, etc.) are useful to screen for secondary causes but cannot confirm IPF.<br /></br>• BAL is helpful for suspected infections or eosinophilic/granulomatous lung disease, not for UIP.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“B-C-B” for ILD work-up:<br /></br>B lood (ANA, ACE, etc.)<br /></br>C T (high-resolution)<br /></br>B iopsy if CT not diagnostic for UIP  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Serum ACE level – Elevated in sarcoidosis (younger patients, bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy, non-caseating granulomas). This patient is older, has basilar fibrosis, and no lymphadenopathy.<br /></br>b. α1-Antitrypsin level – Screens for panacinar emphysema (obstructive PFTs) and cirrhosis; not useful for restrictive ILD.<br /></br>c. ANA assay – Reasonable early screen for connective-tissue–associated ILD, but by itself cannot establish the diagnosis; normal ANA would still leave many ILDs on the table. Histology is required for definitive IPF.<br /></br>d. Bronchoalveolar lavage – May help diagnose infections, hypersensitivity pneumonitis (↑ lymphocytes), eosinophilic pneumonia, etc. It does not provide the architectural pattern (patchwork fibrosis, honey-comb change) needed to diagnose UIP/IPF.<br /></br>e. Lung biopsy – Provides histologic confirmation of usual interstitial pneumonia when CT is nondiagnostic; thus it is the most appropriate next diagnostic step.</p>
59. A 2-year-old boy is brought to the physician for a follow-up examination 2 weeks after being treated for an ear infection. He has been treated for six episodes of acute otitis media and one episode of pneumonia since birth. Three months ago, he underwent placement of tympanostomy tubes. He currently takes no medications. He appears well. He is at the 75th percentile for length and 25th percentile for weight. Examination shows several 0.5- to 1-cm, firm, nontender, mobile lymph nodes in the anterior cervical chain. Tympanostomy tubes are in place bilaterally without discharge.  </br>Laboratory studies show:  </br>Hemoglobin: 12.5 g/dL  </br>Hematocrit: 37%  </br>Leukocyte count: 8100/mm^3  </br>Platelet count: 257,000/mm^3  </br>Serum:  </br>IgA: <7 mg/dL  </br>IgG: 720 mg/dL  </br>IgM: 75 mg/dL  </br>Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: G</br></br>a. Oral antibiotic therapy  </br>b. Oral prednisone therapy  </br>c. Intramuscular injection of immune globulin  </br>d. Intravenous antithymocyte globulin  </br>e. Intravenous immune globulin infusion  </br>f. Bone marrow transplant  </br>g. No intervention is indicated at this time</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>g. No intervention is indicated at this time  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues<br /></br>  – 2-year-old with six episodes of otitis media + one pneumonia → recurrent mucosal infections<br /></br>  – Normal growth (75th % length, 25th % weight) and otherwise well appearing<br /></br>  – Isolated very low IgA (&lt; 7 mg/dL) with normal IgG (720 mg/dL) and IgM (75 mg/dL)<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: recurrent sinopulmonary infections, isolated IgA deficiency<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no chronic diarrhea, no severe opportunistic infections, no failure to thrive, and normal counts of other immunoglobulins and blood cells → rules out combined or severe B-/T-cell defects  </p></br><p>Pattern: “Recurrent mucosal infections + isolated ↓IgA + normal IgG/IgM” = Selective IgA Deficiency (most common primary immunodeficiency).  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify immune labs: Only IgA is profoundly low.<br /></br>2. Correlate with clinical picture: mild, recurrent ENT/lung infections in an otherwise healthy child.<br /></br>3. Determine need for intervention: Most patients are asymptomatic or mildly symptomatic; management is supportive—treat infections as they arise. Routine prophylaxis, steroids, IVIG, or transplant are not recommended. Therefore the appropriate next step is observation.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: Selective IgA deficiency usually requires no routine therapy; avoid IVIG because it contains little IgA and can trigger anti-IgA anaphylaxis.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Note diagnosis of selective IgA deficiency and counsel family regarding increased risk of transfusion reactions (require washed or IgA-deficient blood products in the future).  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: B-cell maturation defect → inability to class-switch to IgA.<br /></br>• Risk factors: sporadic or familial; associated with celiac disease, autoimmune disorders, atopy.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations<br /></br>  – Recurrent sinopulmonary &amp; GI infections (IgA is main mucosal antibody).<br /></br>  – Allergies, autoimmune disease (esp. celiac, SLE, RA).<br /></br>  – Anaphylaxis to blood products containing IgA.<br /></br>• Most remain asymptomatic; overall prognosis is excellent.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics Summary<br /></br>• No baseline therapy.<br /></br>• Treat infections with standard antibiotics when they occur.<br /></br>• Consider prophylactic antibiotics only in exceptionally frequent/severe infections.<br /></br>• Immunoglobulin replacement (IVIG) NOT used—preparations contain minimal IgA and pose anaphylaxis risk.<br /></br>• Vaccinations: routine schedule; consider pneumococcal/flu vaccines to decrease infections.  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Memory Aids<br /></br>“A’s” of IgA deficiency:<br /></br>• Asymptomatic majority<br /></br>• Airways &amp; GI infections<br /></br>• Autoimmune/Atopy associations<br /></br>• Anaphylaxis to blood products<br /></br>• Avoid (no) IVIG  </p></br><p>──────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Oral antibiotic therapy – No active infection; long-term prophylaxis reserved for very severe/recurrent cases.<br /></br>b. Oral prednisone therapy – Steroids have no role in selective IgA deficiency.<br /></br>c. Intramuscular immune globulin – Same issue as IVIG; contains little IgA and can provoke reactions.<br /></br>d. Intravenous antithymocyte globulin – Used for aplastic anemia or transplant rejection, not humoral deficiencies.<br /></br>e. Intravenous immune globulin infusion – Lacks therapeutic IgA content and risks anti-IgA anaphylaxis.<br /></br>f. Bone marrow transplant – Reserved for combined/severe immunodeficiencies (e.g., SCID); unnecessary here.  </p></br><p>Key difference: All incorrect options imply more aggressive therapy appropriate for severe or combined immunodeficiencies, whereas isolated IgA deficiency is usually mild and managed expectantly.</p>
60. A 42-year-old woman comes to the physician because of nausea and vomiting and abdominal cramping and swelling over the past 12 hours. She takes an oral contraceptive. She underwent an appendectomy 5 years ago. Abdominal examination shows distention and moderate tenderness without guarding. An x-ray of the abdomen is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Adynamic ileus</br>b. Mechanical obstruction of the small intestine</br>c. Pelvic abscess</br>d. Perforated abdominal viscus</br>e. Regional enteritis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer: Mechanical obstruction of the small intestine</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• 42-year-old woman with sudden-onset nausea, vomiting, cramping abdominal pain, and progressive distention for 12 h.<br /></br>• Past surgical history: appendectomy 5 years ago → most common cause of postoperative adhesive bands.<br /></br>• Exam: distention + moderate tenderness, no peritoneal signs (so perforation less likely).<br /></br>• Abdominal x-ray (classic test picture) on NBME almost always shows: dilated, stacked (“step-ladder”) loops of small bowel with multiple air-fluid levels and little or no gas in the colon → pathognomonic for mechanical small-bowel obstruction (SBO).</p></br><p>Pertinent positives: colicky pain, vomiting, distention, prior abdominal surgery, step-ladder air-fluid levels.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no recent operation, opioids, sepsis, or electrolyte disturbance (all would favor adynamic ileus); no free air under diaphragm (rules out perforation).</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise thought process:</br>1. Ask: “Is the bowel obstructed or simply not moving (ileus)?”<br /></br>   • X-ray shows discrete, dilated SB loops + air-fluid levels → obstruction.<br /></br>   • Ileus would show uniform gas throughout SB and colon with few fluid levels.</br>2. Identify the level: Large bowel vs small bowel?<br /></br>   • Centrally located loops with valvulae conniventes spanning full width → small bowel.</br>3. Look for the cause: Prior abdominal operation = adhesions (#1 cause of SBO in adults).  </p></br><p>Take-home concept: Prior surgery → think adhesions → mechanical SBO with step-ladder air-fluid levels.</p></br><p>Documentation note: would record “suspected adhesive SBO—NPO, NG tube for decompression, IVF, serial exams; surgical consult.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Adhesions form from peritoneal inflammation → fibrous bands tether bowel → extrinsic compression or kinking → proximal dilation, distal collapse.<br /></br>• Leads to bowel wall edema → secretion accumulation → vomiting, electrolyte loss, risk of strangulation if vascular supply compromised.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Diagnostics &amp; Treatment</br>First-line work-up: upright &amp; supine abdominal x-ray (already done), CBC, CMP, lactate.<br /></br>Management:<br /></br>1. Initial: NPO, aggressive IV fluids + electrolytes, NG tube decompression, analgesia.<br /></br>2. CT with PO/IV contrast if unclear or concern for closed-loop/strangulation.<br /></br>3. Surgery (adhesiolysis) if peritonitis, strangulation signs (fever, leukocytosis, acidosis, tachycardia) or failure of conservative therapy in ~24–48 h.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“4 A’s of SBO”: Adhesions, Abdominal cramps (colicky), Air-fluid levels, Absent stool/ flatus.<br /></br>Picture a ladder (air-fluid “step-ladder”) leaning on an old post-op scar—climb the ladder to remember adhesions cause SBO.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>A. Adynamic (paralytic) ileus<br /></br>   – Usually within 24–72 h of recent surgery, with opioids, sepsis, hypokalemia, or pancreatitis.<br /></br>   – X-ray: diffuse gas in small AND large bowel, minimal air-fluid levels. None of these fit the vignette.</p></br><p>C. Pelvic abscess<br /></br>   – Presents days-weeks after pelvic/abdominal surgery with fever, leukocytosis, localized tenderness, and possibly diarrhea. X-ray does not show diffuse dilated small-bowel loops.</p></br><p>D. Perforated abdominal viscus<br /></br>   – Acute, severe pain with peritoneal signs (guarding, rigidity). Upright chest/abdominal x-ray would show free intraperitoneal air under the diaphragm, not multiple step-ladder levels.</p></br><p>E. Regional enteritis (Crohn disease)<br /></br>   – Chronic relapsing course, weight loss, diarrhea; imaging may show skip lesions, strictures, fistulas, not an acute obstructive pattern in a patient otherwise asymptomatic.</p></br><p>────────────────────────</br>Core Takeaway</br>In a patient with prior abdominal surgery who acutely develops colicky pain, vomiting, distention, and an x-ray with multiple central air-fluid levels, the NBME wants you to choose mechanical small-bowel obstruction due to post-operative adhesions.</p>
61. A 22-year-old woman comes to the physician because of a 2-day history of severe shortness of breath. Over the past week, she has been taking ibuprofen for fever, chills, and muscle pain. She has no history of serious illness and takes no other medications. Her temperature is 38.4°C (101.1°F), pulse is 105/min and irregular, respirations are 28/min, and blood pressure is 70/50 mm Hg. Examination shows jugular venous distention. Crackles are heard throughout both lung fields. An S3 gallop is heard. Pulses are decreased in the upper and lower extremities. An x-ray of the chest shows cardiomegaly and perihilar fluid. An ECG shows frequent premature ventricular contractions. Echocardiography shows cardiomegaly with normal valve function and an ejection fraction of 18%; her estimated cardiac output is 1.4 L/min (N≈4–5). Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Acute myocarditis</br>b. Acute pericarditis</br>c. Constrictive pericarditis</br>d. Ischemic cardiomyopathy</br>e. Pericardial tamponade</br>f. Superior vena cava syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Acute myocarditis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis (key clues)</br>• 22-year-old otherwise healthy woman → very low likelihood of long-standing coronary artery or valvular disease.<br /></br>• 1-week viral prodrome (fever, chills, myalgias; taking ibuprofen) → classic set-up for viral myocarditis (most commonly Coxsackie B, adenovirus, parvovirus B19).<br /></br>• Sudden decompensation with:<br /></br>  – Hypotension (70/50 mm Hg) and tachycardia → cardiogenic shock.<br /></br>  – JVD, diffuse crackles, S3 gallop → acute biventricular failure with pulmonary edema.<br /></br>• Echo: marked cardiomegaly, NORMAL valves, EF 18 % (severe systolic dysfunction).<br /></br>• ECG: frequent PVCs → ventricular irritability typical of inflamed/necrotic myocardium.<br /></br>• Cardiac output 1.4 L/min (N ≈ 4–5) → pump failure.  </p></br><p>These findings point to an acute, global decrease in myocardial contractility that developed days after a viral illness = ACUTE MYOCARDITIS.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1: Identify that the clinical problem is cardiogenic shock (low BP, low CO, pulmonary edema).<br /></br>Step 2: Ask, “Why does this 22-year-old have pump failure?”<br /></br>  • No chronic HTN, CAD risk, or valve disease.<br /></br>  • Occurred days after viral illness → think myocarditis.<br /></br>Step 3: Echo shows dilated, weak ventricle but no valve abnormality and no mention of pericardial fluid → myocarditis/dilated cardiomyopathy, not pericardial disease.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Viral myocarditis can cause rapid-onset dilated cardiomyopathy with severe systolic dysfunction, arrhythmias, and shock in young patients.</p></br><p>What would be documented: fulminant heart failure most consistent with acute viral myocarditis; plan for ICU, inotropes, possible mechanical circulatory support, viral serologies/endomyocardial biopsy if indicated.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>1. Viral infection → direct myocyte injury + immune-mediated destruction.<br /></br>2. Diffuse inflammation → myocardial edema, necrosis → ↓ contractility, ventricular dilation → acute systolic HF.<br /></br>3. Irritable myocardium → ventricular arrhythmias (PVCs, VT).<br /></br>Risk factors: preceding viral URI/GI illness, pregnancy, autoimmune disease.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield points)</br>Initial: ICU, oxygen/ventilation, IV diuretics, vasopressors/inotropes (dobutamine), afterload reduction (ACE inhibitor).<br /></br>Long-term if survives: GDMT for HFrEF (ACEI/ARB/ARNI, beta-blocker, MRA, SGLT2 inhibitor).<br /></br>Some cases: mechanical circulatory support or transplant.<br /></br>Diagnostics often confirmatory: troponin ↑, cardiac MRI with late gadolinium enhancement, endomyocardial biopsy (Dallas criteria) if diagnosis uncertain or to guide therapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Coxsackie B BREAKS the heart” → B for Breaks (myocarditis).<br /></br>Young patient + viral prodrome + new S3/SHF = think myocarditis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>b. Acute pericarditis – Presents with pleuritic chest pain relieved by leaning forward, pericardial friction rub, diffuse ST-segment elevation/PR depression; contractility and EF are NORMAL. No pulmonary edema/JVD from pump failure.<br /></br>c. Constrictive pericarditis – Chronic disease (months–years) after TB, radiation, surgery; causes right-sided failure, Kussmaul sign, but lungs usually clear and EF preserved.<br /></br>d. Ischemic cardiomyopathy – Requires chronic CAD or prior infarcts; rare at age 22 without major risk factors and not preceded by viral prodrome.<br /></br>e. Pericardial tamponade – Hypotension and JVD yes, but lungs are CLEAR (no crackles) and pulses are paradoxical; echo would show large pericardial effusion, not poor EF.<br /></br>f. Superior vena cava syndrome – Facial/upper-extremity edema plethora; no pulmonary edema, no low EF, and BP usually preserved.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Snapshot</br>Viral prodrome + rapid heart failure/arrhythmia in a young person = acute myocarditis.</p>
62. A study is conducted to determine if a hemoglobin A1c greater than 15% predicts diabetic retinopathy. Results show that 88% of all patients without diabetic retinopathy had a hemoglobin A1c below 15%. Which of the following is the most accurate interpretation of these results?	Correct answer: G</br></br>a. False-negative rate is 88%</br>b. False-positive rate is 88%</br>c. Negative predictive value is 88%</br>d. Positive predictive value is 88%</br>e. Power of the study design is 88%</br>f. Sensitivity is 88%</br>g. Specificity is 88%</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>g. Specificity is 88 %</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key phrase: “88 % of all patients WITHOUT diabetic retinopathy had a hemoglobin A1c BELOW 15 %.”<br /></br>• Population being described = patients who truly do NOT have the disease (true disease-negative group).<br /></br>• Result being measured = proportion whose test result is NEGATIVE (A1c &lt; 15 %).<br /></br>That proportion (TN ÷ [TN + FP]) is, by definition, SPECIFICITY.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the denominator: “all patients without diabetic retinopathy” → TN + FP.<br /></br>2. Identify the numerator: those with an A1c &lt; 15 % (a “negative” result) among this disease-negative group → TN.<br /></br>3. TN / (TN + FP) = Specificity. Therefore the statement is saying “The specificity is 88 %.”</p></br><p>Take-home: Specificity measures a test’s ability to correctly label healthy people as negative.</p></br><p>Documentation Pearl: In the assessment of any diagnostic test, record both sensitivity and specificity so readers know how well the test distinguishes disease vs no disease.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review (concise)<br /></br>• Diabetic retinopathy risk rises with chronic hyperglycemia (A1c levels).<br /></br>• This study is evaluating whether an A1c cutoff (&gt; 15 %) can serve as a diagnostic marker for retinopathy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Diagnostic Test Metrics Refresher<br /></br>• Sensitivity = TP / (TP + FN)<br /></br>• Specificity = TN / (TN + FP)<br /></br>• Positive predictive value (PPV) = TP / (TP + FP)<br /></br>• Negative predictive value (NPV) = TN / (TN + FN)<br /></br>• False-positive rate = 1 – specificity = FP / (TN + FP)<br /></br>• False-negative rate = 1 – sensitivity = FN / (TP + FN)</p></br><p>Mnemonic: “SPIN &amp; SNOUT”<br /></br>• SPecificity rules IN (high specificity ⇒ positive result ≈ disease present)<br /></br>• SeNsitivity rules OUT (high sensitivity ⇒ negative result ≈ disease absent)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. False-negative rate 88 % – Would describe FN / (TP + FN); vignette focuses on patients without disease, not with.<br /></br>b. False-positive rate 88 % – Would be FP / (TN + FP); the 88 % represents those who are NOT false positives (it’s the complement).<br /></br>c. NPV 88 % – NPV denominator includes all NEGATIVE tests; statement’s denominator is all NON-diseased patients.<br /></br>d. PPV 88 % – Requires denominator of all POSITIVE tests; not given here.<br /></br>e. Power 88 % – Power concerns hypothesis testing (1 – β); unrelated to test operating characteristics.<br /></br>f. Sensitivity 88 % – Would involve diseased patients; the vignette explicitly uses patients without the disease.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>1. “Specific = Specified group WITHOUT disease.” If the sentence starts with “Among those WITHOUT disease…,” think specificity.<br /></br>2. Picture a 2×2 table: disease status on top, test result on side—read the numerator and denominator to decide which box is being described.</p>
63. A 13-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because of a 3-day history of pain and pressure in her face, itchy eyes, and decreased hearing in both ears. She has not had fever, chills, muscle pain, or nausea. Acetaminophen has not relieved her symptoms. She takes no other medications. One year ago, she had a similar episode and was successfully treated with azithromycin therapy at an urgent care center, and her mother requests another prescription for azithromycin. The patient’s two brothers have well-controlled asthma. Her vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows a supple neck with a few shotty lymph nodes in the upper cervical chain. The conjunctivae have a cobblestone appearance. The inferior nasal turbinates are edematous. The tympanic membranes are injected bilaterally. There is moderate mucus in the posterior pharynx. The tonsils are not enlarged. The lungs are clear to auscultation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. CT scan of the head</br>b. MRI of the sinuses</br>c. Ceftriaxone therapy</br>d. Loratidine therapy</br>e. Prednisone therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Loratadine therapy</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues that point to allergic rhinitis—not bacterial sinusitis or another process:<br /></br>• Itchy eyes + cobblestone conjunctivae → classic for allergic conjunctivitis.<br /></br>• Pale/edematous inferior nasal turbinates &amp;
