clear nasal discharge → allergic rhinitis.<br /></br>• Bilateral ear “pressure” &amp; injected tympanic membranes → eustachian-tube dysfunction secondary to nasal mucosal edema (common with allergies).<br /></br>• No fever, myalgias, or purulent nasal drainage, and symptoms only 3 days → argues against acute bacterial sinusitis (needs ≥10 days of symptoms or severe fever/pain).<br /></br>• Prior “azithromycin helped” is anecdotal; many viral/allergic episodes self-resolve during a course of antibiotics, creating false attribution.  </p></br><p>Pertinent negatives: afebrile, clear lungs, tonsils not enlarged, no sore throat, no systemic symptoms.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: itchy eyes + nasal edema + cobblestoning = allergic triad.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li></br><p>Determine whether this is infectious or allergic.<br /></br>   – Pruritus (itch) virtually rules in allergy.<br /></br>   – Lack of fever/purulent discharge rules out bacterial sinusitis.  </p></br></li></br><li></br><p>First-line therapy for mild/intermittent allergic rhinitis/conjunctivitis in children = a non-sedating oral antihistamine (loratadine, cetirizine, fexofenadine) ± environmental control.<br /></br>   – Intranasal steroids can be added if uncontrolled.</p></br></li></br><li></br><p>Therefore, the most appropriate next step is to start loratadine.</p></br></li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: When a vignette highlights eye/nasal itch with cobblestoning and pale boggy turbinates, think allergic rhinitis and treat with antihistamine (loratadine).</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: allergic rhinitis assessment, symptom duration/onset, absence of fever/purulence, recommend antihistamine, trigger avoidance counseling.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>Allergic rhinitis = IgE-mediated (type I) hypersensitivity to environmental allergens.<br /></br>• Allergen cross-links IgE on mast cells → histamine + leukotriene release → vasodilation, mucosal edema, pruritus, increased secretions.<br /></br>• Chronic nasal inflammation → eustachian-tube edema → conductive hearing changes &amp; tympanic membrane injection.<br /></br>• Conjunctival papillary hypertrophy (“cobblestoning”) from chronic histamine exposure.</p></br><p>Risk factors: atopy, family history of asthma/eczema (her brothers are asthmatic).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review  </p></br><p>First-line:<br /></br>• Second-generation oral antihistamine (loratadine 10 mg daily or weight-based peds dose).<br /></br>Adjunct/second-line if needed:<br /></br>• Intranasal glucocorticoid spray (fluticasone, mometasone).<br /></br>• Antihistamine eye drops (olopatadine).<br /></br>• Leukotriene receptor antagonist (montelukast) in select cases.<br /></br>Diagnostics: none acutely; allergy testing reserved for refractory or unclear cases.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“ITCH = It’s The Classic Hint”<br /></br>I = Itchy eyes/nose<br /></br>T = Turbinates pale &amp; boggy<br /></br>C = Cobblestone conjunctiva<br /></br>H = Histamine blocker (lorata­dine) is first treatment  </p></br><p>Visualize a child rubbing her eyes with tissue paper with pale “boggy” nose—reach for the once-daily little oval loratadine pill.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. CT scan of the head<br /></br>• Indicated for complicated sinusitis (e.g., orbital cellulitis, intracranial extension) or chronic sinus disease &gt;12 wk. No red-flag symptoms here, and radiation risk is unnecessary.</p></br><p>b. MRI of the sinuses<br /></br>• Used for suspected invasive fungal sinusitis, malignancy, or intracranial spread—not appropriate for an uncomplicated, short-duration presentation.</p></br><p>c. Ceftriaxone therapy<br /></br>• Targets acute bacterial sinusitis/otitis media. Bacterial sinusitis requires ≥10 days of symptoms, severe fever (&gt;39 °C), or “double-worsening.” None are present; tympanic membranes are injected but not bulging/purulent. Antibiotics would overtreat and contribute to resistance.</p></br><p>e. Prednisone therapy<br /></br>• Systemic steroids reserved for severe refractory allergic rhinitis or nasal polyposis; adverse-effect profile outweighs benefit for mild/moderate, first-episode disease. Intranasal steroids (not systemic) would precede oral steroids if escalation were needed.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: assuming “facial pressure” automatically equals bacterial sinusitis → remember duration/severity criteria and allergic signs.</p>
64. A 76-year-old woman is scheduled to undergo right knee arthroscopy at an ambulatory surgery center. Before the procedure begins, the surgical team confirms the identity of the patient, the procedure being performed, the side and site of the procedure, the patient's position, and the availability of needed equipment. The procedure is then completed without complications, and the patient is discharged home 4 hours later. Which of the following is most likely increased at this center as a result of the pre-procedure actions of the surgical team?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Efficiency</br>b. Patient satisfaction</br>c. Patient-centered care</br>d. Reliability</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Reliability  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clue: “Before the procedure begins, the surgical team confirms the identity of the patient, the procedure being performed, the side and site of the procedure, the patient’s position, and the availability of needed equipment.”<br /></br>  – This is exactly the “time-out” mandated by the Joint Commission Universal Protocol (a standardized surgical safety checklist).<br /></br>• The vignette then states, “The procedure is completed without complications.” The stem asks what is “most likely increased … as a result of the pre-procedure actions.”  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>• The time-out’s primary purpose is to prevent wrong-patient, wrong-procedure, and wrong-site errors.<br /></br>• When a process is carried out the same way every time and prevents variation-related mistakes, the system’s reliability (the probability that the desired outcome occurs each time) increases.<br /></br>• Efficiency (speed, resource use) may actually decrease slightly during the extra pause. Patient satisfaction and patient-centeredness can improve, but the single attribute most directly targeted and consistently improved by safety checklists is reliability of the care process.<br /></br>• Core takeaway: Checklists and standardized protocols are classic quality-improvement tools to boost reliability—doing the right thing the right way every time.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review (applied to systems-based practice)<br /></br>• Wrong-site surgery is a “never event” caused by latent system failures (communication gaps, labeling errors).<br /></br>• Redesigning workflow with forcing functions (time-out) removes reliance on individual memory/heroics and places it on a robust system, thereby increasing reliability.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Not applicable—this is a systems-based safety intervention.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Time-OUT boosts Reliability IN.” Picture a light switch: flip it “on” (time-out) and the reliability light stays on for every case.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Efficiency<br /></br>  – Adding a pause takes extra seconds and personnel attention; studies show neutral or slightly decreased efficiency, not improvement.<br /></br>b. Patient satisfaction<br /></br>  – Patients rarely witness the time-out (done after sedation or in the OR) so any satisfaction bump is minimal and indirect.<br /></br>c. Patient-centered care<br /></br>  – A time-out is clinician-focused (checking orders, marking site). Patient-centeredness involves shared decision-making, respecting preferences, etc. It can coincide with a time-out but is not its primary outcome.<br /></br>d. Reliability (Correct)<br /></br>  – Directly addresses consistency and error-free performance, the primary measurable outcome of surgical safety checklists.</p>
65. Immediately after extubation following a total thyroidectomy, a 72-year-old woman has stridor and shortness of breath. She has a history of transient ischemic attacks and coronary artery disease. Her only medication is aspirin. She appears to be in distress and is gasping for air. Her pulse is 100/min, respirations are 35/min, and blood pressure is 170/100 mm Hg. Examination shows an intact surgical incision and no ecchymosis. Breath sounds are decreased bilaterally. Arterial blood gas analysis on 40% oxygen by face mask shows:</br>pH 7.3</br>Pco2 60 mm Hg</br>Po2 200 mm Hg</br>O2 saturation 99%</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Cerebral infarction</br>b. Extrinsic tracheal compression</br>c. Laryngeal nerve paralysis</br>d. Pulmonary embolism</br>e. Tension pneumothorax</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>c. Laryngeal nerve paralysis (bilateral recurrent laryngeal-nerve injury)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Immediately after extubation … stridor and shortness of breath” → acute upper-airway obstruction is the classic scenario tested after neck surgery.<br /></br>• Total thyroidectomy is high-risk for bilateral recurrent laryngeal-nerve injury because the nerve courses in the tracheoesophageal groove immediately posterior to the thyroid.<br /></br>• Incision intact, “no ecchymosis” → argues against a neck hematoma (extrinsic tracheal compression).<br /></br>• Breath sounds “decreased bilaterally” + inspiratory stridor → suggests a single central (laryngeal) obstruction, not unilateral lung pathology.<br /></br>• ABG on 40 % FiO₂: pH 7.30 (acidemia) / PaCO₂ 60 mm Hg (hypercapnia) / PaO₂ 200 mm Hg (excellent oxygenation because high FiO₂). Pattern = pure hypoventilation without V/Q mismatch, typical for upper-airway obstruction rather than lung disease.  </p></br><p>Key pertinent positives: recent thyroid surgery, immediate post-extubation stridor, hypercapnic respiratory acidosis.<br /></br>Key pertinent negatives: no neck swelling, no unilateral breath-sound loss, no hypoxemia.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify obstruction site: Stridor = larynx/trachea (extrathoracic).<br /></br>2. Post-thyroidectomy differential:<br /></br>   a) Expanding hematoma → visible swelling/ecchymosis.<br /></br>   b) Bilateral recurrent laryngeal-nerve injury → vocal-cord paralysis.<br /></br>   c) Laryngeal edema.<br /></br>3. Physical exam &amp; ABG make hematoma unlikely, and nerve injury is a well-known, tested complication.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Bilateral recurrent laryngeal-nerve injury produces immediate airway compromise and CO₂ retention despite adequate oxygenation on supplemental O₂.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “Acute respiratory distress with inspiratory stridor after thyroidectomy—suspect bilateral RLN injury; patient emergently re-intubated and ENT consulted for possible tracheostomy.”</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Recurrent laryngeal nerve (branch of CN X) innervates all intrinsic laryngeal muscles except cricothyroid.<br /></br>• Bilateral injury leaves both vocal cords in the paramedian (midline) position → narrow glottic opening → turbulent inspiratory airflow (stridor) and insufficient minute ventilation → hypercapnia and respiratory acidosis.<br /></br>• Unilateral injury causes hoarseness only; bilateral causes life-threatening obstruction.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostics / Management<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Re-intubate to bypass the glottic obstruction.<br /></br>• If unable, perform emergent surgical airway (tracheostomy).<br /></br>Definitive:<br /></br>• Laryngoscopy to confirm immobile cords.<br /></br>• Long-term tracheostomy or cord lateralization if function does not recover.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“Two cut cords → two problems: Stridor &amp; CO₂.”<br /></br>Visual: think of piano keys (vocal cords) stuck together in the middle—no air passes.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>a. Cerebral infarction – Would present with focal neurologic deficits, not stridor; ABG usually shows normal or low PaCO₂ (hyperventilation due to distress), not hypercapnia.<br /></br>b. Extrinsic tracheal compression (neck hematoma) – Should see tense swelling or bruising at the incision site; rapid airway collapse with neck pressure, not present here.<br /></br>d. Pulmonary embolism – Produces hypoxemia (low PaO₂) with low/normal PaCO₂ (tachypnea); no stridor. Breath sounds typically normal.<br /></br>e. Tension pneumothorax – Unilateral absent breath sounds, tracheal deviation, hypotension; causes hypoxemia, not isolated hypercapnia, and does not create stridor.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In a patient who develops immediate stridor and hypercapnic respiratory acidosis after thyroidectomy, think bilateral recurrent laryngeal-nerve paralysis leading to laryngeal airway obstruction. Emergent airway control is mandatory.</p>
66. A 46-year-old man with hypertension comes to the physician for a routine examination. When the physician enters the room, the patient says the office staff were talking about him in the waiting room. The patient has declined antihypertensive therapy in the past; today, he declines it again, saying “it does not matter because the world is going to end soon.” He does not drink alcohol or use illicit drugs. He lives alone and has minimal contact with his family. He says he has one friend and spends his free time playing video games. He works at home for an accounting firm. He appears fearful and older than his stated age. He wears an overcoat and woolen hat although the outside temperature is 80°F. His pulse is 80/min and regular, and blood pressure is 140/98 mm Hg. Limited physical examination shows no abnormalities. On mental status examination, he has a euthymic mood and odd affect. He makes minimal eye contact with the physician and appears anxious when questioned. He says he has not had anxiety or changes in mood. Cognition is intact, and answers to questions are appropriate. His thought process is linear. He reports no hallucinations. Results of laboratory studies are within the reference ranges. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Autism spectrum disorder</br>b. Delusional disorder</br>c. Oppositional defiant disorder</br>d. Schizophrenia</br>e. Schizotypal personality disorder</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>e. Schizotypal personality disorder  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li>Long-standing social isolation<br /></br>   • “Lives alone … minimal contact with family … one friend.”  </li></br><li>Odd or eccentric behavior/appearance<br /></br>   • Heavy overcoat and wool hat on an 80 °F day.<br /></br>   • Minimal eye contact, anxious, “odd affect.”  </li></br><li>Cognitive/perceptual distortions &amp; suspiciousness<br /></br>   • “The office staff were talking about him.”<br /></br>   • “The world is going to end soon.” (odd, quasi-delusional belief but not frankly psychotic)  </li></br><li>Absence of true psychosis or major mood disorder<br /></br>   • No hallucinations, organized thought process, euthymic mood, intact cognition.  </li></br><li>Adult patient; symptoms are pervasive and stable (personality-level, not episodic).</li></br></ol></br><p>The above cluster fits DSM-5 criteria for Schizotypal Personality Disorder (STPD): persistent social deficits, eccentric behavior, and cognitive/perceptual distortions that fall short of schizophrenia.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Is there a primary psychotic disorder?<br /></br>   • Needs ≥2 of: hallucinations, delusions, disorganized speech, disorganized behavior, negative symptoms. Only mild suspiciousness/odd belief present → NO.  </li></br><li>Is there a single, well-encapsulated delusion with otherwise normal functioning?<br /></br>   • Suspiciousness about staff + apocalyptic idea is not single, fixed, nor clearly non-bizarre → NO.  </li></br><li>Lifelong pattern beginning by early adulthood of eccentricity &amp; social anxiety?<br /></br>   • YES → personality disorder cluster A.  </li></br><li>Among cluster A (paranoid, schizoid, schizotypal) which best matches:<br /></br>   • Paranoid PD: suspicious but not overtly odd/eccentric.<br /></br>   • Schizoid PD: detachment without odd beliefs.<br /></br>   • Schizotypal PD: BOTH detachment and odd beliefs/appearance → Correct.</li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Lifelong eccentric behavior + social deficits + unusual beliefs WITHOUT frank psychosis = Schizotypal Personality Disorder.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: record suspicious thoughts, eccentric dress, social isolation, absence of hallucinations or formal thought disorder, and offer follow-up/psychotherapy.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Considered part of schizophrenia spectrum; shares genetic risk with schizophrenia but lacks sustained psychosis.<br /></br>• Deficits in social cognition, abnormal salience of internal thoughts → “ideas of reference,” magical thinking, mild paranoia.<br /></br>• Often progresses to schizophrenia in a minority (~10–25%).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Approach  </p></br><ol></br><li>First-line:<br /></br>   • Psychotherapy (supportive, social skills training, CBT).  </li></br><li>Medications:<br /></br>   • Low-dose atypical antipsychotics (e.g., risperidone, olanzapine) if transient psychotic episodes or intense anxiety.  </li></br><li>Screen for comorbid depression or substance use.  </li></br><li>No imaging/labs required once medical causes ruled out (normal basic labs already provided).</li></br></ol></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Mild Schizo” mnemonic for Schizotypal:<br /></br>• Social anxiety<br /></br>• Hallucinations absent (but illusions possible)<br /></br>• Ideas of reference<br /></br>• Magical thinking<br /></br>• Odd appearance<br /></br>• Impaired friends (few)<br /></br>• Laughable clothing (eccentric)<br /></br>• Detachment</p></br><p>Remember: Paranoid = suspicious, Schizoid = solitary, Schizotypal = solitary + strange.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Options Are Wrong  </p></br><p>a. Autism spectrum disorder<br /></br>   • Onset in early childhood, persistent deficits in social communication &amp; restricted/repetitive behaviors. Adult would report lifelong communication problems, not new-onset “world ending” ideas or paranoid thoughts. Eccentric dress less typical.</p></br><p>b. Delusional disorder<br /></br>   • Requires ≥1 month of one or more non-bizarre, fixed delusions with otherwise intact functioning and no odd behavior. Patient has multiple vague suspicious thoughts, eccentricity, and social deficits → broader pattern of dysfunction inconsistent with isolated delusion.</p></br><p>c. Oppositional defiant disorder<br /></br>   • Childhood disorder (≤18 y) marked by angry/irritable mood, argumentative/defiant behavior toward authority. Age 46 and no defiance → ruled out.</p></br><p>d. Schizophrenia<br /></br>   • Needs ≥6 mo of psychosis with at least two core symptoms (e.g., hallucinations, disorganized speech). He lacks positive symptoms and negative symptoms are mild. Therefore does not meet criteria.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway  </p></br><p>Cluster A personality disorders sit on a continuum with schizophrenia. In an adult with lifelong social isolation plus eccentric appearance and quasi-psychotic ideas—yet NO sustained hallucinations or disorganized thought—think Schizotypal Personality Disorder.</p>
67. A 4-year-old girl is brought to the office by her mother because of a 1-week history of vaginal itching. The mother says her daughter is scratching her vaginal area frequently and the area appears irritated. The patient has not had fever, pain with urination, or urinary frequency. She has been toilet-trained for 1 year. The mother is not concerned about sexual abuse. When questioned alone, the patient says that no one has touched her inappropriately. Examination shows erythema of the vaginal area. There is no vaginal discharge, foul odor, bleeding, tears, or ecchymoses. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Begin antibiotic therapy</br>b. Begin antifungal therapy</br>c. Order evaluation by child protective services</br>d. Recommend that the patient switch to cotton underwear and avoid bubble baths</br>e. Schedule gynecologic examination</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Recommend that the patient switch to cotton underwear and avoid bubble baths  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• 4-year-old (prepubertal, low estrogen → very thin, easily irritated vulvar mucosa).<br /></br>• 1-week of “itching,” no dysuria, no frequency, no fever → suggests local irritation rather than UTI or systemic infection.<br /></br>• Exam: erythematous vulva only; NO discharge, odor, bleeding, tears, ecchymoses → argues against candidiasis, bacterial vaginitis, or trauma/abuse.<br /></br>• Mother denies abuse concern; child interviewed alone states no inappropriate touching.  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: pruritus + erythema.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: discharge, malodor, trauma, systemic signs.<br /></br>Pattern: classic nonspecific (irritant) vulvovaginitis of young girls.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning  </p></br><p>Step-wise thought process:<br /></br>1. Prepubertal vulva is hypo-estrogenized → thin epithelium; perineum often exposed to irritants (soaps, bubble baths, tight under-wear).<br /></br>2. Absence of discharge or systemic symptoms makes infectious etiologies unlikely.<br /></br>3. First-line management of nonspecific vulvovaginitis = eliminate irritants &amp; improve hygiene:<br /></br>   • cotton underwear (breathable, non-occlusive)<br /></br>   • avoid bubble baths/perfumed soaps<br /></br>   • gentle cleansing, possible sitz baths<br /></br>4. No antibiotic, antifungal, or invasive evaluation needed unless symptoms persist or new findings emerge.  </p></br><p>Take-home: In a healthy, prepubertal girl with isolated pruritus/erythema and no infectious or abuse signs, start with conservative environmental changes.  </p></br><p>Documentation points if real patient: history of bathing products, clothing, wiping technique; absence of trauma; counseling on hygiene; plan for follow-up.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Low estrogen → thin vulvar / vaginal mucosa + minimal labial fat = susceptible to chemical/mechanical irritation.<br /></br>• Common irritants: bubble baths, fragranced wipes/soaps, tight nylon underwear, prolonged dampness in diapers/swimsuits.<br /></br>• Chronic scratching perpetuates inflammation → erythema &amp; itching cycle.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics  </p></br><p>First-line (conservative):<br /></br>• Switch to loose, 100 % cotton underwear.<br /></br>• Stop bubble baths; use mild, unscented soap only on hair/skin, not directly on vulva.<br /></br>• Teach front-to-back wiping, daily warm sitz bath without additives.<br /></br>Second-line / if refractory (&gt;2 wk or new findings):<br /></br>• Consider focused cultures, topical mupirocin if GAS isolated, or topical antifungal if candidal discharge appears.<br /></br>• Referral to pediatric gynecology only if trauma, masses, or persistent unexplained bleeding/pain.  </p></br><p>No routine labs or imaging necessary at this stage.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“Irritated LITTLE LADY” mnemonic for prepubertal vulvovaginitis:<br /></br>• L = Low estrogen<br /></br>• I = Irritants (bubble baths, scented wipes)<br /></br>• T = Tight synthetic clothing<br /></br>• T = Toilet-training wiping issues<br /></br>• L = Loose cotton underwear fixes it<br /></br>• E = Eliminate perfumed exposures  </p></br><p>Visualize a little girl happily switching from nylon to soft cotton shorts and the itch disappearing; ties environment to symptom resolution.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Begin antibiotic therapy<br /></br>   • Bacterial vulvovaginitis (eg, Group A Strep) presents with thin, serosanguinous or purulent discharge, foul odor, possible perineal pain. None are present. Empiric antibiotics risk unnecessary adverse effects and resistance.  </p></br><p>b. Begin antifungal therapy<br /></br>   • Vulvovaginal candidiasis is rare pre-menarche; would show thick cottage-cheese discharge, significant vulvar edema. Absent here.  </p></br><p>c. Order evaluation by child protective services<br /></br>   • Sexual abuse suspicion requires physical trauma (lacerations, bruising), myriad infections, behavioral changes, or inconsistent history. Vignette explicitly lacks these red-flag features and both child and parent deny inappropriate contact.  </p></br><p>e. Schedule gynecologic examination<br /></br>   • Invasive speculum or colposcopic exam unnecessary unless abuse suspected or symptoms persist despite conservative care.  </p></br><p>––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––––<br /></br>Bottom Line: Most prepubertal vulvar itching without discharge is due to simple irritant vulvovaginitis; fix the environment first—cotton underwear and no bubble baths.</p>
68. An 87-year-old man comes to the physician because of a 2-day history of visual changes in his right eye. He notices that the lines of his daily crossword puzzle look curved, and the blinds in his apartment appear wavy. He wears magnifying lenses for reading. There is no pain in his eye, no photophobia, and no history of trauma to the area. He had cataract removal from the right eye 5 years ago. There is no history of serious illness. On examination, visual acuity is 20/200 in his right eye and 20/100 in his left eye. The right pupil is irregular, but it is reactive to light. The left pupil is round and reactive. This examination finding is unchanged since his cataract operation. Palpation of both globes through closed eyelids shows no abnormalities. His lenses appear clear. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Dilated funduscopic examination</br>b. Slit-lamp examination</br>c. Visual field testing</br>d. CT scan of the head</br>e. Temporal artery biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Dilated funduscopic examination  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Straight lines (crossword puzzle, window blinds) look curved or wavy” = metamorphopsia → classic for macular pathology, especially age-related macular degeneration (AMD).<br /></br>• Rapid (2-day) change, painless, elderly patient → suggests conversion from dry to wet (neovascular) AMD with subretinal fluid/hemorrhage.<br /></br>• Pupils reactive, clear lens, normal IOP on palpation → rules out acute angle-closure, cataract, lens dislocation, and severe glaucoma attack.<br /></br>• No systemic symptoms (headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication) → makes giant-cell arteritis unlikely.  </p></br><p>Key positives: sudden central visual distortion, age 87.<br /></br>Key negatives: no pain, no photophobia, no trauma, clear lens.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Localize the problem → central retina (macula).<br /></br>Step 2: Determine urgency → could be wet AMD; vision-saving therapy (intravitreal anti-VEGF) is time-sensitive.<br /></br>Step 3: First diagnostic action → directly look at the macula for drusen, subretinal hemorrhage, gray-green neovascular membrane, or fluid. This requires a dilated funduscopic exam in the office today.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any new metamorphopsia or rapid central vision change in an elderly patient mandates immediate dilated funduscopy to evaluate for wet AMD.  </p></br><p>Documentation: note visual acuity, presence/absence of drusen, subretinal hemorrhage, pigment epithelial detachment, and plan for urgent retinal referral.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Dry (non-exudative) AMD: drusen (yellow extracellular deposits) and RPE (retinal pigment epithelium) atrophy → gradual central vision loss.<br /></br>Wet (exudative) AMD: choroidal neovascularization grows through Bruch membrane → leakage and hemorrhage → acute metamorphopsia and vision loss.  </p></br><p>Risk factors: age &gt;65, smoking, HTN, family history, Caucasian race.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Pearls<br /></br>Diagnostic<br /></br>• Immediate: Dilated funduscopic exam ± Amsler grid in clinic.<br /></br>• Confirmatory/eye-specialist: Optical coherence tomography (OCT) or fluorescein angiography to delineate neovascular membrane.  </p></br><p>Treatment<br /></br>• Wet AMD: intravitreal anti-VEGF agents (ranibizumab, aflibercept, bevacizumab) ASAP; +/- photodynamic therapy.<br /></br>• Dry AMD: lifestyle (smoking cessation), AREDS2 vitamin/mineral supplementation.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“M-etamorphopsia → M-acular (AMD)”<br /></br>“Wet AMD makes straight lines W<em>A</em>V*Y” (WET → WAVY).<br /></br>First thought = “Dilate and look!”  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>B. Slit-lamp examination: Evaluates anterior segment (cornea, lens, anterior chamber). The lens is already described as clear; metamorphopsia is a retinal, not anterior-segment, symptom.<br /></br>C. Visual field testing: Screens for peripheral or focal field defects (e.g., glaucoma, optic nerve lesions). AMD affects central vision; visual field testing does not visualize the retina.<br /></br>D. CT scan of the head: Used for intracranial processes (stroke, hemorrhage). Sudden painless central distortion without neurologic signs is ocular, not cerebral.<br /></br>E. Temporal artery biopsy: Diagnostic for giant-cell arteritis, which typically presents with monocular loss, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, elevated ESR. None are present here.  </p></br><p>──────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>New onset metamorphopsia in an elderly patient = suspect wet AMD → perform immediate dilated funduscopic examination to confirm and expedite sight-saving therapy.</p>
69. A 27-year-old woman comes to the office for a health maintenance examination. She says she feels well. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination shows no lymphadenopathy. A 2-cm nodule is palpated in the left lobe of the thyroid gland. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Results of laboratory studies, including measurement of serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration, are within the reference ranges. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. 131I scintigraphy</br>b. Levothyroxine therapy and reexamination in 6 weeks</br>c. Reexamination of the thyroid gland in 6 months</br>d. Repeat measurement of serum thyroid-stimulating hormone concentration in 6 weeks</br>e. Ultrasonography-guided fine-needle aspiration biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Ultrasonography-guided fine-needle aspiration (FNA) biopsy  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key findings: 27-year-old woman, feels well, single 2-cm thyroid nodule, no lymphadenopathy, normal TSH and other labs.<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: Solitary palpable nodule ≥1 cm.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No hyper- or hypothyroid symptoms; TSH not suppressed; no compressive symptoms; no suspicious lymph nodes.<br /></br>• Pattern: “Euthyroid patient + solitary thyroid nodule ≥1 cm.” ATA and AACE guidelines recommend tissue diagnosis with ultrasound-guided FNA (or at least a dedicated diagnostic ultrasound first, followed by FNA for nodules &gt;1 cm).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Evaluate thyroid function.  → TSH normal (euthyroid).<br /></br>2. Determine need for radionuclide scan.  → Only indicated if TSH is low (to look for toxic “hot” nodule).<br /></br>3. Nodule sizing/characterization.  → ≥1 cm requires cytology because malignancy risk can be 5-15 %.<br /></br>4. Safest, most accurate way to obtain cytology.  → US-guided FNA.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a euthyroid patient, ANY solid thyroid nodule ≥1 cm (or ≥1.5 cm if purely cystic) warrants ultrasound-guided FNA to rule out carcinoma.  </p></br><p>What to document: size, location, consistency of nodule; TSH/result; counseling regarding need for FNA; consent obtained.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Most thyroid nodules are benign (colloid or hyperplastic nodule), but 5-15 % harbor malignancy (papillary &gt; follicular &gt; medullary, anaplastic).<br /></br>• Normal TSH implies nodule is not autonomously functioning (“cold” on scan); “cold” nodules have higher malignancy risk and need cytology.<br /></br>• FNA differentiates benign colloid/cystic nodules from malignant ones with 90-95 % accuracy, guiding definitive therapy (lobectomy vs observation).  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Relevant Diagnostics &amp; Treatment<br /></br>First-line diagnostic:<br /></br>• High-resolution neck ultrasound (size, composition, calcifications, vascularity).<br /></br>• Immediate US-guided FNA for:<br /></br>  – Solid hypoechoic nodule ≥1 cm ± suspicious US features (irregular margins, micro-calcifications, taller-than-wide).<br /></br>  – Any nodule ≥2 cm, even if low-risk pattern.<br /></br>Post-FNA management:<br /></br>• Benign cytology → repeat US in 12–24 mo.<br /></br>• Suspicious/malignant cytology → surgery (lobectomy vs total thyroidectomy depending on type/size/extra-thyroidal extension).<br /></br>• Non-diagnostic → repeat FNA.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Nodule game plan = 3 T’s”:<br /></br>1. TSH first.  (Low TSH → nuclear scan)<br /></br>2. Transducer (ultrasound).<br /></br>3. Tissue (US-FNA if ≥1 cm solid or ≥2 cm cystic).  </p></br><p>Visual mnemonic: Picture a “1-cm ruler” attached to an FNA needle—if the nodule is at least as tall as the ruler and TSH is NOT low, stick it!  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 131I scintigraphy<br /></br>• Appropriate only when TSH is suppressed (possible toxic adenoma) to identify a “hot” nodule. Normal TSH makes a non-functioning (“cold”) lesion more likely; scan would not alter management.  </p></br><p>b. Levothyroxine therapy with re-examination in 6 wk<br /></br>• Suppressive therapy is no longer recommended; little size reduction, risk of iatrogenic hyperthyroidism, and malignancy cannot be ruled out without cytology.  </p></br><p>c. Re-examination of the thyroid in 6 mo<br /></br>• Delays diagnosis; any solid nodule ≥1 cm in a euthyroid patient needs immediate evaluation, not watchful waiting.  </p></br><p>d. Repeat TSH in 6 wk<br /></br>• Her current euthyroid status is established; repeating TSH provides no new information about malignancy risk.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Euthyroid patient + solitary thyroid nodule ≥1 cm = proceed directly to ultrasound-guided fine-needle aspiration to establish cytologic diagnosis and rule out carcinoma.</p>
70. A 17-year-old boy is brought to the emergency department by his mother because of a 20-minute episode of severe chest pain and sweating that started 1 hour ago while he was watching television. On arrival, he is alert and repeatedly expresses concern that something is “very wrong.” He reports that during the episode, his heart was “pounding,” he felt short of breath, and he was sure he was having a heart attack and was going to die. One month ago, he had a similar episode that occurred in the evening 30 minutes after his high school football practice. At that time, results of laboratory studies and an ECG obtained in the emergency department showed no abnormalities. After the initial episode, he quit the football team, and he has continued to limit his physical activity because he is afraid of bringing on another episode. He reports no insomnia or changes in appetite. His mother states that he was diagnosed with an innocent heart murmur at the age of 6 months, but it has not been noted on examinations since the age of 1 year. His paternal grandfather died of a myocardial infarction 3 years ago at the age of 68 years. The patient takes no medications. He does not smoke cigarettes, drink alcohol, or use illicit drugs. He performs well in school. He is 180 cm (5 ft 11 in) tall and weighs 73 kg (161 lb); BMI is 23 kg/m2. His temperature is 37.0°C (98.6°F), and blood pressure is 130/83 mm Hg. No murmurs are heard on cardiac examination. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. ECG is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: G</br></br>a. Acute stress disorder</br>b. Delusional disorder</br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder</br>d. Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy</br>e. Idiopathic recurrent pericarditis</br>f. Panic disorder</br>g. Paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardia</br></br></br><p>Correct answer: Panic disorder (choice f)</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Sudden, severe episode with ≥4 classic autonomic/psychologic symptoms of a panic attack: chest pain, palpitations (“heart pounding”), sweating, shortness of breath, fear of dying.<br /></br>• Episodes are UNEXPECTED (watching TV, post-practice downtime) – there is no consistent trigger.<br /></br>• Recurrent: second attack occurs 1 month after the first.<br /></br>• Maladaptive behavioral change ≥1 month: quit the football team, avoids exertion because he “is afraid of bringing on another episode.”<br /></br>• Normal physical exam and ECG – rules out organic cardiac disease.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: recurrent, unexpected panic attacks + persistent concern/behavior change for ≥1 mo = panic disorder (DSM-5).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify the panic attack (abrupt surge of intense fear &amp; ≥4 symptoms).<br /></br>2. Look for recurrence.<br /></br>3. Check for ≥1 month of worry or avoidance.<br /></br>4. Exclude medical causes (normal vitals, exam, ECG).<br /></br>Core takeaway: On exams, teenager or young adult with “heart attack–like” episodes, normal work-up, and subsequent avoidance = panic disorder.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Dysfunction of loci cerulei &amp; increased noradrenergic activity → sudden sympathetic surge.<br /></br>• Strong genetic component; often first manifests in adolescence-early adulthood.<br /></br>• Without treatment, can lead to agoraphobia, depression, substance use.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Acute: benzodiazepine (e.g., lorazepam) for rapid relief if severe in ED.<br /></br>Long-term first-line:<br /></br>  – SSRI (sertraline, paroxetine, fluoxetine) or SNRI (venlafaxine).<br /></br>  – Cognitive-behavioral therapy (exposure-based).<br /></br>  – Consider adding short-term benzo while SSRI titrated.<br /></br>Medical work-up: baseline ECG, basic labs to rule out thyroid, electrolyte, stimulant use when presentation is chest-pain dominant; once organic disease excluded, focus on psych tx.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“PANIC” = Palpitations, Abdominal distress, Nausea/Numbness, Intense fear of death, Chest pain/Chills/Choking.<br /></br>Panic disorder = “ PANIC attacks Aren’t Needed for Instant Cardiac cath” (i.e., normal cardiac work-up).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Acute stress disorder – requires exposure to a traumatic event within the past month and dissociative symptoms (flashbacks, numbness). None present.<br /></br>b. Delusional disorder – fixed false belief lasting &gt;1 mo without other psychosis. Patient recognizes attacks are episodes, not fixed belief.<br /></br>c. Generalized anxiety disorder – chronic, diffuse worry about multiple domains ≥6 mo plus restlessness, muscle tension, sleep issues; not paroxysmal attacks.<br /></br>d. Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy – would show murmur (systolic crescendo–decrescendo at left sternal border that ↑ with Valsalva/standing); often exertional, abnormal ECG. Exam &amp; ECG normal.<br /></br>e. Idiopathic recurrent pericarditis – positional, pleuritic chest pain; often follows viral infection; ECG would show diffuse ST elevation/PR depression. Absent here.<br /></br>g. Paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardia – causes abrupt palpitations but heart rate usually 150–250 bpm on event ECG; no intense fear/behavior change criteria for panic disorder, and baseline ECG often reveals WPW or accessory pathway changes.</p></br><p>Key differences: Panic disorder = psychogenic episodes + fear of dying + behavioral change + normal studies.</p>
71. A 72-year-old man comes to the physician for a routine health maintenance examination. Abdominal examination shows a nontender, pulsatile mass in the epigastrium. Abdominal ultrasonography shows a 7.2-cm aneurysm. The physician reviews several studies of patients with abdominal aneurysms that are greater than 7 cm in size. The rupture rate is estimated to be 20% per year. Following rupture of an abdominal aneurysm, one half of patients die before reaching a hospital. For patients who do reach a hospital, the perioperative mortality rate is approximately 50%. The mortality rate for patients who undergo elective repair of an aneurysm is 5%. After discussing the risks and benefits of surgical repair of the aneurysm, the patient decides not to undergo an operation. Which of the following best represents this patient's risk for mortality from rupture of the aneurysm during the next year?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. 5%</br>b. 10%</br>c. 15%</br>d. 20%</br>e. 50%</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. 15 %</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• 7.2-cm abdominal aortic aneurysm (AAA) → size &gt;5.5 cm = high rupture risk.<br /></br>• Annual rupture rate for AAAs &gt;7 cm is given as 20 %.<br /></br>• Additional data provided to calculate <em>mortality</em> once rupture occurs:<br /></br>  – 50 % die before reaching the hospital.<br /></br>  – Of the remaining 50 % who make it to the hospital, peri-operative mortality = 50 %.<br /></br>• Patient declines elective repair (elective mortality 5 % would have been his surgical risk). We must now quantify his 1-year <em>death</em> risk if he does nothing.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Calculate probability of rupture in one year<br /></br>   • 20 % (0.20).<br /></br>2. If rupture occurs, calculate probability of death from that rupture<br /></br>   • Pre-hospital death: 50 % (0.50).<br /></br>   • Hospital arrival: 50 % (0.50) × peri-op death 50 % (0.50) = 0.25.<br /></br>   • Total death given rupture = 0.50 + 0.25 = 0.75 (75 %).<br /></br>3. Overall 1-year mortality from aneurysm = rupture probability × death-given-rupture<br /></br>   • 0.20 × 0.75 = 0.15 (15 %).  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Multiply the annual rupture risk by the conditional probability of dying once rupture occurs.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: In real practice you would document the shared-decision discussion outlining 1-year 15 % mortality without surgery versus 5 % elective surgical mortality.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)<br /></br>• AAAs enlarge due to degeneration of tunica media; risk factors include age, male sex, smoking, hypertension.<br /></br>• Rupture risk rises steeply once diameter &gt;5.5 cm (≈8 %/year at 6 cm, &gt;20 %/year when &gt;7 cm).<br /></br>• Elective open or endovascular repair recommended for symptomatic AAAs or asymptomatic AAAs ≥5.5 cm.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Notes<br /></br>• First-line: Elective repair (open or EVAR) for AAAs ≥5.5 cm if patient accepts.<br /></br>• Imaging: Annual US if &lt;4 cm, every 6 mo if 4–5.4 cm; CT/CTA pre-op.<br /></br>• Medical: Strict BP control, smoking cessation, statin, antiplatelet therapy.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“7-cm AAA: 20-75-15 rule”<br /></br>• &gt;7 cm → 20 % rupture /yr.<br /></br>• 75 % of ruptures fatal.<br /></br>• Net annual death risk ≈15 %.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. 5 % – This is the mortality for <em>elective</em> repair, not for observation.<br /></br>b. 10 % – Would correspond to rupture probability 20 % with 50 % fatality; ignores the additional 25 % who die after reaching hospital.<br /></br>d. 20 % – This is the rupture <em>incidence</em>, not mortality. Assumes every rupture is fatal, which is untrue.<br /></br>e. 50 % – Reflects only pre-hospital deaths or peri-op deaths alone; does not integrate both probabilities with rupture incidence.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>With a 7.2-cm AAA left unrepaired, the patient faces approximately a 15 % chance of death within the next year from rupture.</p>
72. A 58-year-old man with an 8-year history of schizoaffective disorder, manic type, is brought to the clinic for a follow-up examination. Two weeks ago, he was hospitalized for a manic episode. Risperidone was added to his medication regimen; the dosage was increased to the maximum recommended prior to discharge. At that time, neurologic examination showed mild cogwheel rigidity. There was no evidence of tremors. Muscle strength was 5/5. He has no other history of serious medical or psychiatric illness. His other medications are valproic acid and clonazepam. Today, examination shows drooling, masked facies, and decreased blinking. He has a slow, shuffling gait with decreased arm swing. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Mental status examination shows normal speech and a mildly anxious mood. There is no evidence of psychosis. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Addition of diphenhydramine to the medication regimen</br>b. Addition of lithium carbonate to the medication regimen</br>c. Cognitive behavioral therapy</br>d. Decreasing the dosage of risperidone</br>e. Increasing the dosage of clonazepam</br>f. Psychotherapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>Addition of diphenhydramine to the medication regimen</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key medication clue: “Risperidone … dosage was increased to the maximum recommended.”<br /></br>• Timeline: Neurologic findings began within 2 weeks of dose escalation.<br /></br>• Cardinal findings today: drooling, masked facies, ↓ blinking, slow shuffling gait, ↓ arm swing, cogwheel rigidity.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no resting tremor, no muscle-strength loss, no psychosis, no other systemic illness.<br /></br>Pattern: An antipsychotic started or dose–escalated → sub-acute onset (days-weeks) of parkinsonian signs = drug-induced parkinsonism, one of the extrapyramidal symptoms (EPS).</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>1. Identify the adverse effect: High-potency antipsychotics (haloperidol, risperidone, fluphenazine) block D2 receptors in the nigrostriatal pathway → cholinergic predominance → parkinsonism.<br /></br>2. First-line treatment for antipsychotic-induced parkinsonism: Add an anticholinergic agent (benztropine or diphenhydramine).<br /></br>   • This relieves rigidity and gait disturbance without sacrificing antipsychotic effect needed for mood stabilization/psychosis.<br /></br>3. Alternative (second-line): Lower the dose or switch to a lower-potency/atypical agent, but this risks relapse of mania/psychosis; therefore not the preferred immediate step when anticholinergic therapy is tolerated.</p></br><p>Take-home concept: Acute dystonia &amp; parkinsonism = “Park the Benz or Benadryl” (benztropine/diphenhydramine).</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record new extrapyramidal symptoms, timed relation to medication change, discussion of risks/benefits, anticholinergic plan, patient education on anticholinergic side-effects.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• D2 blockade in basal ganglia ↓ dopaminergic tone → relative ↑ acetylcholine.<br /></br>• Clinical triad: rigidity, bradykinesia, masked facies (same mechanism as idiopathic Parkinson disease but drug-induced).<br /></br>• Anticholinergics restore dopamine-acetylcholine balance.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate therapy<br /></br>• Diphenhydramine 25–50 mg IV or PO PRN OR Benztropine 1–2 mg PO/IM.<br /></br>• Monitor for anticholinergic ADR (dry mouth, constipation, urinary retention, confusion—especially in elderly).<br /></br>No additional labs/imaging needed; diagnosis is clinical.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Addition of lithium carbonate – Unnecessary; patient already mood-stabilized on valproate, lithium affords no EPS benefit and raises toxicity risk.<br /></br>c. Cognitive-behavioral therapy – Helpful adjunct for mood disorders but does not treat EPS.<br /></br>d. Decreasing the dosage of risperidone – Could help but sacrifices antipsychotic coverage; guideline-preferred first step is anticholinergic addition unless EPS are severe or anticholinergic contraindicated.<br /></br>e. Increasing clonazepam – Benzodiazepines relieve akathisia, not parkinsonism; would worsen sedation and falls.<br /></br>f. Psychotherapy – Same rationale as (c); no effect on parkinsonism.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “EPS timeline”:<br /></br>  – Hours: Acute dystonia → treat with benztropine/diphenhydramine.<br /></br>  – Days–weeks: Parkinsonism → benztropine/diphenhydramine.<br /></br>  – Weeks–months: Akathisia → β-blocker (propranolol) or benzodiazepine.<br /></br>  – Months–years: Tardive dyskinesia → stop/switch antipsychotic, consider valbenazine/deutetrabenazine.<br /></br>• Mnemonic: ADAPT (Acute Dystonia, Akathisia, Parkinsonism, Tardive).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Takeaway<br /></br>Drug-induced parkinsonism from dopamine blockade is best managed acutely with an anticholinergic agent such as diphenhydramine—relieving symptoms while maintaining necessary antipsychotic therapy.</p>
73. A 67-year-old man with metastatic lung cancer comes to the physician because of malaise and lethargy during the past 3 days. Medications are ibuprofen and lisinopril. He appears cachectic but is oriented to person, place, and time. His temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 102/min, respirations are 13/min, and blood pressure is 105/75 mm Hg. Examination shows decreased skin turgor. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 10.6 g/dL</br>Hematocrit 36%</br>Leukocyte count 11,100/mm3</br>Platelet count 226,000/mm3</br>Serum:</br>Na+ 136 mEq/L</br>K+ 3.6 mEq/L</br>Cl- 96 mEq/L</br>HCO3- 28 mEq/L</br>Ca2+ 11.8 mg/dL</br>Urea nitrogen 22 mg/dL</br>Glucose 98 mg/dL</br>Creatinine 0.9 mg/dL</br>Phosphorus 2.5 mg/dL</br>Albumin 2.9 g/dL</br>Which of the following is the most appropriate initial pharmacotherapy?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Calcitonin</br>b. Hydrocortisone</br>c. Plicamycin</br>d. 0.9% Saline</br>e. Zoledronic acid</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Calcitonin  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Cancer history – Metastatic lung cancer is a classic cause of humoral hyper-calcemia of malignancy (PTH-rP secretion or osteolytic bone mets).<br /></br>• Symptoms – New-onset malaise, lethargy, tachycardia, mild hypotension, ↓ skin turgor → all compatible with symptomatic hypercalcemia + dehydration.<br /></br>• Laboratory clues –<br /></br>  – Measured Ca²⁺ 11.8 mg/dL with hypoalbuminemia (Alb 2.9 g/dL).<br /></br>  – Corrected Ca²⁺ = 11.8 + 0.8 × (4 – 2.9) ≈ 12.7 mg/dL (moderate hypercalcemia).<br /></br>  – BUN/Cr = 22/0.9 → prerenal azotemia from volume depletion, not renal failure.  </p></br><p>Key pattern: “Malignancy + symptomatic moderate-to-severe hypercalcemia that needs rapid lowering.”  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach to hypercalcemia of malignancy:<br /></br>1.  Volume repletion with isotonic saline to correct dehydration &amp; enhance renal Ca²⁺ excretion (supportive, NOT pharmacologic).<br /></br>2.  Rapid pharmacologic Ca²⁺ lowering while waiting for long-acting therapy → Salmon calcitonin (onset 4–6 h, peaks ~12 h).<br /></br>3.  Durable control → IV bisphosphonate (eg, zoledronic acid) or denosumab (onset 2–4 d).  </p></br><p>Because the stem explicitly asks for “initial pharmacotherapy,” we choose the agent that produces the fastest drug-mediated fall in serum calcium: calcitonin.  </p></br><p>Take-home:<br /></br>• Calcitonin = fastest drug for hypercalcemia (hours).<br /></br>• Bisphosphonate = definitive but slow (days).<br /></br>• Isotonic saline = essential first supportive step but considered fluid management rather than pharmacotherapy.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record corrected calcium level, mental status, volume status, and orders for 0.9 % saline, calcitonin dosing (4 IU/kg IM/SC q12 h), and plan for bisphosphonate once euvolemic.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Humoral hypercalcemia of malignancy (HHM)<br /></br>• Etiology: PTH-related peptide secretion (SCC lung, renal, bladder, breast).<br /></br>• Mechanism: ↑ bone resorption, ↓ renal Ca²⁺ excretion, suppressed native PTH.<br /></br>• Clinical: Polyuria, polydipsia, lethargy, altered sensorium, dehydration, constipation.  </p></br><p>Calcitonin<br /></br>• Hormone from thyroid parafollicular (C) cells.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Direct inhibition of osteoclastic bone resorption + ↑ renal Ca²⁺ excretion.<br /></br>• Onset: 4–6 h; Tachyphylaxis after 48–72 h → must pair with bisphosphonate.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Highlights<br /></br>1. Isotonic saline 200–300 mL/h → maintain urine output 100–150 mL/h.<br /></br>2. Calcitonin 4 IU/kg IM or SC q12 h (may increase to q6 h).<br /></br>3. Zoledronic acid 4 mg IV over ≥15 min after patient is euvolemic.<br /></br>4. Dialysis reserved for Ca²⁺ &gt;18 mg/dL, anuric renal failure, or heart failure preventing hydration.<br /></br>5. Glucocorticoids helpful only for vitamin D–mediated hypercalcemia (eg, sarcoid, lymphoma).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>High-Yield Memory Aids<br /></br>“Calci-TON-IN turns the calcium down IN a hurry.”<br /></br>Order of speed:<br /></br>Saline (supportive) → CalciTONIN (hours) → biS-phosphonate (Slow, days).  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Hydrocortisone – Glucocorticoids lower Ca²⁺ in vitamin D excess (granulomatous disease, lymphoma) by ↓ intestinal absorption. Ineffective and too slow for HHM.<br /></br>c. Plicamycin (mithramycin) – Antineoplastic that lowers Ca²⁺ but high toxicity; rarely used now that safer bisphosphonates exist.<br /></br>d. 0.9 % Saline – Essential, but the stem asks for “pharmacotherapy.” Saline is supportive fluid therapy, not a drug that directly lowers Ca²⁺.<br /></br>e. Zoledronic acid – Definitive therapy for HHM but onset is 48–72 h, so not the best INITIAL agent when rapid reduction is needed.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Core Concept<br /></br>For symptomatic or moderate-to-severe hypercalcemia of malignancy, begin isotonic saline for volume repletion and give calcitonin for immediate pharmacologic calcium lowering while arranging definitive treatment with a bisphosphonate.</p>
74. A 47-year-old man is brought to the emergency department because of severe left groin pain since he fell 2 hours ago. He is unable to walk because of the pain. He does not have pain in other joints. He has a 20-year history of ulcerative colitis. His only medication is daily prednisone. On examination, any attempts at range of motion of the left hip elicit pain. Muscle strength is intact distally and in the right lower extremity. Distal pulses are 2+. An x-ray of the pelvis and left lower hip shows moderate loss of joint space and diffuse osteopenia. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. DEXA scan</br>c. MRI of the hip</br>d. Nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug therapy</br>e. Aspiration of the hip joint</br>f. Total hip arthroplasty</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Observation only  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Acute event: “severe left-groin pain since he fell 2 hours ago.”<br /></br>• X-ray: “moderate loss of joint space and diffuse osteopenia,” but NO fracture, dislocation, subchondral collapse, or effusion.<br /></br>• Chronic background: 20 y ulcerative colitis on daily prednisone → explains diffuse osteopenia.<br /></br>• Physical exam: pain with any hip motion, but intact distal strength &amp; pulses → neurologic / vascular compromise ruled out.<br /></br>Key pattern: Minor traumatic fall + negative radiograph + pre-existing but previously asymptomatic degenerative/osteopenic changes. Nothing on image or exam requires an urgent diagnostic study or therapeutic intervention.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Rule out emergent causes of hip pain (fracture, dislocation, septic arthritis) → X-ray negative, afebrile, no systemic signs.<br /></br>2. Identify chronic findings (joint-space narrowing, osteopenia) as incidental and not the reason for today's acute pain.<br /></br>3. Decide on immediate management: patient is on chronic glucocorticoids, so NSAIDs markedly increase risk of GI bleeding; surgery is unwarranted; no imaging will change management acutely.<br /></br>4. Best course = expectant management (ice, limited weight bearing, acetaminophen) and re-evaluate if symptoms persist.  </p></br><p>Core Take-away: A negative post-fall radiograph with only chronic changes calls for simple observation—especially when NSAIDs are relatively contraindicated by daily steroid use.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Chronic prednisone decreases osteoblastic activity → osteopenia; also accelerates cartilage loss → joint-space narrowing.<br /></br>• These are slow, painless changes; an acute pain spike after trauma is almost always soft-tissue contusion if imaging shows no fracture.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment / Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>• Rest, ice, protected weight bearing, acetaminophen.<br /></br>• Educate on red-flag symptoms (fever, escalating pain, inability to bear weight after 48 h).<br /></br>Long-term (out-patient):<br /></br>• Bone-health optimization (calcium/vitamin D, bisphosphonate consideration) and GI prophylaxis if NSAIDs ever become necessary.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Negative film after a fall? If nothing acute is seen at all → OBSERVE the call.”<br /></br>Addendum: “Steroid + NSAID = Bleed” → reminds you NSAID choice is risky here.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. DEXA scan – Useful for chronic osteoporosis assessment, not for acute pain management in ED.<br /></br>c. MRI of the hip – Ordered for occult fracture or avascular necrosis suspicion when X-ray is normal but high clinical concern; here X-ray already shows chronic changes explaining negative exam, and no signs point to occult fracture.<br /></br>d. NSAID therapy – First-line for osteoarthritis, but significantly increases GI bleeding risk when combined with systemic steroids.<br /></br>e. Aspiration of the hip joint – Indicated for suspected septic arthritis (fever, leukocytosis, effusion), none of which are present.<br /></br>f. Total hip arthroplasty – Reserved for end-stage, function-limiting arthritis after conservative measures fail; not indicated acutely or after a single painful episode.  </p></br><p>─────────────────────<br /></br>Document in ED Note<br /></br>• Negative imaging for fracture/dislocation.<br /></br>• Discussed conservative care and NSAID avoidance due to chronic prednisone.<br /></br>• Return precautions provided.</p>
75. A 74-year-old woman is brought to the clinic by her family because of a 7-month history of progressive confusion and urinary incontinence. During the past year, she has had multiple falls; she sustained wrist fractures 1 year ago and a left tibia fracture 3 months ago. She has hypertension, osteoporosis, and depression. She is evasive when asked how much alcohol she drinks. She appears frail. Vital signs are within normal limits. Physical examination shows a wide-based gait and marked ataxia. She is unable to attempt the Romberg test. Cranial nerve examination shows nystagmus and an inability to move her eyes horizontally. On cognitive testing, she recalls zero of three objects after 5 minutes. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 10 g/dL</br>Mean corpuscular volume 102 μm3</br>Serum</br>Vitamin B12 (cobalamin) 980 pg/mL (N=160-950)</br>Folate 3.1 ng/mL (N=2.7-17)</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Folic acid deficiency</br>b. Lewy body dementia</br>c. Normal-pressure hydrocephalus</br>d. Progressive supranuclear palsy</br>e. Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Progressive confusion (7 mo) → cognitive dysfunction/dementia component of Korsakoff.<br /></br>• Multiple falls, wide-based gait, marked ataxia → cerebellar/vestibular dysfunction (Wernicke component).<br /></br>• Nystagmus + inability to move eyes horizontally → classic ophthalmoplegia of Wernicke encephalopathy (most often horizontal).<br /></br>• Macrocytic anemia (MCV 102 µm³) with normal-high B12, low-normal folate, &amp; heavy alcohol use clues → chronic alcoholism, which causes thiamine (vitamin B1) deficiency and alcohol-induced macrocytosis.<br /></br>• Evasive about alcohol; frail elderly female → high-risk population.<br /></br>Triad present: 1) Confusion, 2) Ataxia, 3) Ophthalmoplegia → pathognomonic for Wernicke encephalopathy. Chronic memory loss = Korsakoff—together “Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify neurologic triad → points strongly toward Wernicke.<br /></br>2. Ask “What precipitates Wernicke?” → thiamine deficiency, usually alcoholism or malnutrition. Patient meets both.<br /></br>3. Check for competing diagnoses: NPH has gait + cognitive + urinary, but no ophthalmoplegia. Folate deficiency gives macrocytosis but NOT eye signs/ataxia. PSP gives vertical, not horizontal, gaze palsy.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In any patient with confusion + gait ataxia + ocular motor findings—especially with alcoholism—think Wernicke-Korsakoff, give IV thiamine before glucose.<br /></br>Documentation pearl: “Administered IV thiamine 100 mg prior to dextrose; will monitor mental status and ocular signs.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thiamine is a cofactor for pyruvate dehydrogenase, α-ketoglutarate dehydrogenase, transketolase.<br /></br>• Deficiency → impaired cerebral glucose utilization → focal neuronal injury in mammillary bodies, thalamus, periaqueductal gray.<br /></br>• Acute Wernicke (reversible): confusion, ataxia, ophthalmoplegia.<br /></br>• Untreated → chronic Korsakoff (irreversible): anterograde &amp; retrograde amnesia, confabulation due to mammillary-body damage.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics<br /></br>Immediate:<br /></br>1. IV thiamine 100 mg BEFORE glucose (giving glucose first can precipitate/exacerbate encephalopathy).<br /></br>2. Then IV dextrose/fluids, magnesium repletion.<br /></br>3. Encourage alcohol cessation; multivitamin supplementation.<br /></br>Work-up:<br /></br>• CMP, Mg²⁺, liver enzymes, head CT/MRI if focal deficits persist.<br /></br>• Folate &amp; B12 often drawn but are not causative here.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“COAT RACK” mnemonic for Wernicke-Korsakoff:<br /></br>C – Confusion<br /></br>O – Ophthalmoplegia<br /></br>A – Ataxia<br /></br>T – Thiamine deficiency<br /></br>R – Retrograde amnesia<br /></br>A – Anterograde amnesia<br /></br>C – Confabulation<br /></br>K – Korsakoff  </p></br><p>Visualize a coat rack next to a bar; the drunk patient grabs it (ataxia), eyes dart (nystagmus), cannot remember where she left her coat (amnesia).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Folic acid deficiency<br /></br>• Gives macrocytic anemia &amp; can cause mild cognitive changes, but it does NOT cause ophthalmoplegia or pronounced gait ataxia. Neuro signs of folate deficiency are minimal compared with B12 deficiency.  </p></br><p>b. Lewy body dementia<br /></br>• Characterized by fluctuating cognition, vivid visual hallucinations, REM-sleep behavior disorder, and parkinsonism. No eye-movement palsy or macrocytosis.  </p></br><p>c. Normal-pressure hydrocephalus<br /></br>• Triad: gait disturbance (magnetic, not ataxic), urinary incontinence, dementia. Eye findings are absent; imaging would show enlarged ventricles.  </p></br><p>d. Progressive supranuclear palsy<br /></br>• Presents with early POSTURAL falls &amp; vertical (especially downward) gaze palsy, axial rigidity. Horizontal gaze usually spared initially. No macrocytosis or strong alcohol link.  </p></br><p>e. Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome (CORRECT)<br /></br>• Fits full neurologic triad + alcoholism + macrocytosis from chronic alcohol use. Rapid recognition and thiamine administration are essential to prevent permanent memory loss.</p>
76. Eight days after he was involved in a motor vehicle collision in which he was the restrained driver, a hospitalized 28-year-old man has hypertension. He underwent extensive reconstructive orthopaedic operations for injuries to his left clavicle, shoulder, and femur and exploratory laparotomy for a ruptured spleen. Urine toxicology screening on admission was positive for Δ-9-tetrahydrocannabinol, cocaine, and opiates. He was intubated and mechanically ventilated during the procedures and was extubated on hospital day 4. He receives oral acetaminophen-oxycodone as needed. His blood pressure has been 180/110 mm Hg; it decreases to 150/80 mm Hg when he receives his pain medication. An abdominal bandage covers the surgical incision. The nurse tells the physician that the patient rates his pain as a 10 on a 10-point scale and is constantly asking for pain medications. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in pharmacotherapy for this patient’s hypertension?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Continued administration of acetaminophen-oxycodone every 4 hours</br>b. Continuous intravenous labetalol infusion with titration</br>c. Intravenous nitroprusside therapy</br>d. Patient-controlled morphine</br>e. Switching from acetaminophen-oxycodone to tramadol</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>d. Patient-controlled morphine (intravenous PCA)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Post-op day 8 poly‐trauma patient; now persistently hypertensive (≈180/110 mm Hg).<br /></br>• Blood pressure falls 30 mm Hg systolic whenever he receives his opioid.<br /></br>• Rates pain “10/10,” repeatedly requests medication ⇒ obvious undertreated pain.<br /></br>• No other causes of secondary HTN suggested (normal ventilation, no head injury, no renal injury; he is afebrile).  </p></br><p>Pertinent positives: severe pain, clear BP–pain relationship.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives: no evidence of fluid overload, renal failure, withdrawal, or endocrine cause.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: sympathetic surge from acute pain is a very common, reversible cause of postoperative hypertension.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step 1 – Identify etiology of hypertension.<br /></br> • Inadequate analgesia → catecholamine release → tachy-/hypertension.</p></br><p>Step 2 – Correct the cause, not the number.<br /></br> • Give adequate, reliable analgesia instead of adding an antihypertensive drip.</p></br><p>Step 3 – Choose the analgesic strategy that provides continuous, patient-specific titration.<br /></br> • Oral PRN or even scheduled q4 h dosing is still “provider-controlled” and often leaves gaps.<br /></br> • Intravenous patient-controlled analgesia (PCA) with morphine is gold standard for acute, severe postoperative pain: rapid onset, predictable absorption, and the patient can titrate within safety limits.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Post-op hypertension caused by pain should be managed by optimizing analgesia, most effectively with IV PCA opioids.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: must record pain score, current regimen, decision to start PCA, opioid risk discussion, and monitoring plan (respiratory rate, sedation scale).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>Acute pain → ↑ sympathetic outflow → ↑ catecholamines → vasoconstriction + tachycardia → hypertension.<br /></br>Relieve pain → sympathetic tone normalizes → BP falls.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Notes</br>First-line for severe post-surgical pain = IV opioids using PCA pump.<br /></br>• Morphine commonly chosen; others include hydromorphone or fentanyl.<br /></br>• Typical PCA orders: demand dose, lock-out interval, optional basal rate.<br /></br>Monitor: respiratory depression, sedation, constipation (start bowel regimen).</p></br><p>No additional labs/imaging are required; the “test” is clinical (pain score vs BP).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“Pain Pushes Pressure.” If BP drops after an opioid dose, fix the pain, not the pressure medication.</p></br><p>PCA = “Patient Controls Analgesia” → best for severe, fluctuating postoperative pain.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Scheduled acetaminophen-oxycodone q4 h<br /></br>   • Still oral (variable absorption), fixed interval; patient already needs more frequent dosing. Does not provide rapid titration like PCA IV.</p></br><p>b. Continuous IV labetalol<br /></br>   • Treats the number, not the cause; risks masking hypovolemia, bradycardia, bronchospasm.</p></br><p>c. IV nitroprusside<br /></br>   • Potent arterial/venous dilator reserved for hypertensive emergencies (end-organ damage). Over-treatment here; problem is pain.</p></br><p>e. Switch to tramadol<br /></br>   • Weaker µ-agonist + SNRI properties; inferior analgesia for severe post-op pain. Would likely worsen pain-related HTN.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: Focusing on comorbid substance use and under-treating legitimate acute pain. Acute pain management should never be withheld because of remote drug use.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Take-Home Message</br>In postoperative patients, persistent hypertension that reliably improves with opioid dosing is almost always pain-mediated. Provide adequate analgesia—ideally with IV PCA opioids—to resolve both the pain and the sympathetic hypertension.</p>
77. A 37-year-old primigravid woman at 16 weeks’ gestation comes to the physician to discuss the results of amniocentesis. She has an IQ of 70. She is employed as a housekeeping assistant in a hotel and has been married to a coworker for 6 months. She has no family history of serious illness. Karyotype analysis of the fetus showed trisomy 21. Her pregnancy has been otherwise uncomplicated. She is informed of the abnormalities associated with the fetal genotype and still wants to continue the pregnancy. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Schedule the patient for ongoing prenatal care</br>b. Discuss the situation with the patient's husband</br>c. Try to convince the patient to change her decision</br>d. Seek a court-appointed guardian</br>e. Refer the patient for psychiatric evaluation to determine competence</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Schedule the patient for ongoing prenatal care  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 37-year-old adult, IQ 70 (mild intellectual disability), correctly understands the result (trisomy 21), expresses a clear wish to continue pregnancy, no evidence of psychosis, delirium, or coercion.<br /></br>• Pertinent positive: coherent, consistent choice that aligns with her values (“still wants to continue the pregnancy”).<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no confusion, no fluctuating consciousness, no indication she cannot care for herself.  </p></br><p>These points satisfy the 4 elements of decision-making capacity (DMC):<br /></br>1. Communicates a choice<br /></br>2. Understands the relevant information<br /></br>3. Appreciates consequences<br /></br>4. Can reason about options  </p></br><p>Therefore, the physician must respect her autonomous decision and proceed with routine prenatal care.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Confirm DMC → present.<br /></br>2. Patient makes an informed decision → continue pregnancy.<br /></br>3. No further ethical or legal barriers → implement standard prenatal management.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: Decision-making capacity is task-specific, not IQ-specific. Adults with mild intellectual disability often retain DMC for personal health decisions.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: note informed discussion of trisomy 21, confirmation of understanding, patient’s stated preference, and plan for routine prenatal follow-up.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (brief)<br /></br>Trisomy 21 (Down syndrome): meiotic nondisjunction risk rises with maternal age; associated anomalies include cardiac (AV septal defects), GI (duodenal atresia), cognitive impairment, and leukemia risk. The woman’s choice does not alter these facts but guides perinatal planning (e.g., fetal echocardiography, delivery prep).</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Management &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First-line: routine prenatal care plus targeted assessments for Down syndrome pregnancies (level-II anatomy scan, fetal echo). No additional legal or psychiatric intervention is warranted.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“C-U-AR (See You Are) Able” for capacity:<br /></br>C – Communicate choice<br /></br>U – Understand information<br /></br>A – Appreciate consequences<br /></br>R – Reason about options<br /></br>If all four are intact → respect choice.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Discuss with the patient’s husband<br /></br>• Spouse has no legal authority over competent adult medical decisions; breaches confidentiality if done without consent.</p></br><p>c. Try to convince the patient to change her decision<br /></br>• Coercion violates autonomy and informed consent principles.</p></br><p>d. Seek a court-appointed guardian<br /></br>• Guardianship is for individuals who lack DMC; not applicable here.</p></br><p>e. Refer for psychiatric evaluation to determine competence<br /></br>• No evidence of mental illness or impaired DMC; unnecessary referral delays care.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>A competent adult—even with mild intellectual disability—has the right to make informed decisions about her pregnancy. Respect her autonomy and proceed with standard prenatal care.</p>
78. A 29-year-old male US Navy veteran comes to the local Veterans Affairs clinic because of a 2-week history of purulent drainage from a small opening at the site of amputation of his left arm. For the past 10 days, he has had increasing pain, tenderness, and redness at the wound site. Thirteen months ago, the patient sustained a traumatic amputation of the left arm below the elbow when an improvised explosive device detonated while he was on a combat mission in the Middle East. Management at that time consisted of aggressive surgical debridement of the residual limb, which has healed slowly; a small part of the wound appeared to heal incompletely. The patient appears well. Vital signs are within normal limits. Examination of the left upper extremity shows a 3-mm opening in the scar with surrounding erythema; the site is draining purulent material. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following studies is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Bone biopsy of the wound site  </br>b. Complete blood count with differential  </br>c. Erythrocyte sedimentation rate and serum C-reactive protein concentration  </br>d.
