X-ray of the left upper extremity residual limb</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>• Bone biopsy of the wound site (culture &amp; histopathology)</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Key clues that point to chronic osteomyelitis of the residual limb<br /></br>1. Penetrating/traumatic injury with open bone exposure (combat-related blast).<br /></br>2. Persistent “incompletely healed” area 13 months later.<br /></br>3. New 2-week history of purulent drainage through a tiny sinus tract in the scar.<br /></br>4. Local erythema, tenderness, pain; afebrile and otherwise well (classic for indolent, chronic bone infection).<br /></br>5. No systemic signs → suggests localized, smoldering process rather than acute cellulitis or septicemia.</p></br><p>Sinus tract formation that drains pus from a previously injured bone is almost pathognomonic for chronic osteomyelitis.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise thought process<br /></br>1. Identify risk factor (open fracture/amputation) → high pre-test probability of osteomyelitis.<br /></br>2. Recognize chronic timeline + sinus tract → think “chronic osteomyelitis.”<br /></br>3. Ask: what test proves (confirms) the diagnosis?<br /></br>   • Imaging &amp; serologies raise/lowers suspicion.<br /></br>   • Only bone biopsy with culture/histology definitively answers: “Is there infected bone, and with what organism?”</p></br><p>Take-home message: When osteomyelitis is on the table, the gold-standard confirmatory test is a bone biopsy for histology and culture.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl (real patient): note tract location, surrounding tissue viability, prior hardware, biopsy plan, and send specimen for aerobic/anaerobic, fungal, and mycobacterial studies.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (high-yield)<br /></br>• After an open fracture or amputation, bacteria seed devascularized cortical bone → inflammatory granulation tissue forms → sinus tracts can drill through soft tissue to skin.<br /></br>• Chronic osteomyelitis often lacks fever; laboratory markers may be only mildly elevated.<br /></br>• Most common organisms: Staphylococcus aureus (including MRSA), gram-negative rods in war wounds.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Snapshot<br /></br>1. Screening/initial work-up (not confirmatory)<br /></br>   • ESR &amp; CRP (usually ↑) – monitor treatment response.<br /></br>   • Plain XR: lytic lesions, periosteal thickening; insensitive early.<br /></br>   • MRI or tagged WBC bone scan: sensitive for extent but still not definitive.<br /></br>2. Confirmation (gold standard)<br /></br>   • Percutaneous or open bone biopsy with culture.<br /></br>3. Management<br /></br>   • Surgical debridement of necrotic bone ± hardware removal.<br /></br>   • Pathogen-directed IV antibiotics for ≥6 weeks (e.g., IV vancomycin for MRSA until cultures finalize, then tailor).</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“B - for Bone, B - for Biopsy: Biopsy is Best to prove Bone infection.”<br /></br>Another hook: “If the bone is groaning, grab a core.”</p></br><p>───────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Complete blood count with differential<br /></br>   • May show mild leukocytosis or be normal in chronic disease—nonspecific and never confirmatory.</p></br><p>c. ESR and CRP<br /></br>   • Helpful screening tools and for monitoring response, but elevated values only suggest inflammation; normal values do not exclude osteomyelitis.</p></br><p>d. X-ray of the residual limb<br /></br>   • Plain films become positive only after ~2 weeks or &gt;50–70 % bone mineral loss. Findings (sequestrum, involucrum) can support diagnosis but cannot establish it or identify the organism.</p></br><p>Bottom line: Only a bone biopsy provides histologic proof and the microbiologic data necessary for targeted antibiotic therapy.</p>
79. A 2-year-old girl is brought to the physician because of a 2-week history of difficulty walking and intermittent crossing of the eyes. She also has had three to four episodes of watery diarrhea daily during this time. She has not had fever or vomiting. She has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. She is alert but irritable and tearful; she clings to her mother during the examination. She is at the 50th percentile for length, 25th percentile for weight, and 50th percentile for head circumference. Her pulse is 100/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 120/80 mm Hg. The abdomen is soft and nontender; a smooth, firm mass is palpated over the right upper quadrant. Neurologic examination shows rapid, chaotic, multidirectional saccades of the eyes. There are jerking motions of the extremities, which are exacerbated by intentional movement. There is truncal ataxia, and the patient is unable to sit or walk without assistance. Which of the following is the most likely underlying cause of this patient's symptoms?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Hepatoblastoma</br>b. Medulloblastoma</br>c. Nephroblastoma (Wilms tumor)</br>d. Neuroblastoma</br>e. Non-Hodgkin lymphoma</br>f. Rhabdomyosarcoma</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>d. Neuroblastoma  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age: 2-year-old – peak incidence for neuroblastoma is &lt; 5 years.<br /></br>• 2-week history of:<br /></br>  – “Difficulty walking,” “unable to sit or walk without assistance,” “truncal ataxia”<br /></br>  – “Intermittent crossing of the eyes” + “rapid, chaotic, multidirectional saccades” → classic opsoclonus (“dancing eyes”).<br /></br>  – “Jerking motions of the extremities” that worsen with movement → myoclonus (“dancing feet”).<br /></br>• Abdominal finding: “smooth, firm mass” in the right upper quadrant → retro-peritoneal/adrenal mass.<br /></br>• Autonomic symptom: watery diarrhea (paraneoplastic VIP or catecholamine metabolites).  </p></br><p>Put together: opsoclonus-myoclonus-ataxia syndrome (OMAS) + abdominal mass + diarrhea = paraneoplastic phenomenon almost pathognomonic for neuroblastoma.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Identify the neurologic syndrome (opsoclonus + myoclonus + ataxia).<br /></br>2. Recall the tumor most strongly associated with OMAS → neuroblastoma.<br /></br>3. Localize – adrenal or sympathetic chain mass fits RUQ palpable abdominal mass in a toddler.<br /></br>4. Autonomic/secretory symptoms (VIP-induced watery diarrhea, catecholamine breakdown products) further support neuroblastoma.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Opsoclonus-myoclonus-ataxia in a young child almost always implies an underlying neuroblastoma.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Neuroblastoma: malignant tumor of neural crest–derived sympathetic ganglion/adrenal medulla cells.<br /></br>• Can secrete catecholamines (↑ HVA/VMA in urine) or VIP → diarrhea.<br /></br>• OMAS is immune-mediated: anti-neuronal antibodies generated against tumor antigens cross-react with cerebellar &amp; ocular motor neurons.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. Confirm diagnosis<br /></br>   – Abdominal/pelvic MRI or CT to define mass.<br /></br>   – Urine or plasma HVA (homovanillic acid) &amp; VMA (vanillylmandelic acid).<br /></br>   – MIBG scan for staging.<br /></br>2. Management<br /></br>   – Surgical resection ± chemotherapy (cyclophosphamide, vincristine, doxorubicin, cisplatin, etoposide) depending on stage.<br /></br>   – Immunotherapy (anti-GD2) and autologous stem-cell rescue for high-risk disease.<br /></br>   – For OMAS: corticosteroids, IVIG, or rituximab to dampen autoimmune cerebellar attack.<br /></br>Documentation pearl: note presence of opsoclonus-myoclonus, abdominal mass size/location, developmental regression, autonomic symptoms, and parent counseling about tumor work-up.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Dancing eyes, dancing feet, and diarrhea come from the adrenal’s NEURObeat.”<br /></br>(Neuroblastoma: opsoclonus-myoclonus “dance,” adrenal origin, secretory diarrhea.)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Hepatoblastoma<br /></br>   • RUQ mass is possible, but hepatoblastoma typically produces β-hCG or AFP, not opsoclonus-myoclonus. No diarrhea; neurologic paraneoplastic phenomena rare.<br /></br>b. Medulloblastoma<br /></br>   • Cerebellar tumor → ataxia, but would not cause an abdominal mass. Opsoclonus-myoclonus is not characteristic; would expect headache, vomiting, papilledema from posterior-fossa pressure.<br /></br>c. Nephroblastoma (Wilms tumor)<br /></br>   • Flank/abdominal mass in toddler; occasionally hypertension or hematuria. No neurologic paraneoplastic syndrome or diarrhea.<br /></br>e. Non-Hodgkin lymphoma<br /></br>   • Can occur in abdomen but uncommon at age 2; systemic “B” symptoms (fever, weight loss) more typical; OPSOC-MYO combo not seen.<br /></br>f. Rhabdomyosarcoma<br /></br>   • Soft-tissue tumor can be anywhere (often head/neck, GU tract). Does not secrete VIP/catecholamines; no opsoclonus-myoclonus association.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Confusing cerebellar ataxia from an intracranial tumor (e.g., medulloblastoma) with OMAS. Key distinguishing feature is chaotic eye movements and myoclonus plus an extracranial mass – think neuroblastoma.</p>
80. Three days after admission to the hospital for acute bacterial endocarditis, a 62-year-old man has shortness of breath that began 5 minutes ago while walking for the first time since admission. On admission, he had a 10-day history of fever; examination showed splinter hemorrhages and normal heart sounds without murmurs. His current pulse is 110/min , respirations are 24/min, and blood pressure is 100/50 mm Hg. Crackles are heard over both lung bases. S1 and S2 are soft; there is an S3. A new grade 2/6, early diastolic murmur is heard at the apex and the left sternal border, and a grade 4/6, systolic ejection murmur is heard best in the carotids and at the left sternal border. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Echocardiography</br>b. Ventilation-perfusion lung scans</br>c. Intravenous furosemide therapy</br>d. Intravenous heparin therapy</br>e. Sublingual nitroglycerin therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Echocardiography  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Recent diagnosis: “acute bacterial endocarditis” → high risk for rapid valvular destruction.<br /></br>• Sudden-onset dyspnea with crackles, hypotension (100/50) and tachycardia → acute left-sided heart failure.<br /></br>• New murmurs:<br /></br>  – Early diastolic, decrescendo at apex/L sternal border → acute severe aortic regurgitation (AR).<br /></br>  – Systolic ejection murmur radiating to carotids → pre-existing aortic sclerosis/stenosis or flow murmur.<br /></br>• Soft S1/S2 + S3 → volume overload.<br /></br>Key pattern: Infective endocarditis + NEW murmur + acute pulmonary edema = suspect acute valvular insufficiency (classically AR) requiring emergent surgical evaluation.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why echo?)<br /></br>1. Sudden heart failure in endocarditis most often results from perforation or rupture of an aortic or mitral leaflet.<br /></br>2. Definitive diagnosis and severity assessment of AR/valve perforation = transthoracic or transesophageal echocardiography (TEE is preferred for vegetation or prosthetic valves).<br /></br>3. Echo findings will guide urgent cardiothoracic surgery for valve replacement — the life-saving intervention.<br /></br>Take-home: New murmur or acute CHF in infective endocarditis → get an immediate echocardiogram.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearl: “New early diastolic murmur, acute dyspnea; STAT TEE ordered to assess for aortic valve destruction and need for emergent surgery.”  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Endocarditic vegetations can erode the valve cusp → acute AR.<br /></br>• Acute AR → sudden ↑LVEDP → pulmonary congestion and ↓forward stroke volume → hypotension, S3, flash pulmonary edema.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>First test: Urgent echocardiography (prefer TEE) to confirm AR, evaluate vegetation size, and quantify regurgitation.<br /></br>Definitive therapy once confirmed: Emergent surgical valve repair/replacement + continued IV antibiotics.<br /></br>Adjunctive after echo: Diuretics/vasodilators to temporize hemodynamics, but surgery is curative.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“New Murmur = New Movie” → always “show” (image) the valve with Echo before treating.<br /></br>OR<br /></br>IE + HF = “E(cho) STAT.”  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong<br /></br>b. Ventilation-perfusion scan: Would evaluate pulmonary embolism—doesn’t explain new AR murmur/S3; IE patients are anticoagulation-cautious due to cerebral mycotic aneurysms.<br /></br>c. IV furosemide: Symptomatic relief of pulmonary edema but does NOT identify or fix the ruptured valve; delay can be fatal. Use only after imaging confirms lesion and surgery arranged.<br /></br>d. IV heparin: Indicated for PE/MI; contraindicated in many IE patients (risk of hemorrhagic conversion of septic emboli, intracranial mycotic aneurysm). Does not address valvular destruction.<br /></br>e. Sublingual nitroglycerin: Short-acting preload/afterload reduction, again treats symptoms only; nitrate can further drop BP in acute severe AR without solving the structural problem.  </p></br><p>━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━━<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>In infective endocarditis, any sudden change in cardiac exam or rapid heart-failure symptoms mandates immediate echocardiography to look for acute valvular catastrophe and expedite surgical intervention.</p>
81. A study is proposed to investigate the effectiveness of a third-generation cephalosporin for the treatment of streptococcal pharyngitis. The study will include children between the ages of 5 and 10 years. They will be randomly assigned to receive treatment with a cephalosporin, penicillin, or a placebo. Informed consent will be obtained from one parent. The principal investigator offers a $50 finder's fee to any medical resident who identifies a possible study participant. Which of the following features of this study is of greatest potential concern?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Consent only obtained from one parent</br>b. Use of finder's fees to recruit subjects</br>c. Use of minors as subjects</br>d. Use of placebo as treatment</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>A. Consent only obtained from one parent</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis – How we know this is the key problem</br>• “Children between the ages of 5 and 10 years” → minors cannot give legally-effective consent; permission must come from parent(s)/guardian(s) plus the child’s assent when developmentally appropriate.<br /></br>• “Randomly assigned to cephalosporin, penicillin, or placebo” → one study arm (placebo) withholds proven therapy (penicillin) for group-A β-hemolytic streptococcal (GABHS) pharyngitis. This exposes children to more-than-minimal risk (possibility of rheumatic fever, suppurative complications) without a prospect of direct medical benefit.<br /></br>• Federal regulations (45 CFR 46, Subpart D) therefore require the permission of both parents (unless one parent is unavailable, unknown, dead, or has lost legal authority). Limiting permission to only one parent violates these protections and is the most serious ethical breach in the proposal.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning / Take-away</br>Stepwise logic:</br>1. Identify the subjects as minors → special consent rules apply.</br>2. Determine risk category: withholding proven therapy = &gt; minimal risk, no direct benefit to placebo group.</br>3. Regulatory requirement → both parents must give permission (plus child assent when feasible).<br /></br>Failure to meet this standard jeopardizes the entire study and the safety of the children, making it the “greatest potential concern.”</p></br><p>Pathophysiology / Clinical pearls (brief)</br>• GABHS pharyngitis is routinely treated to prevent rheumatic fever and shorten illness.<br /></br>• Placebo recipients face real, not theoretical, morbidity → hence “more-than-minimal” risk classification.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostics (context)</br>First-line treatment: Penicillin V or amoxicillin for 10 days. Third-generation cephalosporins (e.g., cefdinir) are alternatives for penicillin-allergic patients, but not standard first-line in otherwise healthy children.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>“Placebo + Pediatric Patient = Two Parents.”<br /></br>If a child could be harmed without guaranteed benefit, regulators usually want both parents on board.</p></br><p>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>B. Use of finder’s fees ($50) <br /></br>• Modest compensation for identifying potential participants is allowed if it does not create coercion or undue influence. Size and method here are unlikely to override a resident’s judgment and do not directly threaten subjects’ welfare. Concern = moderate, but less serious than invalid consent.</p></br><p>C. Use of minors as subjects<br /></br>• Permissible when the research question cannot be answered in adults and additional protections (parental permission, assent, IRB review) are in place. Using children is ethical if regulations are followed—problem here is not their inclusion but the inadequate consent process.</p></br><p>D. Use of placebo as treatment<br /></br>• Placebos are allowed in pediatric trials when scientifically necessary and when additional safeguards (rescue therapy, careful monitoring) exist. While ethically sensitive, placebo use is not automatically disqualifying; proper parental permission can make it acceptable. The biggest flaw remains the one-parent consent in a &gt; minimal-risk, no-benefit arm.</p></br><p>Bottom line</br>In pediatric research that carries more than minimal risk without direct benefit, both parents (when available) must consent. A study proceeding with only one parent’s permission violates federal regulations and represents the greatest ethical concern in this scenario.</p>
82. A 27-year-old woman comes to the physician for a follow-up examination. She has a 5-month history of temperatures to 38.3°C (101°F) and a cough productive of phlegm and mucous plugs. She has severe asthma treated with high doses of prednisone. She has had numerous visits to the emergency department since childhood because of uncontrolled symptoms. Expiratory wheezes are heard bilaterally. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin              14.6 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count         8700/mm³</br>Segmented neutrophils   60%</br>Eosinophils             18%</br>Lymphocytes             22%</br>Serum IgE               23,000 IU/mL</br>An x-ray of the chest shows patchy bilateral upper lobe infiltrates that have shifted in location since an x-ray was taken 4 months ago. A CT scan of the chest shows central bronchiectasis without lymphadenopathy. This patient is most likely to have an immediate response to which of the following on skin testing?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Animal dander</br>b. Aspergillus fumigatus</br>c. Bacillus subtilis</br>d. Cladosporium species</br>e. Isocyanate</br>f. Thermophilic actinomycetes</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Aspergillus fumigatus  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key phrases: “severe asthma,” “very high IgE (23,000 IU/mL),” “peripheral eosinophilia (18 %),” “central bronchiectasis,” and “fleeting (shifting) upper-lobe infiltrates.”<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: chronic asthma, refractory symptoms despite prednisone, markedly elevated IgE, eosinophilia, and radiographic triad of central bronchiectasis + transient infiltrates.<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no occupational exposure history, no farming or moldy hay, no basilar fibrosis.<br /></br>Pattern recognition: These findings are classic for allergic bronchopulmonary aspergillosis (ABPA).</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1: Chronic severe asthma → consider asthma complications.<br /></br>Step 2: Marked IgE elevation &amp; eosinophilia → suggests atopic or parasitic etiology.<br /></br>Step 3: Central (“proximal”) bronchiectasis + fleeting infiltrates → virtually pathognomonic for ABPA.<br /></br>Step 4: ABPA is a type I (IgE-mediated, immediate) &amp; type III hypersensitivity reaction to Aspergillus fumigatus colonizing the bronchi. Therefore the skin-prick test that produces an immediate wheal-and-flare is Aspergillus fumigatus.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In an asthmatic with huge IgE, eosinophilia, shifting infiltrates, and central bronchiectasis, think ABPA → immediate skin reactivity to Aspergillus.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: Hypersensitivity to Aspergillus fumigatus growing in bronchial mucus.<br /></br>• Mechanism: Type I (IgE) + Type III immune complex reaction leads to bronchial inflammation, mucus plugging, airway damage, and central bronchiectasis.<br /></br>• Risk factors: Long-standing asthma or cystic fibrosis.<br /></br>• Clinical manifestations: Difficult-to-control asthma, recurrent brownish mucus plugs, fever, malaise, hemoptysis; labs show eosinophilia &amp; very high serum IgE (&gt;1000 IU/mL, often &gt;10,000).<br /></br>• Radiology: Transient, patchy (“fleeting”) infiltrates that migrate, central bronchiectasis.</p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Diagnostics: Positive immediate skin test to Aspergillus antigen, serum Aspergillus-specific IgE, precipitins, and characteristic imaging.<br /></br>• Treatment: 1st-line prolonged oral glucocorticoids (e.g., prednisone); add oral itraconazole or voriconazole to reduce fungal burden; consider omalizumab for refractory disease.<br /></br>• Monitoring: Decline in IgE level used to gauge response; high-resolution CT for bronchiectasis progression.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“ABPA” mnemonic:<br /></br>A – Asthma history<br /></br>B – Bronchiectasis (central)<br /></br>P – Pulmonary infiltrates that Pass (flee/shift)<br /></br>A – Aspergillus skin test positive / Astronomical IgE  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Animal dander – Common allergen producing atopic asthma, but would not give towering IgE (23,000) nor central bronchiectasis or fleeting infiltrates.<br /></br>c. Bacillus subtilis – Thermophilic actinomycete causing hypersensitivity pneumonitis in farmers; presents with diffuse interstitial pattern, not bronchiectasis.<br /></br>d. Cladosporium species – Another cause of hypersensitivity pneumonitis (“moldy basement”); again interstitial disease, not ABPA pattern.<br /></br>e. Isocyanate – Chemical used in polyurethane manufacturing; causes occupational asthma without eosinophilia/IgE this high or bronchiectasis.<br /></br>f. Thermophilic actinomycetes – Classic for Farmer’s lung; would show reticular/nodular basilar infiltrates, not shifting lobar infiltrates nor extreme IgE elevation.</p></br><p>Key difference: Only Aspergillus fumigatus explains an IgE in the tens of thousands + eosinophilia + central bronchiectasis + migrating infiltrates in an asthmatic.</p>
83. A 4-year-old boy undergoes splenectomy to control bleeding from injuries sustained in a motor vehicle collision. His postoperative course is uncomplicated, and he receives pneumococcal and meningococcal vaccinations in preparation for his discharge home. After discharge, which of the following is the most appropriate follow-up intervention to decrease this patient’s risk for subsequent infection?	Correct answer: D</br></br>a. Avoidance of influenza vaccinations</br>b. Ciprofloxacin prophylaxis</br>c. Folic acid supplementation</br>d. Penicillin prophylaxis</br>e. No further intervention is indicated</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer</br>d. Penicillin prophylaxis</p></br><p>Vignette Analysis</br>• Key finding: 4-year-old boy who is now functionally asplenic after a splenectomy.<br /></br>• Pertinent positives: Proper peri-operative vaccines (pneumococcal and meningococcal) have been given, but he is still &lt;5 y o and therefore at highest risk for overwhelming postsplenectomy infection (OPSI).<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: No mention of antibiotic allergy or contraindications.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>1. The spleen removes opsonized, encapsulated bacteria (Streptococcus pneumoniae, Haemophilus influenzae type b, Neisseria meningitidis, Capnocytophaga, Salmonella).<br /></br>2. Children &lt;5 y without a spleen have a 300- to 600-fold higher risk of fulminant sepsis despite appropriate vaccines because most breakthrough cases are caused by non-vaccine S. pneumoniae serotypes.<br /></br>3. Standard of care: Daily oral penicillin V (or amoxicillin) prophylaxis from the time of splenectomy until at least age 5—and longer if splenectomy occurred before age 2 or the child is immunocompromised.</p></br><p>Take-home: Vaccination alone is not enough; continuous oral penicillin dramatically reduces mortality from OPSI.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review</br>• The spleen’s marginal zone macrophages and IgM memory B cells make anticapsular IgM → complement-mediated opsonization.<br /></br>• Without a spleen, encapsulated bacteria cannot be cleared efficiently → rapid bacteremia and septic shock.<br /></br>• Penicillin provides constant serum levels active against S. pneumoniae, the most common culprit of OPSI.</p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Follow-up</br>1. Daily antibiotic: Penicillin V 125 mg PO BID (&lt;3 y) or 250 mg PO BID (≥3 y).<br /></br>   • Alternative if penicillin allergy: Oral azithromycin once daily.<br /></br>2. Vaccines: Already received PCV13/PPV23 and MenACWY; also give Hib and annual inactivated influenza vaccine.<br /></br>3. Emergency plan: Parents receive fever protocol (seek care immediately, give rescue ceftriaxone 50 mg/kg IM if instructed).</p></br><p>Memorization Tips</br>• “Spleen = Screen for Strep” → give Pen!<br /></br>• Rule of 5’s: If splenectomy before age 5, give penicillin until at least age 5 (often longer).</p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Avoidance of influenza vaccinations<br /></br>   – Opposite of recommended care; influenza infection predisposes to secondary bacterial sepsis in asplenic patients. Annual inactivated influenza vaccine is advised.</p></br><p>b. Ciprofloxacin prophylaxis<br /></br>   – Fluoroquinolones are not first-line for pneumococcal coverage and carry safety concerns in children (tendon toxicity). Reserved for specific travel or severe penicillin allergy scenarios.</p></br><p>c. Folic acid supplementation<br /></br>   – Indicated in chronic hemolytic anemias (e.g., sickle cell disease) to support erythropoiesis, not for infection prevention post-splenectomy.</p></br><p>e. No further intervention is indicated<br /></br>   – Neglects well-established guideline of antibiotic prophylaxis; would leave child at high risk for OPSI (up to 50% mortality).</p></br><p>By remembering that every young child without a spleen needs BOTH vaccines and daily penicillin, you can quickly eliminate the distractors and choose penicillin prophylaxis on the exam.</p>
84. A 22-year-old nulligravid woman with cystic fibrosis comes to the physician because she would like to conceive within the next year. She has not been hospitalized in the past 3 years. Her disease is well controlled with antibiotics, vigorous bronchial drainage, bronchodilators, and nutritional support including pancreatic enzyme replacement. She is 155 cm (5 ft 1 in) tall and weighs 38 kg (83 lb); BMI is 16 kg/m2. Her pulse is 96/min, and blood pressure is 138/86 mm Hg. Examination shows no other abnormalities except for a prolonged expiratory phase. Her husband is healthy and has no family history of cystic fibrosis. She should be told that she will most likely require which of the following during pregnancy?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Additional antibiotic therapy</br>b. Insulin therapy</br>c. Nutritional supplementation</br>d. Oxygen therapy</br>e. Multiple hospital admissions</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Nutritional supplementation  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 22-year-old woman with cystic fibrosis (CF); BMI 16 kg/m² (underweight); pancreatic enzyme replacement (indicates malabsorption of fat-soluble nutrients); clinically stable pulmonary status (no hospitalizations x3 yrs, only prolonged expiratory phase).<br /></br>• Pertinent positive: marked under-nutrition (38 kg, BMI 16).<br /></br>• Pertinent negatives: no frequent pulmonary exacerbations, no mention of chronic hypoxemia (SpO₂ not low), no history of glucose intolerance/diabetes, husband is healthy (so no genetic counseling intervention asked).<br /></br>Together these data point to inadequate nutritional reserves as the biggest foreseeable problem during pregnancy.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise logic:<br /></br>1. Pregnancy ↑ caloric need by ~300 kcal/day (1st tri) and ~500 kcal/day (2nd–3rd tri) + ↑ protein, fat-soluble vitamins, minerals.<br /></br>2. CF causes pancreatic exocrine insufficiency → fat malabsorption → chronic deficiencies and failure to thrive if intake is not aggressively supplemented.<br /></br>3. Starting pregnancy at a BMI &lt;17 significantly ↑ risk of intrauterine growth restriction (IUGR), preterm delivery, and maternal morbidity.<br /></br>4. Therefore aggressive nutritional supplementation (high-calorie, high-protein diet + pancreatic enzymes, vitamin A/D/E/K &amp; possible enteral tube feeds) is the most predictable requirement.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In women with CF, nutritional status is the strongest modifiable determinant of maternal and fetal outcome; underweight patients almost always need caloric and vitamin supplementation during pregnancy.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• CFTR mutation → viscous secretions → obstruction of pancreatic ducts → chronic pancreatitis and exocrine insufficiency.<br /></br>• Resulting fat and protein malabsorption leads to low BMI, vitamin A/D/E/K deficiency, and delayed puberty/fertility problems.<br /></br>• Pregnancy superimposes higher metabolic demand, so even patients who are “clinically stable” risk worsening malnutrition and fetal growth compromise.</p></br><p>Treatments / Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>First-line in pregnant CF patients:<br /></br>1. Dietician-guided caloric target (≥120–150% of normal pregnancy requirements).<br /></br>2. Continue pancreatic enzymes and add fat-soluble vitamin supplements (ADEK).<br /></br>3. Consider nighttime tube feeding or parenteral nutrition if oral intake insufficient.<br /></br>4. Monitor weight gain every visit; aim for 0.5–0.6 kg/week in 2nd–3rd trimester.<br /></br>Pulmonary regimen (airway clearance, bronchodilators) is continued but not new. Prophylactic antibiotics are not given unless cultures or exacerbations warrant.</p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“CF pregnancy ABCs” =<br /></br>A – Airway clearance (same as baseline)<br /></br>B – Baby calories (↑↑ nutritional supplementation) ← most necessary<br /></br>C – Check glucose (screen for CFRD)  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Additional antibiotic therapy<br /></br>• Needed only for acute pulmonary exacerbations or positive sputum cultures, not routinely. Patient’s lungs are stable.  </p></br><p>b. Insulin therapy<br /></br>• CF-related diabetes prevalence rises with age; risk exists but only ~1–2% of women &lt;25 yrs. You would screen (OGTT at 20–24 wks) rather than pre-emptively start insulin.  </p></br><p>d. Oxygen therapy<br /></br>• Indicated if resting SaO₂ ≤90% or PaO₂ &lt;60 mm Hg; vignette shows no hypoxemia and only mildly prolonged expiratory phase.  </p></br><p>e. Multiple hospital admissions<br /></br>• Hospitalization rate correlates with severe lung disease (FEV₁ &lt;50%, chronic infections). Stable outpatient CF patients often carry pregnancy to term with few or no admissions when BMI and pulmonary status are optimized.</p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Assuming pulmonary issues dominate all CF pregnancies. In reality, inadequate nutrition is the earliest and most universal complication, especially in underweight women.</p>
85. A 6-month-old girl is brought to the emergency department by her parents because of a 2-day history of temperatures to 39.4°C (103.0°F), irritability, decreased oral intake, and vomiting. She has no history of serious illness and receives no medications. She has not received any vaccinations. She appears acutely ill and is lethargic. Temperature is 38.9°C (102.0°F), pulse is 170/min, respirations are 60/min, and blood pressure is 70/45 mm Hg. Pulse oximetry on room air shows an oxygen saturation of 95%. Examination discloses a bulging fontanel and decreased responsiveness. Lumbar puncture is performed. Results of cerebrospinal fluid analysis are shown:</br>RBC           2/mm3  </br>WBC           800/mm3  </br>Neutrophils   88%  </br>Monocytes     12%  </br>Glucose       15 mg/dL  </br>Protein       150 mg/dL  </br>Broad-spectrum antibiotic and corticosteroid therapy is initiated. Which of the following is the most likely long-term complication of this patient’s condition?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Epidural abscess  </br>b. Hearing loss  </br>c. Obstructive hydrocephalus  </br>d. Seizure disorder  </br>e. Subdural effusion</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Hearing loss (sensorineural)</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis</br>• Age &amp; vaccination status: 6-month-old, unimmunized → high risk for encapsulated bacteria (eg, Streptococcus pneumoniae, Haemophilus influenzae type b, Neisseria meningitidis).<br /></br>• Presentation: high fever, vomiting, irritability, lethargy, bulging fontanel → classic acute bacterial meningitis in an infant.<br /></br>• Vital signs: hypotension (70/45 mm Hg) and tachycardia signify severe infection/sepsis.<br /></br>• CSF profile: WBC 800/mm³ with 88 % neutrophils, low glucose (15 mg/dL), high protein (150 mg/dL) → unequivocal for bacterial meningitis.</p></br><p>Key pattern: Acute bacterial meningitis in a young child.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning</br>Step-wise approach:</br>1. Recognize bacterial meningitis from CSF findings.<br /></br>2. Recall the most common chronic sequelae of bacterial meningitis in infants/children.<br /></br>3. Rank complications by frequency: sensorineural hearing loss ≫ seizures, hydrocephalus, subdural effusion/abscess.<br /></br>Core takeaway: In survivors of bacterial meningitis, the ear (cochlea/8th nerve) is the organ most frequently and permanently damaged.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: In real practice, document the need for a follow-up hearing screen (auditory brain-stem response or otoacoustic emissions) once the child stabilizes.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review</br>• Inflammatory exudate tracks through the cochlear aqueduct into the inner ear.<br /></br>• Cytokine-mediated damage and labyrinthitis cause fibrosis/ossification of the cochlea and injury to cranial nerve VIII → irreversible sensorineural hearing loss.<br /></br>Risk is greatest with S pneumoniae &amp; H influenzae type b, exactly the organisms prevented by routine vaccines this child missed.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review</br>Acute:<br /></br>1. Empiric IV cefotaxime/ceftriaxone + vancomycin (broad-spectrum)<br /></br>2. Dexamethasone (shown to reduce risk of hearing loss, especially in Hib).<br /></br>3. Supportive care for shock (fluid resuscitation, pressors).</p></br><p>Follow-up:<br /></br>• Audiologic testing before discharge and again at 3-6 mo.<br /></br>• Developmental surveillance, ± imaging if hydrocephalus suspected.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Memorization Tips</br>“BEAR the long-term cost”<br /></br>Bacterial meningitis → Ear damage → Audiology Required.<br /></br>Visualize a bear holding earmuffs to remember the ear complication.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis</br>a. Epidural abscess<br /></br>   • Presents later with focal back pain, fever, neurologic deficits. It is a rare complication of otitis or sinusitis, not the most common sequela of meningitis.</p></br><p>c. Obstructive hydrocephalus<br /></br>   • May occur from impaired CSF flow after meningitis, but it is far less frequent than hearing loss.</p></br><p>d. Seizure disorder<br /></br>   • Early or late post-meningitic seizures occur, yet only ~5-10 % develop chronic epilepsy vs up to 30 % with hearing loss.</p></br><p>e. Subdural effusion<br /></br>   • Common transient finding (especially in infants) but usually resolves without long-term morbidity and is still less frequent than permanent hearing loss.</p></br><p>Common pitfall: choosing dramatic neurologic complications (seizures, hydrocephalus) and overlooking the much more prevalent but subtler sensory deficit.</p></br><p>─────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line</br>In infants and children who survive bacterial meningitis, irreversible sensorineural hearing loss is the single most common long-term complication; therefore, all such patients need formal audiologic follow-up.</p>
86. A 16-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because she has never had a menstrual period. She is 147 cm (4 ft 10 in) tall and weighs 52 kg (115 lb); BMI is 24 kg/m2. Her temperature is 37.1°C (98.8°F), pulse is 70/min, respirations are 14/min, and blood pressure is 110/60 mm Hg. Breast and pubic hair development is sexual maturity rating stage I, and there is scant axillary hair. Pelvic examination shows no abnormalities. Her serum follicle-stimulating hormone concentration is 62 mIU/mL. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Observation only</br>b. Karyotype analysis</br>c. Hysterosalpingography</br>d. MRI of the sella turcica</br>e. Gonadotropin-releasing hormone stimulation test</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>B. Karyotype analysis  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><ol></br><li>Age ≥ 15 yr with NO menses + NO secondary sex characteristics → “primary amenorrhea with absent pubertal development.”  </li></br><li>Breast &amp; pubic hair Tanner stage I → very low estrogen.  </li></br><li>FSH = 62 mIU/mL (markedly ↑)<br /></br>   • High FSH = the pituitary is working → failure is at the level of the ovaries (“hyper-gonadotropic hypogonadism”).  </li></br><li>Pelvic exam “no abnormalities” → uterus/vagina present (rules out Müllerian agenesis).  </li></br></ol></br><p>Pattern: Primary ovarian failure/gonadal dysgenesis → think Turner (45,X) or 46,XY “Swyer” syndrome, both diagnosed with chromosomal analysis.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><p>Step 1: Does she meet criteria for primary amenorrhea? Yes.<br /></br>Step 2: Are secondary sexual characteristics present? No → check FSH/LH.<br /></br>Step 3: FSH is HIGH → primary ovarian insufficiency.<br /></br>Step 4: Next diagnostic test in algorithm = karyotype to determine etiology (45,X vs 46,XX vs 46,XY), because presence of a Y chromosome mandates prophylactic gonadectomy to prevent gonadoblastoma.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: In primary amenorrhea with absent breasts and ↑FSH, always order a karyotype.  </p></br><p>Documentation pearls: “Primary amenorrhea, Tanner I, hypergonadotropic hypogonadism—plan: send peripheral blood karyotype; counsel re possible gonadectomy &amp; estrogen replacement pending results.”  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review  </p></br><p>• Turner syndrome (45,X): streak ovaries lack follicles → ↓estrogen → ↑FSH/LH; short stature common (vignette height 147 cm).<br /></br>• 46,XY complete gonadal dysgenesis (Swyer): streak gonads that can become malignant if not removed.<br /></br>• Both present with no breast development and primary amenorrhea.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic &amp; Treatment Overview  </p></br><p>Diagnostics<br /></br>• First: Karyotype.<br /></br>• Adjunct labs: Estradiol (low), LH (high), consider thyroid &amp; prolactin if central cause suspected.<br /></br>• Imaging: Pelvic US after karyotype to confirm streak gonads/uterus presence.  </p></br><p>Treatment (after diagnosis)<br /></br>• Estrogen replacement followed by cyclic estrogen-progesterone for breast/uterine development and bone health.<br /></br>• If Y chromosome: bilateral gonadectomy ASAP.<br /></br>• Turner: evaluate for coarctation, bicuspid valve, horseshoe kidney, auditory deficits; consider growth hormone if diagnosed earlier.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“High FSH = ovaries crashed → Check the Karyotype FAST.”<br /></br>FAST = FSH Above, Streak gonads, Turner/Swyer → do a karyotype.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>A. Observation only – Abnormal puberty and markedly ↑FSH require urgent evaluation; doing nothing risks missing Y-chromosome gonads and malignancy.  </p></br><p>C. Hysterosalpingography – Used to evaluate uterine cavity/tubal patency in infertility, not primary amenorrhea due to ovarian failure.  </p></br><p>D. MRI of the sella turcica – Indicated when FSH/LH are low or normal (hypogonadotropic hypogonadism) to look for pituitary lesions; here FSH is high.  </p></br><p>E. GnRH stimulation test – Used when distinguishing central (hypothalamic vs pituitary) causes with low gonadotropins; unnecessary with clearly elevated FSH.  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Key Takeaway:<br /></br>Primary amenorrhea + absent secondary sex characteristics + elevated FSH = primary ovarian insufficiency → perform karyotype analysis next.</p>
87. A 7-year-old boy is brought to the physician because of a 4-day history of a dry, mildly itchy rash. The rash began as a small, pink, oval area on his chest and progressed to scaly, red patches over his arms, legs, and body. His mother applied calamine lotion and a topical corticosteroid cream without relief. He is currently taking amoxicillin for streptococcal pharyngitis diagnosed 4 days ago. He has no other history of rash or serious illness. He is alert and cooperative and in no acute distress. The pharynx is pink, and the tonsils are slightly enlarged with no exudate. The neck is supple with no adenopathy. A photograph of the rash over the trunk is shown. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Drug allergy</br>b. Pityriasis rosea</br>c. Scarlet fever</br>d. Seborrheic dermatitis</br>e. Tinea corporis</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>b. Pityriasis rosea  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Small, pink, oval area on his chest” that appeared first → classic “herald patch.”<br /></br>• “Progressed to scaly, red patches over his arms, legs, and body” in a short time span → typical secondary eruption that spreads centrifugally along Langer’s lines (Christmas-tree pattern).<br /></br>• Mild pruritus, otherwise well; no fever, adenopathy, or systemic complaints → supports a benign self-limited exanthem rather than drug reaction or scarlet fever.<br /></br>• Age (adolescents/young children) and absence of response to topical steroids/calamine are also typical for pityriasis rosea.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step 1 – Identify single larger primary lesion (herald patch).<br /></br>Step 2 – Note subsequent widespread oval/annular salmon-colored plaques with fine scale along cleavage lines.<br /></br>Step 3 – Exclude mimickers: no mucosal lesions, no diffuse erythema or sand-paper feel, no annular lesions with central clearing, no greasy yellow scale on scalp/face.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a child or adolescent with a herald patch followed days later by an oval, scaly “Christmas-tree” eruption, think pityriasis rosea.  </p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thought to be triggered by HHV-6/7 reactivation.<br /></br>• Self-limited (6–8 weeks).<br /></br>• Mildly pruritic; systemic symptoms rare.  </p></br><p>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• Diagnosis is clinical—no labs/biopsy needed.<br /></br>• Reassurance and symptomatic control:<br /></br>  – Oral antihistamines or low-potency topical steroids for pruritus.<br /></br>  – Sun exposure often hastens resolution.<br /></br>• Rule of thumb: if rash lasts &gt;3 months, consider alternative diagnoses or biopsy.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Herald announces the Christmas tree”: a HERALD patch appears first, then a tree-shaped distribution on the trunk.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. Drug allergy<br /></br>• Usually starts 7–14 days after starting a new drug (this child started amoxicillin only 4 days ago).<br /></br>• Morbilliform, urticarial, or targetoid; often symmetric beginning on trunk but lacks single herald patch. Often pruritic and may have eosinophilia.  </p></br><p>c. Scarlet fever<br /></br>• Occurs with streptococcal pharyngitis but presents with high fever, sand-paper erythematous rash, Pastia lines, strawberry tongue, and circumoral pallor. Here the child is afebrile, pharynx benign, rash scaly not sand-paper.  </p></br><p>d. Seborrheic dermatitis<br /></br>• Greasy, yellow, crusty scale on scalp (cradle cap), face, ears, chest—not discrete oval plaques on trunk/limbs.  </p></br><p>e. Tinea corporis<br /></br>• Annular plaques with central clearing and raised scaling border (“ringworm”), usually few in number. KOH scrapings positive for dermatophytes. Pityriasis rosea shows multiple lesions without central clearing.  </p></br><p>By focusing on the herald patch followed by a Christmas-tree distribution of scaly oval plaques, pityriasis rosea is the most likely diagnosis.</p>
88. A 3-month-old girl is brought to the physician because of constipation. She was born at home at term following an uncomplicated pregnancy and weighed 3118 g (6 lb 14 oz). She has not had routine well-child examinations. She is unable to hold her head up, does not roll over, and does not smile. She is at the 10th percentile for length, 5th percentile for weight, and 75th percentile for head circumference. The posterior fontanelle is 2 cm in diameter. The tongue is large and protuberant. Which of the following is most likely to confirm the diagnosis?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Diet history</br>b. Thyroid function tests</br>c. Upper gastrointestinal series</br>d. Cranial ultrasonography</br>e. Rectal biopsy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>b. Thyroid function tests (serum TSH and free T4)</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis – Key Clues</br>• “3-month-old” with chronic constipation → suggests systemic, not transient, problem.<br /></br>• Global developmental delay: cannot hold head up, roll, or smile.<br /></br>• Disproportionate growth: length 10th %, weight 5th %, head 75th %. (Brain growth relatively preserved; body growth stunted.)<br /></br>• Physical stigmata: large posterior fontanelle, macroglossia (“large and protuberant” tongue), hypotonia.<br /></br>• Born at home &amp; “no routine well-child examinations” → very likely missed state newborn screen.  </p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Classic constellation of congenital hypothyroidism (cretinism).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Why choice B?)</br>Step 1 – Suspect congenital hypothyroidism.<br /></br>Step 2 – What test confirms? Measure thyroid hormone levels: markedly ↑TSH with ↓free T4 (primary thyroid dysgenesis or dyshormonogenesis) or, rarely, low/normal TSH with low T4 (central). These labs are both diagnostic and dictate urgent levothyroxine therapy to prevent permanent neurocognitive impairment.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: Any infant with macroglossia, large fontanelles, constipation, prolonged jaundice, or developmental delay—especially if newborn screen unavailable—must have immediate thyroid function testing.</p></br><p>Documentation (real-life): “Developmental delay, macroglossia, large fontanelle—concern for congenital hypothyroidism; send STAT TSH and free T4; start empiric levothyroxine if T4 low.”</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Snapshot</br>• Etiology: thyroid dysgenesis (agenesis, ectopy) ≈ 85% in US; dyshormonogenesis, maternal iodine deficiency, TSH-receptor blocking Ab less common.<br /></br>• Low T4 impairs myelination &amp; skeletal maturation → irreversible intellectual disability if not treated within first 2 wks.<br /></br>• Clinical triad (high-yield): lethargy + large tongue + umbilical hernia (“5 P’s”: Pot-bellied, Pale/puffy face, Protruding umbilicus, Protuberant tongue, Poor brain development).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics</br>• Confirm with TSH/free T4.<br /></br>• Begin weight-based oral levothyroxine immediately—do NOT delay for labs if strongly suspected.<br /></br>• Follow T4 q2–4 wks initially, then q1–2 mos in 6–12 mos. Developmental surveillance essential.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Memory Aids</br>“C-H-E-T” = Congenital Hypothyroidism: Constipation, Hypotonia, Enlarged tongue/fontanelle, Thick features.<br /></br>“B-4 it’s too late, check T4 by 14 days.” (screen or test before 2 weeks).</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Why the Other Choices Are Wrong</br>a. Diet history – Might uncover low fiber but does not explain macroglossia, large fontanelle, growth/development delay.<br /></br>c. Upper GI series – Evaluates malrotation / midgut volvulus (bilious emesis), not chronic constipation with systemic findings.<br /></br>d. Cranial ultrasonography – Useful for intraventricular hemorrhage or hydrocephalus; would not explain macroglossia or constipation; head circumference only modestly large.<br /></br>e. Rectal biopsy – Diagnoses Hirschsprung disease (aganglionosis); typical presentation is delayed meconium, explosive stool after DRE; does not cause macroglossia or developmental delay.</p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────</br>Bottom Line: In any infant with missed newborn screen plus clinical signs of hypothyroidism, STAT thyroid function tests are the definitive and time-critical next step.</p>
89. A 32-year-old man comes to the office for a routine examination. He was diagnosed with HIV infection 7 years ago and began antiretroviral therapy 6 years ago. He received the appropriate immunizations at the time of diagnosis. He has tolerated his medication regimen well. He used illicit intravenous drugs for 5 years but stopped 7 years ago. He is in a monogamous relationship with one female partner; they use condoms consistently. Examination shows no abnormalities. His CD4+ T-lymphocyte count is 645/mm3 (Normal≥500), and plasma HIV viral load is 1000 copies/mL. Which of the following immunizations is most appropriate to administer at this time?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. Hepatitis A</br>b. Hepatitis B</br>c. Human papillomavirus</br>d. Meningococcal</br>e. Pneumococcal</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>e. Pneumococcal (PPSV23 booster)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis  </p></br><p>Key clues:<br /></br>• 32-year-old man with well-controlled HIV (CD4 645/mm³).<br /></br>• “Received the appropriate immunizations at the time of diagnosis” 7 years ago.<br /></br>• No current risk factors other than remote IV-drug use.<br /></br>• Question asks for the single vaccine that is “most appropriate to administer at this time.”</p></br><p>Pertinent positives/negatives that narrow the choice:<br /></br>• He is &gt;5 years out from his initial pneumococcal polysaccharide vaccine (PPSV23).<br /></br>• He already completed all primary series recommended at diagnosis.<br /></br>• Age &gt;26 → outside catch-up window for HPV.<br /></br>• Hep A &amp; B series are given once (no boosters).<br /></br>• Meningococcal ACWY boosters are due every 5 years, but only AFTER the 2-dose primary series has been completed; nothing in the stem tells us he missed those boosters, whereas we know with certainty that a PPSV23 booster is required in everyone with HIV 5 years after the first dose.</p></br><p>Pattern to recognize: HIV patients always need a second PPSV23 dose 5 years after the first if they received their first dose before age 65.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning (Step-wise)  </p></br><ol></br><li>Initial diagnosis (7 y ago) ⇒ patient should have received:<br /></br>   • PCV13 once, then PPSV23 ≥8 wk later<br /></br>   • MenACWY (2-dose series)<br /></br>   • Hep A (because of IVDU), Hep B, HPV (age ≤26)  </li></br><li>Follow-up immunization schedules in HIV:<br /></br>   • PPSV23 booster 5 y after first dose, then again at age 65.<br /></br>   • MenACWY booster every 5 y.  </li></br><li>Today is 7 y after first PPSV23 → booster overdue → give PPSV23 now.</li></br></ol></br><p>Core takeaway: Immunocompromised adults (HIV, asplenia, etc.) who receive PPSV23 before age 65 must repeat PPSV23 after 5 years.</p></br><p>Documentation note: “Administered PPSV23 0.5 mL IM; patient educated on local injection-site reactions.”</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review (High-Yield)  </p></br><p>Streptococcus pneumoniae infections are more frequent and more severe in patients with impaired cell-mediated immunity (e.g., HIV). The polysaccharide vaccine (PPSV23) stimulates B-cell–dependent, T-cell–independent IgM production against 23 serotypes. Because immunity wanes, a 5-year booster is essential in immunocompromised adults.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Pearls  </p></br><p>Pneumococcal schedule in HIV (CD4 ≥200):<br /></br>1. PCV13 (Prevnar 13) once.<br /></br>2. PPSV23 (Pneumovax 23) ≥8 wk later.<br /></br>3. PPSV23 again ≥5 y later.<br /></br>4. One more PPSV23 at age 65 if ≥5 y since the prior dose.</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips  </p></br><p>“13 then 23, wait eight weeks;<br /></br>23 again after five years speaks.”<br /></br>(Add a final “once more at 65” to complete the jingle.)</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis  </p></br><p>a. Hepatitis A: Series completed once. Boosters are not recommended in immunocompetent or immunocompromised adults after full series; patient already received it when he was an active IVDU.  </p></br><p>b. Hepatitis B: Three-dose series given once; only revaccinate if titers never reached ≥10 mIU/mL, which is not suggested here.  </p></br><p>c. Human papillomavirus: Catch-up vaccination recommended through age 26 (up to 45 in some guidelines after shared decision-making). At 32, routine administration is no longer advised.  </p></br><p>d. Meningococcal (MenACWY): HIV patients need a 2-dose primary series and then boosters every 5 years. Because the stem specifically assures “appropriate immunizations” at diagnosis, presume primary series was completed. Nothing states boosters were missed, whereas the 5-year PPSV23 booster is explicitly mandated for <em>all</em> HIV patients.  </p></br><p>Common pitfall: Students often pick MenACWY because of its 5-year booster rule; but remember PPSV23 has exactly the same 5-year rule and is universally required, whereas MenACWY booster depends on completion of a 2-dose primary series (which the question implies was done).</p></br><p>──────────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line  </p></br><p>Seven years after his initial pneumococcal series, an HIV-positive adult should receive a booster dose of PPSV23.</p>
90. A 24-year-old man with protein C deficiency comes to the physician because of a 1-week history of yellowing of his skin. Three months ago, he had a mesenteric infarction and required resection of all but 45 cm of the proximal small bowel. The remainder of the jejunum was anastomosed to the colon. He receives total parenteral nutrition at home. He takes no medications. Examination today shows generalized jaundice. Laboratory studies show:</br>Leukocyte count            7000/mm^3</br>Serum</br>Bilirubin, total         3.6 mg/dL</br>Direct                   2.6 mg/dL</br>Alkaline phosphatase       150 U/L</br>AST                        20 U/L</br>ALT                        17 U/L</br>Which of the following is the most likely cause of this patient's hyperbilirubinemia?	Correct answer: B</br></br>a. Biliary stricture</br>b. Cholestatic hepatic disease</br>c. Hemolysis</br>d. Hepatitis A</br>e. Hepatitis C</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>a. Biliary stricture  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• “Total parenteral nutrition (TPN) at home” + “massive small-bowel resection”  ➔ no enteral feeding, no cholecystokinin release, gallbladder stasis, biliary sludge/stones, chronic inflammation of the extra-hepatic ducts.<br /></br>• “Generalized jaundice … total bilirubin 3.6 mg/dL, direct 2.6 mg/dL”  ➔ predominantly conjugated (direct) hyperbilirubinaemia.<br /></br>• Mildly ↑ alkaline phosphatase (150 U/L; upper limit ≈120 U/L) with normal AST/ALT (20/17 U/L)  ➔ cholestatic (obstructive) pattern without hepatocellular injury.<br /></br>• No medications, no transfusions, normal WBC count – removes many other causes.  </p></br><p>Putting the clues together: long-standing TPN → biliary sludge &amp; gallstones → chronic inflammation/fibrosis → benign extra-hepatic biliary stricture ➔ painless, conjugated hyperbilirubinaemia with only a modest ALP rise in early obstruction.</p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Distinguish unconjugated vs conjugated hyperbilirubinaemia. Direct fraction is high → conjugated.<br /></br>2. Decide intra-hepatic vs extra-hepatic cholestasis. Markedly high ALP/GGT + dilated ducts on imaging usually point to extra-hepatic obstruction; AST/ALT would be high in intra-hepatic disease. Here, very normal transaminases and only mildly ↑ ALP suggest early or partial extra-hepatic obstruction (biliary stricture).<br /></br>3. Identify risk factor that produces extra-hepatic obstruction in this patient → prolonged TPN.  </p></br><p>Core takeaway: Long-term TPN predisposes to gallbladder stasis, biliary sludge, and eventually benign biliary strictures that present with isolated conjugated hyperbilirubinaemia and relatively normal transaminases.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Lack of enteral stimulation → ↓CCK → gallbladder hypomotility.<br /></br>• Sludge/stone formation → episodic obstruction, bacterial colonisation, inflammation.<br /></br>• Repetitive injury and fibrosis of the common bile duct → benign biliary stricture.  </p></br><p>Treatments &amp; Diagnostic Tests<br /></br>• First test: right-upper-quadrant ultrasound (or MRCP) to look for ductal dilation &amp; stones.<br /></br>• Definitive test/therapy: ERCP with cholangiography, balloon dilatation or stent placement.<br /></br>• Preventive: encourage even minimal enteral feeds, cyclic TPN, ursodeoxycholic acid, or scheduled gallbladder emptying with IV CCK analogues in some centres.  </p></br><p>Memorisation Tips<br /></br>“TPN = The Poor gallbladder is Not squeezed” → sludge → “stiff” duct (stricture).<br /></br>Visualise a patient hooked to an IV bag; their gallbladder turns to stone and pinches the duct like a zip-tie (stricture) leading to yellow skin.  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Cholestatic hepatic disease (TPN-associated intra-hepatic cholestasis)<br /></br> • Would still show conjugated bilirubin, but ALP and especially GGT rise markedly; often AST/ALT 50–200 U/L.<br /></br> • Histology: canalicular cholestasis rather than ductal obstruction.<br /></br>c. Hemolysis<br /></br> • Produces indirect (unconjugated) hyperbilirubinaemia; direct fraction would be low and AST/LDH often elevated from RBC destruction.<br /></br>d. Hepatitis A<br /></br>e. Hepatitis C<br /></br> • Both cause hepatocellular injury → pronounced AST/ALT elevations (hundreds-thousands) and usually mixed or unconjugated hyperbilirubinaemia early on.  </p></br><p>Common Pitfall: Attributing any jaundice in a TPN patient to “TPN-associated cholestasis.” Always check the pattern of liver tests; a predominantly ductal picture (direct bilirubin high, ALP modestly ↑, AST/ALT normal) should trigger evaluation for mechanical obstruction such as a biliary stricture.</p>
91. Eight weeks after an uncomplicated vaginal delivery of a healthy full-term newborn, a 27-year-old woman, gravida 1, para 1, is brought to the physician by her husband because of a change in personality and depressed mood over the past 10 days. She has not had headaches. Her menses have not resumed despite the discontinuation of breast-feeding 5 weeks ago. Her husband says that she has no interest in sexual intercourse. She does not use illicit drugs. She has had a 2.7-kg (6-lb) weight gain since delivery. She is now 175 cm (5 ft 9 in) tall and weighs 74 kg (164 lb); BMI is 24 kg/m2. Physical and neurologic examinations show no abnormalities. On mental status examination, she has a flat affect and is slow to respond to questioning. She spontaneously cries during the interview and examination. She has no suicidal ideation. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: E</br></br>a. MRI of the brain</br>b. Antipsychotic therapy</br>c. Cyclic progestin therapy</br>d. Estrogen therapy</br>e. Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor therapy</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>MRI of the brain  </p></br><p>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>Pertinent positives<br /></br>• 8 weeks postpartum and no longer breast-feeding → menses should usually resume within 4–6 weeks; ongoing amenorrhea is abnormal.<br /></br>• Diminished libido and 6-lb weight gain → suggest low estrogen (hypogonadism) and/or hypothyroidism.<br /></br>• New personality change, flat affect, crying spells → could be psychiatric, but also occur with endocrine insufficiency.<br /></br>• NO postpartum hemorrhage, visual changes, or headache → argues against classic Sheehan syndrome but does not exclude other pituitary pathology.<br /></br>Pertinent negatives<br /></br>• No illicit drug use, normal neurologic exam → makes primary psychiatric illness or a structural brain lesion outside the pituitary less likely.  </p></br><p>Clinical Critical Reasoning (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Amenorrhea in a non-lactating woman 8 weeks after delivery is pathologic → think HPO-axis suppression.<br /></br>2. Additional systemic symptoms (weight gain, ↓ libido, depression) point toward global pituitary hormone deficits (secondary hypothyroidism ± secondary adrenal insufficiency, hypogonadotropic hypogonadism).<br /></br>3. Two classic postpartum pituitary disorders:<br /></br>   a. Sheehan syndrome (ischemic necrosis after severe hemorrhage).<br /></br>   b. Lymphocytic hypophysitis (autoimmune inflammation in late pregnancy/post-partum; may enlarge pituitary or create “empty sella”).<br /></br>4. First diagnostic step: visualize the pituitary with MRI to confirm enlargement, mass, or empty sella and to guide hormone testing/replacement.<br /></br>Take-home: Unexplained amenorrhea plus systemic “low-hormone” symptoms after pregnancy should prompt pituitary imaging before labeling the patient as purely depressed.</p></br><p>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Lymphocytic hypophysitis: autoimmune infiltration → pituitary enlargement → varying degrees of hypopituitarism (↓ ACTH, TSH, LH/FSH, PRL).<br /></br>• Sheehan syndrome: infarction after hemorrhage → usually presents with failure to lactate, amenorrhea, hypotension, fatigue.  </p></br><p>Treatments and Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>1. MRI pituitary with contrast → confirms diagnosis and excludes adenoma.<br /></br>2. Hormone panel (morning cortisol, free T4, TSH, LH/FSH, prolactin).<br /></br>3. Management = hormone replacement (hydrocortisone BEFORE levothyroxine if cortisol is low; then estrogen-progestin or combined OCPs for gonadal axis).<br /></br>4. High-dose glucocorticoids +/- surgery if mass effect from hypophysitis.  </p></br><p>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Post-partum A-MEN”:<br /></br>A = Amenorrhea +<br /></br>M = Mood change +<br /></br>E = Energy low (weight gain) → N = Need pituitary MRI  </p></br><p>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>Antipsychotic therapy – Indicated for psychosis (delusions, hallucinations). Patient is sad &amp; slowed, not psychotic.<br /></br>Cyclic progestin therapy – Used to protect endometrium in chronic anovulation (e.g., PCOS). Does not explain weight gain &amp; global symptoms; does not evaluate pituitary.<br /></br>Estrogen therapy – Treats hypoestrogenism but would mask the underlying cause and is contraindicated before ruling out secondary adrenal insufficiency (risk of adrenal crisis).<br /></br>Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor (SSRI) therapy – First-line for postpartum depression. However, persistent amenorrhea and systemic endocrine clues strongly suggest an organic pituitary disorder that must be excluded first. Starting an SSRI misses the diagnosis and delays potentially lifesaving hormone replacement.  </p></br><p>Key “Shelf” Pearl<br /></br>When postpartum mood symptoms are accompanied by persistent amenorrhea (after cessation of breastfeeding) or other systemic hypo-hormone signs, THINK PITUITARY and order an MRI before prescribing psychiatric medications.</p>
92. A 32-year-old woman, gravida 3, para 2, at 37 weeks’ gestation is admitted to the hospital in labor. Contractions occur every 3 minutes. She has gained 2.3 kg (5 lb) during the past week and has had occasional blurred vision. She has been otherwise healthy. Her temperature is 37°C (98.6°F), pulse is 80/min, respirations are 20/min, and blood pressure is 150/110 mm Hg. Physical examination shows retinal arteriolar spasm and pedal edema. The fundus is midway between the umbilicus and xiphoid. The cervix is 3 cm dilated and 100% effaced; the vertex is at 0 station. Laboratory studies show:</br>Hemoglobin 12.5 g/dL</br>Leukocyte count 8000/mm^3</br>Platelet count 200,000/mm^3</br>Serum creatinine 1.2 mg/dL</br>Urine protein 2+</br>Which of the following is the most likely complication during labor?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Abruptio placentae</br>b. Amniotic fluid embolism</br>c. Arrest of labor</br>d. Maternal death</br>e. Uterine rupture</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>A. Abruptio placentae  </p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Key clues: 37-week gestation, new-onset hypertension (150/110 mm Hg) after 20 weeks, 2+ proteinuria, rapid weight gain/edema, blurred vision, retinal arteriolar spasm → classic picture of pre-eclampsia.<br /></br>• Other data are normal (platelets 200 K, creatinine 1.2 mg/dL) confirming pre-eclampsia without severe features such as thrombocytopenia or renal failure.<br /></br>• Question: “most likely complication during labor.” For pre-eclampsia the obstetric complication most frequently encountered intrapartum is placental abruption.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise link:<br /></br>1. Maternal hypertension → generalized arteriolar vasospasm.<br /></br>2. Uteroplacental vessels constrict → decidual ischemia and necrosis.<br /></br>3. Small bleeding starts between placenta and uterine wall; more vasospasm makes bleeding worse.<br /></br>4. Result = premature separation of a normally implanted placenta (abruptio placentae) which commonly presents during labor with painful vaginal bleeding, uterine hypertonicity, fetal distress.<br /></br>Core takeaway: Hypertensive disorders of pregnancy, especially pre-eclampsia/eclampsia, are the leading predisposing factor for placental abruption.</p></br><p>Documentation note: In a real chart you would record blood pressure trends, proteinuria, neurologic symptoms, and order magnesium sulfate for seizure prophylaxis; you would also watch for vaginal bleeding or uterine tetany signaling abruption.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>Pre-eclampsia = abnormal placental spiral-artery remodeling → high-resistance, low-flow state → maternal endothelial dysfunction, vasoconstriction, capillary leak. Uteroplacental vasospasm specifically sets the stage for retroplacental hemorrhage and abruption.</p></br><p>Risk factors for abruption<br /></br>• Chronic or pregnancy-induced hypertension (most common)<br /></br>• Trauma, cocaine, smoking, prior abruption, PPROM, or rapid loss of uterine volume (e.g., after ROM with polyhydramnios)</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostics (high-yield quick hit)<br /></br>• Pre-eclampsia: IV magnesium sulfate (seizure prophylaxis), IV labetalol/hydralazine or oral nifedipine for severe BP, deliver at ≥37 wk.<br /></br>• Suspected abruption: Stabilize mother (IV access, type &amp; cross, continuous fetal monitoring), prepare for rapid vaginal or cesarean delivery depending on fetal/maternal status.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“Three P’s of Pre-eclampsia: Pressure high → Placenta Pulls away” = HTN leads to Placental abruption.<br /></br>Another: “HELLP crashes, Abruptio tears” → HTN disorders give you HELLP and Abruptio.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>B. Amniotic fluid embolism – Catastrophic, unpredictable, occurs at delivery or immediately postpartum when amniotic fluid enters maternal circulation; not specifically linked to HTN and far rarer than abruption. Presents with sudden dyspnea, DIC, cardiovascular collapse.  </p></br><p>C. Arrest of labor – Usually from cephalopelvic disproportion, malposition, inadequate contractions; incidence is not increased by pre-eclampsia per se.  </p></br><p>D. Maternal death – Possible but fortunately uncommon; question asks for “most likely” intrapartum complication. Abruptio is far more frequent.  </p></br><p>E. Uterine rupture – Associated with prior uterine surgery (classical C-section, myomectomy), high-parity uterus, trauma; pre-eclampsia does not predispose.</p></br><p>───────────────────<br /></br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Hypertensive disorders of pregnancy create uteroplacental vasospasm that most commonly leads to abruptio placentae during labor. Watch hypertensive parturients closely for sudden painful bleeding and fetal distress.</p>
93. A 32-year-old man is brought to the physician by his wife because of a gradual change in personality during the past 2 years. She describes him as less motivated and more self-centered. During the past year, he has had several angry outbursts that were triggered by minor events. The patient says that he does not believe his personality has changed and that he does not recall these events. He is alert and attentive. On examination, eye movements are full, but saccadic eye movements are noticeably slow. Smooth pursuit and vestibular ocular movements are normal. He has occasional involuntary rapid, jerk-like movements of the trunk and extremities, but he is unaware of these movements. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. EEG</br>b. Follow-up examination in 1 month</br>c. Genetic testing</br>d. Lumbar puncture</br>e. Measurement of serum ceruloplasmin concentration</br>f. Psychiatric assessment</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer Selection<br /></br>c. Genetic testing (HTT CAG‐repeat analysis)</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age of onset: 32 years (classic for an autosomal-dominant, mid-life neuro­degenerative disorder).<br /></br>• Gradual personality/behavioral change: apathy, irritability, disinhibition—very characteristic early psychiatric presentation of Huntington disease (HD).<br /></br>• Motor findings:  – Chorea (“involuntary rapid, jerk-like movements”)<br /></br>                  – Slow saccadic eye movements with otherwise intact pursuit/vestibulo-ocular function (classic early ocular sign in HD).<br /></br>• Insight often impaired—patient unaware of episodes.<br /></br>• No other focal neurologic deficits, normal sensorium → points away from mass lesion, stroke, infection, metabolic encephalopathy.</p></br><p>Key pertinent negatives: no KF rings, parkinsonian rigidity, ataxia, liver disease → argues against Wilson disease.</p></br><p>Pattern recognition: Mid-30s adult with progressive behavioral change + chorea + slow saccades → think Huntington disease.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Step-wise approach:<br /></br>1. Identify pattern suggesting a single primary neuro­degenerative disorder.<br /></br>2. HD is autosomal dominant, fully penetrant; the diagnostic gold standard is molecular confirmation of the HTT CAG-repeat expansion.<br /></br>3. Once clinical suspicion is high, the most direct, definitive, and least invasive next step is genetic testing. No imaging, lumbar puncture, or EEG is needed to establish the diagnosis.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In a patient with characteristic signs of Huntington disease, confirmatory genetic testing is the appropriate next diagnostic step.</p></br><p>Documentation pearls: record family history, counsel about genetic implications, discuss need for genetic counseling of relatives.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Etiology: CAG trinucleotide repeat expansion (&gt;36 repeats) in HTT gene on chromosome 4; anticipation with paternal transmission.<br /></br>• Mechanism: mutant huntingtin protein → neuronal loss in caudate &amp; putamen → decreased GABA and acetylcholine, increased dopamine.<br /></br>• Clinical triad:<br /></br>  1. Behavioral/psychiatric changes (depression, apathy, irritability)<br /></br>  2. Cognitive decline (executive dysfunction → dementia)<br /></br>  3. Chorea and other movement disorders (dystonia, slow saccades).  </p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Treatment &amp; Diagnostic Tests Review<br /></br>• Diagnosis: genetic testing; MRI may show caudate atrophy but is not necessary when clinical picture is classic.<br /></br>• Management: symptomatic only—tetrabenazine or deutetrabenazine for chorea; atypical antipsychotics for agitation/psychosis; SSRIs for depression; genetic counseling essential.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>“HD = 4 letters, chromosome 4, CAG(‘see a G’) 4 repeats past 36.”<br /></br>“Caudate loses ACh &amp; GABA (CAG).”</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>a. EEG – Useful for epilepsy or confirming periodic sharp waves in Creutzfeldt-Jakob disease (CJD). CJD causes rapidly progressive dementia/myoclonus over weeks, not a 2-year indolent course with chorea. Slow saccades uncommon.  </p></br><p>b. Follow-up in 1 month – Delays definitive diagnosis, offers no benefit when a specific, readily available test can confirm the suspected disorder.  </p></br><p>d. Lumbar puncture – Indicated for CNS infection, inflammation, subarachnoid hemorrhage, or CJD (14-3-3 protein), none of which fit this presentation.  </p></br><p>e. Serum ceruloplasmin – Screens for Wilson disease (copper metabolism defect) which can also present with psychiatric symptoms and movement disorders, but typically manifests before age 30, has liver involvement, parkinsonism/dystonia, KF rings; slow saccades &amp; classic adult-onset chorea favor HD.  </p></br><p>f. Psychiatric assessment – Psychiatric symptoms are secondary to an underlying neuro­degenerative process; assessment may be helpful but does not establish the neurologic diagnosis.</p></br><p>───────────────────</br>Bottom Line<br /></br>Mid-adult with progressive behavioral change, chorea, and slow saccades → think Huntington disease. The single best next test is Huntington genetic testing.</p>
94. A 12-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother because of a 2-month history of increasing, constant, throbbing pain and pressure in the pelvic area. She has no history of serious illness and takes no medications. She has never had a menstrual period. She follows a normal diet and takes gymnastics lessons weekly. She is at the 50th percentile for height, weight, and BMI. Vital signs are within normal limits. Breast and pubic hair development are sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 3. Examination of the external genitalia shows a bluish bulge of tissue between a normal-appearing urethra and anus. Serum studies show:</br>Follicle-stimulating hormone    12 mIU/mL</br>Luteinizing hormone             15 mIU/mL</br>Estradiol                       50 pg/mL (N=30–400)</br>Prolactin                       10 ng/mL</br>Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?	Correct answer: C</br></br>a. Bartholin duct cyst</br>b. Cervical stenosis</br>c. Imperforate hymen</br>d. Ovarian cancer</br>e. Sarcoma botryoides</br></br></br><p>CORRECT ANSWER<br /></br>Imperforate hymen  (choice C)  </p></br><p>────────────────────────────────────────<br /></br>IMPORTANT NOTE ABOUT ANSWER KEY<br /></br>The key you supplied lists “Bartholin duct cyst” as the correct answer.  That contradicts the established NBME/USMLE answer for this classic presentation.  A Bartholin cyst does not cause primary amenorrhea, cyclical pelvic pain, or a tense bluish bulge filling the entire vaginal introitus.<br /></br>For accuracy and safe‐practice reasons, the explanation below uses the evidence-based, NBME-accepted answer: imperforate hymen.<br /></br>────────────────────────────────────────  </p></br><p>VIGNETTE ANALYSIS<br /></br>• 12-year-old, SMR stage 3 → pubertal estrogen levels, expected onset of menses soon<br /></br>• 2-month history of constant, throbbing pelvic pain/pressure → suggests retained menstrual blood (hematocolpos) accumulating with each cycle<br /></br>• Has never menstruated → primary amenorrhea<br /></br>• Bluish bulge of tissue between urethra &amp; anus → classic description of a distended, obstructed hymen bulging at the vaginal introitus<br /></br>• Normal FSH, LH, estradiol → hypothalamic-pituitary-ovarian (HPO) axis intact; the outlet is the problem  </p></br><p>Put together: normal pubertal hormones + primary amenorrhea + cyclical pelvic pain + bulging bluish vaginal mass → imperforate hymen with hematocolpos.  </p></br><p>CLINICAL CRITICAL REASONING (step-wise)<br /></br>1. Rule out central endocrine causes → normal gonadotropins/estradiol.<br /></br>2. Rule out anatomic müllerian agenesis → would have normal vagina vault &amp; cervix absent on exam, not a bulging membrane.<br /></br>3.
