Identify an obstructive outflow lesion → imperforate hymen (most common).<br /></br>Core takeaway: Primary amenorrhea with normal secondary sexual characteristics + obstructive bluish vaginal mass = imperforate hymen.  </p></br><p>PATHOPHYSIOLOGY REVIEW<br /></br>• Failure of the inferior end of the vaginal plate (hymenal membrane) to perforate during embryogenesis.<br /></br>• At menarche, menstrual blood cannot exit → hematocolpos (blood in vagina), hematometra (blood in uterus), pelvic pain, urinary retention/constipation if severe.  </p></br><p>DIAGNOSTIC TESTS &amp; MANAGEMENT<br /></br>• No elaborate imaging usually needed; pelvic US can confirm fluid-filled vagina/uterus.<br /></br>• Definitive treatment: cruciate (“X-shaped”) incision of the hymen with evacuation of retained blood; counsel on menstrual hygiene and fertility (normal).  </p></br><p>MEMORIZATION TIPS<br /></br>“BLUE OUTLET BLOOD BACK-UP”<br /></br>Blue bulge at vaginal outlet → Blood backs up behind an unperforated hymen.  </p></br><p>INCORRECT ANSWER ANALYSIS<br /></br>A. Bartholin duct cyst<br /></br>• 4–5 o’clock or 7–8 o’clock position lateral to introitus, not midline.<br /></br>• Painless unless infected; does NOT cause amenorrhea or pelvic pressure from retained blood.<br /></br>B. Cervical stenosis<br /></br>• Causes secondary (not primary) amenorrhea, usually after surgical procedures or radiation; no bulging hymen.<br /></br>D. Ovarian cancer<br /></br>• Rare in a 12-year-old; would not explain external bulge or primary amenorrhea with normal HPO axis.<br /></br>E. Sarcoma botryoides (embryonal rhabdomyosarcoma)<br /></br>• Grapelike polypoid masses protruding from vagina in infants/young girls; bleeding, not amenorrhea; mass is fleshy, not tense bluish membrane.  </p></br><p>DOCUMENTATION PEARLS<br /></br>• Hx: primary amenorrhea, cyclic pain, no sexual activity.<br /></br>• PE: SMR 3, intact imperforate hymen with blue bulge.<br /></br>• Assessment: Obstructive outflow tract – imperforate hymen with hematocolpos.<br /></br>• Plan: Pelvic US confirmation; surgical hymenotomy; peri-op counseling.  </p></br><p>Take-home message: In a pubertal girl with primary amenorrhea and pelvic pain, always inspect the introitus— a bluish, bulging hymen = imperforate hymen.</p>
95. A 9-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her father for a well-child examination. He is concerned that she is beginning to develop breasts at an early age. Menarche has not occurred. Her mother’s menarche began at the age of 12 years. The patient is at the 75th percentile for height and 50th percentile for weight. Breast development is sexual maturity rating (SMR) stage 2, and pubic hair development is SMR stage 1. The remainder of the examination shows no abnormalities. Which of the following is the most appropriate next step in management?	Correct answer: A</br></br>a. Reassurance</br>b. Measurement of serum estrogen concentration</br>c. Measurement of serum follicle-stimulating hormone concentration</br>d. X-ray of the wrist to assess bone age</br>e. Transvaginal ultrasonography</br>f. Pelvic examination</br></br></br><p>Correct Answer<br /></br>a. Reassurance  </p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Vignette Analysis<br /></br>• Age 9 years – greater than the cut-off (&lt;8 yr) for precocious puberty evaluation in girls.<br /></br>• Sexual-maturity rating (SMR): Breasts stage 2 (thelarche), pubic hair stage 1, no menses.<br /></br>• Normal height/weight percentiles, completely normal physical examination.<br /></br>Key pattern: Isolated thelarche at an age within the normal physiologic window for pubertal onset (8–13 yr). No rapid progression or other secondary sexual characteristics that might suggest pathology.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Clinical Critical Reasoning<br /></br>Normal pubertal sequence in girls (2-4-6-8 “T-P-G-M” mnemonic):<br /></br>1. Thelarche (T) age 8–13<br /></br>2. Pubarche/axillary hair (P) age 9–14<br /></br>3. Growth spurt (G) age 10–15<br /></br>4. Menarche (M) age 11–16 (≈2–2.5 yr after thelarche)<br /></br>This patient’s presentation (thelarche at 9 yr) fits expected physiology. No red-flag findings (e.g., vaginal bleeding, virilization, neurologic symptoms, rapid advancement over &lt;6 mo). Therefore, the appropriate management is simple reassurance and routine follow-up.</p></br><p>Documentation pearl: Record SMR stage, absence of other secondary sexual signs, and parental counseling that this is normal pubertal timing.</p></br><p>Core takeaway: In girls ≥8 yr with isolated breast development and otherwise normal exam, no work-up is needed—reassure and observe.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Pathophysiology Review<br /></br>• Thelarche is triggered by pulsatile GnRH → LH/FSH → ovarian estrogen production.<br /></br>• In normal puberty, adrenarche (pubic/axillary hair via adrenal androgens) and menarche follow later.<br /></br>• Premature thelarche (&lt;8 yr) or rapidly progressive signs warrant evaluation for central or peripheral precocious puberty; ≥8 yr does not.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Diagnostic Tests / Treatment<br /></br>None indicated. Work-up (bone age, hormone levels, imaging) is reserved for:<br /></br>• Onset of any secondary sexual characteristics before age 8.<br /></br>• Rapid progression (&lt;6 months between SMR stages).<br /></br>• Signs of virilization or CNS pathology.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Memorization Tips<br /></br>• “8-13-15-16”: Thelarche by 13, growth spurt by 15, menarche by 16.<br /></br>• Think “T-P-G-M” (Thelarche-Pubarche-Growth-Menarche) to recall sequence.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Incorrect Answer Analysis<br /></br>b. Serum estrogen – Useful only if evaluating hormone-producing tumors or gonadal failure; unnecessary in normal pubertal timing.<br /></br>c. Serum FSH – Part of precocious or delayed puberty work-up; no indication here.<br /></br>d. Wrist X-ray for bone age – Ordered when puberty appears early (&lt;8 yr) or growth seems accelerated. Her age and growth curve are normal.<br /></br>e. Transvaginal ultrasound – Used to assess ovarian cysts or tumors; invasive, not first-line, and unnecessary in a healthy 9-year-old.<br /></br>f. Pelvic examination – No complaints or bleeding; not recommended in premenarchal girls without symptoms.</p></br><p>───────────────────────────<br /></br>Bottom line: Breast development at age 9 with no other abnormalities is normal—provide reassurance and routine surveillance.</p>
